If so, the compilation receives protection.

C. **Incorrect—too narrow.** While the statement correctly identifies that public information cannot itself be protected, it unnecessarily splits the list into separate trade secrets. The company need not treat the purchasing history separately; the entire compilation can be protected. The employee's argument that "because some of the list's contents are public, the entire list loses protection" is not how courts analyze compilations. The compilation doctrine permits integrated protection of mixed public and non-public information if the compilation as a whole is not generally known and derives value from its assembly.

D. **Incorrect—too broad.** This overstates the protection available. Once information enters a trade secret collection, it does not receive "blanket protection" based merely on its inclusion. If the employee took only the customer names (which are publicly available in commercial directories) without the proprietary purchasing data, the company could not claim misappropriation of those names alone. The protection extends only to the non-public elements and to the compilation itself. The statement's "blanket protection" language wrongly suggests that inclusion in a confidential file makes all contents protected regardless of their public availability—a rule that courts would reject.

***

## Question Q1.14 — Trade Secrets vs. Confidential Information Under Contract

### 1. Stem
Two companies enter into a joint venture agreement to develop a new product. The agreement includes a comprehensive non-disclosure clause stating that all information shared under the agreement is "confidential and proprietary" and may not be disclosed without consent. Company A shares detailed marketing research data, competitive analysis, and long-term business strategies with Company B under this clause. The data is marked "Confidential" but is not otherwise protected (no password protection, held on shared servers, distributed to 20 employees of Company B). After the joint venture ends, Company B uses the marketing data and competitive analysis to enter the market. Company A sues. Company B argues that the information was never a trade secret because Company A failed to implement reasonable protective measures beyond the contractual restriction. Which statement best resolves whether the information qualifies for trade secret protection?

### 2. Options
A. The information is not a trade secret because the contractual confidentiality clause cannot substitute for reasonable protective measures; without physical or digital security measures, the UTSA's "reasonable efforts" requirement is not met.

B. The information may qualify for trade secret protection if it meets the UTSA's three elements, but Company B could defend the misappropriation claim by showing that the information was derived from Company A's inadequate security posture rather than improper means.

C. The information qualifies as a trade secret under the NDA alone; once parties agree that information is confidential, the UTSA's reasonable efforts requirement is satisfied.

D. The information qualifies for trade secret protection if: (1) it meets the first two UTSA elements (information with independent economic value, not generally known), (2) the parties' contractual relationship itself constitutes a "duty to maintain its secrecy" under UTSA § 1(2), and (3) the company took reasonable efforts *given the context* (including the contractual restrictions and limited internal distribution).

### 3. Correct Answer
D

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect—too rigid.** This answer treats the "reasonable efforts" requirement as requiring certain technical measures (passwords, digital security) independent of context. However, courts recognize that "reasonable" efforts vary based on industry, company size, and information type. A confidentiality clause supported by limited internal distribution and employee confidentiality obligations can constitute reasonable efforts in some contexts. For example, in professional services (law firms, consulting) or in joint venture contexts, courts have recognized that contractual restrictions, employee training, and limited circulation are appropriate "reasonable efforts" even without military-grade cybersecurity. The UTSA requires efforts that are "reasonable under the circumstances," not specific technological measures.

B. **Incorrect.** This answer suggests that inadequate security is itself a defense to misappropriation, which misses the legal structure. The question is not whether Company B's use was "improper means" (it might not have been if Company B simply accessed unprotected information with proper authorization under the joint venture agreement), but rather whether Company A has a claim based on improper *use* of authorized information. The defense is not "Company A should have secured the information better," but rather "Company A authorized sharing this information, so my use is not improper." This is a different analysis.

C. **Incorrect.** The NDA alone does not satisfy the "reasonable efforts" requirement; however, the NDA *combined* with other protective measures (limited distribution, employee confidentiality obligations, clear labeling) can satisfy it. Courts distinguish between a bare promise of confidentiality and efforts that demonstrate the information is actually treated as confidential. An NDA on a document that is then freely shared with no restrictions suggests inconsistency—the owner's conduct contradicts the confidentiality claim.

D. **Correct.** This answer correctly identifies the multifaceted analysis courts apply:

(1) **UTSA Elements (1) and (2)**: The marketing research, competitive analysis, and business strategies must qualify as "information" and derive "independent economic value" from not being generally known. This is likely satisfied if the data is proprietary market analysis not widely available.

(2) **"Duty to Maintain Secrecy"**: The UTSA § 1(2)(b) establishes liability for those who knew or had reason to know that knowledge was "acquired...under circumstances giving rise to a duty to maintain its secrecy or limit its use." In a joint venture context, the contractual relationship itself creates such a duty. Company B's employees accepted information shared under a confidentiality clause; this creates an explicit duty. Courts recognize that contractual relationships can establish duties to maintain secrecy.

(3) **Reasonable Efforts in Context**: The company's efforts are assessed "under the circumstances." Here, the circumstances include: (a) the contractual confidentiality clause, (b) internal circulation limited to 20 employees, (c) confidentiality markings, (d) likely employee confidentiality obligations (standard in joint ventures). These efforts, taken together, may constitute "reasonable" efforts even though the information was not locked in a vault or password-protected.

Courts have embraced this contextual approach. In the joint venture context, particularly, courts recognize that unlimited access to confidential information among joint venture partners does not destroy trade secret status if the partners have committed contractually to maintain secrecy and limited circulation to those who need access.

***

## Question Q1.15 — The Temporal Scope of the "Reasonableness" Standard

### 1. Stem
A software company developed its customer relationship management (CRM) system in 2005 and maintained it as a trade secret through the following protective measures: (1) physical access restrictions to the server room, (2) password protection, (3) employee NDAs, (4) quarterly security trainings, and (5) a "need-to-know" distribution policy. In 2015, the company's IT infrastructure became outdated but remained functional. By 2020, the company still used the same 2005 security protocols, which IT experts agreed were significantly below industry standards for that year. Cloud computing, multi-factor authentication, encryption, and endpoint protection had become standard practice, but the company did not implement these. In 2022, a remote employee was hacked and the CRM system was compromised. A former employee of the rival company obtained access and disclosed the system's architecture and code to his new employer. The company sued for misappropriation. The defendant argued that the company failed to maintain "reasonable efforts" to keep the CRM system secret because its security measures, by 2022 standards, were grossly inadequate. How should a court evaluate this argument?

### 2. Options
A. The defendant is correct because security standards are objective and evolving; if the company failed to adopt industry-standard practices by 2022, it failed to maintain reasonable efforts in that year.

B. The defendant is incorrect because the company implemented security measures in 2005 that were state-of-the-art at the time; reasonable efforts, once established, need not be updated as technology advances.

C. The defendant's argument raises a genuine question about whether the company's reasonable efforts eroded over time as industry standards evolved; courts should examine whether the company's failure to upgrade security systems constituted a material gap between its efforts and evolving industry norms.

D. The defendant's argument is irrelevant because trade secret protection cannot be lost once established; the company's security measures in 2005 determined its status, and subsequent changes to security standards cannot retroactively destroy a pre-existing trade secret.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect—too rigid.** While industry standards are relevant, courts do not impose a strict requirement that companies adopt every emerging security practice in real-time. This would create instability for trade secret protection and impose unreasonable burdens on smaller companies or those in resource-constrained situations. Moreover, a strictly objective standard would encourage "arms races" where mere adoption of the newest technology becomes the baseline, making trade secret protection increasingly difficult over time. Courts recognize that reasonableness must account for practical constraints, company size, and cost-benefit considerations.

B. **Incorrect—too static.** This answer wrongly freezes the "reasonable efforts" standard at the moment the company first implemented security measures. The UTSA contemplates an ongoing obligation to maintain reasonable efforts. The scenario's facts note that the company "still used the same 2005 security protocols...by 2022"—suggesting not just that it failed to update, but that it actively maintained outdated practices despite knowing about advancements. Courts have recognized that "reasonable efforts" must adapt to changed circumstances. In a famous pre-UTSA case, *Defcon-1, Inc. v. U.S. District Court* (488 F.3d 1143, 9th Cir. 2007), the court noted that what constituted reasonable security in earlier years might be inadequate later. The statement's "once established" language wrongly suggests that security measures become frozen in time.

C. **Correct.** This answer properly identifies the temporal dimension of the "reasonableness" standard. The UTSA requires efforts that are "reasonable under the circumstances"—a standard that necessarily contemplates changing circumstances over time. As industry practices evolve, as technology advances, and as threats increase, courts examine whether the company's prior measures remained reasonable or whether new threats created obligations to upgrade. Several factors support this approach:

(1) **Practical Necessity**: By 2022, cybersecurity threats had evolved dramatically. Remote work created new vulnerabilities; cloud infrastructure became standard, and failing to implement basic multi-factor authentication left systems vulnerable to common attack vectors. Courts recognize that complacency in the face of known, evolving threats may undermine reasonableness.

(2) **Cost-Benefit**: However, courts also balance cost and burden. A small company may not afford the latest security infrastructure, whereas a large software company should. The company's resources, the information's value, and the company's actual knowledge of risks are relevant.

(3) **Evidentiary Value**: The company's failure to upgrade despite knowing that industry standards had changed could be evidence of negligence or indifference to secrecy, which courts view negatively. In *nClosures*, the court criticized the company's inconsistency between its confidentiality claims and its actual conduct. Here, maintaining 2005 security measures in 2022 suggests the company's real commitment to secrecy eroded.

(4) **Trade-Off with Competitive Advantage**: If the company saved significant costs by not upgrading but at the expense of security, this suggests a business choice to deprioritize secrecy, which undermines protection.

The defendant's argument thus raises legitimate questions about whether the company's efforts remained reasonable given known, evolved industry standards and specific threats.

D. **Incorrect.** This answer wrongly suggests that trade secret status is determined once and for all at the moment of first establishment. This is not how the law works. Trade secrets can be *lost* through disclosure or when they cease to meet the UTSA's requirements. While this scenario does not involve disclosure, it does involve a question about whether the company's ongoing protective efforts remained adequate. If the company's security measures deteriorated below the reasonable threshold, this could affect its ability to claim protection for newly misappropriated information. Moreover, the answer's claim that "subsequent changes to security standards cannot retroactively destroy a pre-existing trade secret" is overstated. While a secret established in 2005 remains a secret unless disclosed, the company's entitlement to injunctive relief or damages in 2022 might be affected by its failure to maintain reasonable efforts in the interim. Some courts have held that unreasonable failure to upgrade security can reduce the plaintiff's damages or affect preliminary injunction analysis.

***

## Question Q1.16 — Policy Implications of the Patent vs. Trade Secret Choice

### 1. Stem
A pharmaceutical company has developed a novel drug compound that meets all patentability standards (utility, novelty, non-obviousness, eligible subject matter) and could command 15-17 years of effective patent protection (accounting for FDA review delays). However, the company's pharmaceutical scientists believe that the drug might face generic competition before patent expiration if competitors reverse-engineer it through authorized clinical trials or obtain it through legal discovery in litigation. Meanwhile, the company's proprietary manufacturing process for producing the compound—which remains undisclosed and is embedded in trade secret procedures—could theoretically provide indefinite competitive advantage. The company faces a strategic choice: patent the compound (which requires full public disclosure) and maintain the manufacturing process as a trade secret, or keep both the compound and the process secret to avoid disclosure and maintain indefinite protection. From a policy perspective, does trade secret law's existence create a risk that the company would choose secrecy over patent disclosure, thereby depriving the public of the benefit of knowing about the drug?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, trade secret law creates a substantial risk of non-disclosure because the incentive structure of indefinite protection via secrecy exceeds the 17-20 year patent term, so companies will rationally avoid disclosure.

B. No, the company will choose patent protection because patent law provides stronger protection (blocking independent discovery) than trade secret law, making the patent option economically superior despite the disclosure requirement.

C. Yes, in theory, but the Supreme Court in *Kewanee* concluded that this risk is "remote indeed" for clearly patentable inventions because: (1) other firms will likely independently discover the same solution, (2) the need to commercialize requires eventual public exposure, and (3) the patent's strong protection (blocking independent discovery and competitors' efforts) provides superior value compared to secrecy's weakness.

D. No, because trade secret law includes a "public health exception" that requires disclosure of pharmaceuticals after a certain period, preventing indefinite secrecy.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This reflects a plausible concern, but it is the exact argument that *Kewanee* considered and rejected. Justice Douglas's dissent articulated this risk: that companies would use indefinite trade secret protection to avoid the mandatory disclosure of patent law, thereby circumventing the public policy favoring eventual public access to innovations. However, the Supreme Court majority rejected the conclusion that this risk was sufficient to preempt trade secret law. The Court's reasoning was that for clearly patentable inventions, this risk is "remote indeed." While indefinite secrecy theoretically provides superior protection to 17-year patents, the Court identified practical factors that make companies unlikely to forgo patents for clearly patentable inventions.

B. **Incorrect.** This answer is too categorical. While it is true that patent protection is stronger in some respects (blocking independent discovery and reverse engineering), the statement's conclusion that patents are "economically superior" is not always true. For some clearly patentable inventions, trade secret protection might actually be preferable if (1) the secret can feasibly remain hidden (internal manufacturing processes, algorithms used only internally), (2) the competitive advantage from indefinite secrecy exceeds the value of 17-20 years of exclusive patent rights (if competitors can rapidly design around the patent or if the market lifespan exceeds the patent term), or (3) disclosure would reveal information that competitors could use in adjacent markets. For example, some companies have patented their core technology while keeping optimization techniques and manufacturing tricks secret. The economic comparison is not straightforward.

C. **Correct.** This reflects the Supreme Court's conclusion in *Kewanee Oil Co. v. Bicron Corp.* (416 U.S. 470, 1974). The Court explicitly addressed Justice Douglas's concern (that indefinite trade secret protection would create incentives to avoid patent disclosure) but concluded that the risk is remote for clearly patentable inventions. The Court's reasoning:

(1) **Independent Discovery Risk**: The "ripeness-of-time" concept of invention predicts that if a particular solution is worth discovering, others likely will discover it in "relatively short" periods. The Kewanee Court noted: "If something is to be discovered at all, very likely it will be discovered by more than one person." This independent discovery breaks the exclusive advantage of trade secret protection.

(2) **Commercialization Exposure**: To generate profits from an invention, companies typically must commercialize it—sell it or use it visibly. Once products embodying the invention reach the market, competitors will study them, reverse-engineer them, or become inspired to develop alternatives. Trade secret protection for the core invention becomes difficult once the invention is commercialized. The pharmaceutical drug itself would be disclosed through clinical trials and FDA approval; secrecy of the compound itself is impractical.

(3) **Patent's Superior Strength**: Patents provide broader protection than trade secrets. A patent blocks even independent discovery and reverse engineering. For a clearly patentable invention expected to reach the market, the patent's superiority becomes apparent. The company's choice to patent rather than seek secrecy is economically rational for drugs that will be clinically tested and approved.

In the scenario, the drug compound itself would likely be disclosed through FDA clinical trials, making secrecy impractical. The company would rationally patent the compound and protect the manufacturing process as a trade secret. This combination (patent disclosure + trade secret manufacturing process) optimizes both public access and private competitive advantage.

D. **Incorrect.** While some jurisdictions have attempted to create exceptions to trade secret protection for public health or safety reasons, there is no general "public health exception" in trade secret law. The UTSA and DTSA contain no such exception. The whistleblower immunity in the DTSA (§ 1833(b)) allows disclosure of trade secrets to government officials for reporting suspected violations, but this is not a general public health exception—it applies narrowly to whistleblowers reporting legal violations. For pharmaceuticals, the FDA review process, not trade secret law itself, ensures that essential safety and efficacy information is disclosed, but this is a regulatory requirement, not a trade secret law exception.

***

## Question Q1.17 — Reverse Engineering Contractual Restrictions and Shrink-Wrap Licenses

### 1. Stem
A software company sells its compiled executable program with a terms-of-service agreement displayed during installation that states: "Licensee may not reverse-engineer, decompile, disassemble, or analyze the software to determine its underlying source code or trade secrets." A customer purchases the software, installs it, and clicks "I Accept" on the terms of service. The customer then uses a legal decompiler tool to reverse-engineer the software and extracts key algorithm details. The software company sues for misappropriation of trade secrets and breach of contract. The customer argues that reverse engineering is always a "proper means" under trade secret law, and that contractual restrictions cannot override this entitlement. Which statement best resolves the customer's argument?

### 2. Options
A. The customer is correct because reverse engineering is an absolute right under trade secret law; contractual restrictions are void as contrary to the public policy favoring fair competition.

B. The customer is incorrect because trade secret law and contract law operate independently; the terms-of-service restriction creates a contractual duty not to reverse-engineer, and breach of this duty is separate from and independent of trade secret misappropriation.

C. The customer is partially correct that reverse engineering of lawfully obtained products is generally a proper means, but contractual restrictions can narrow this entitlement; if the customer validly agreed to the restriction, reverse engineering in breach of contract may constitute misappropriation if the restriction is enforceable.

D. The customer's argument is irrelevant because compiled executable code is not a trade secret; reverse engineering executable code is never wrongful under trade secret law.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This overstates the reverse engineering right. While it is true that reverse engineering a publicly available product is generally a "proper means" under trade secret law, this right can be contractually limited. The UTSA's definition of "improper means" includes "breach of a duty...to maintain its secrecy or limit its use" (UTSA § 1(1)(ii)). If a customer validly agrees to a contractual restriction on reverse engineering, breach of that duty can constitute misappropriation. Courts have recognized that contractual restrictions on reverse engineering are enforceable if they are (1) clearly communicated, (2) supported by consideration, and (3) not unconscionable. The public policy favoring fair competition does not override all contractual protections; it limits certain restrictions (e.g., non-competes), but a narrow restriction on reverse engineering in a licensed product is generally enforceable.

B. **Incorrect—though not entirely wrong.** The statement correctly notes that contract law and trade secret law operate independently and that breach of contract is a separate claim. However, the conclusion that these claims are "independent of" misappropriation is too categorical. Under the UTSA, breach of a contractual duty *can* constitute the "improper means" or "duty to maintain secrecy" elements that make conduct "improper" for trade secret purposes. The two bodies of law interact. A breach of contract that involves disclosing or using a trade secret may constitute both contract breach and trade secret misappropriation. The statement's "independent" framing is too rigid; the proper analysis is that contract law informs whether conduct is "improper" for trade secret purposes.

C. **Correct.** This answer properly recognizes that reverse engineering rights can be contractually limited without violating trade secret policy. Several principles support this:

(1) **Contractual Freedom**: Trade secret law does not prohibit parties from contracting for greater protection than the law provides. If a company wants to prevent reverse engineering (a normally permitted activity), it can require customers to agree not to reverse-engineer. Consumers who accept such terms have forgone their general reverse engineering right.

(2) **"Improper Means" Includes Breach of Duty**: The UTSA defines improper means to include breach of a contractual or confidential duty. If a customer agrees to a restriction on reverse engineering and then reverse-engineers anyway, this constitutes breach of a "duty...to limit its use" (UTSA § 1(2)(b)(2)).

(3) **Enforceability Conditions**: Courts do enforce such restrictions if they are (a) clearly disclosed (which a terms-of-service agreement typically is), (b) reasonably specific (the restriction must clearly identify what is prohibited), (c) supported by consideration (access to the software is consideration for the license restriction), and (d) not unconscionable. Overbroad restrictions (e.g., prohibiting any analysis whatsoever) might be unenforceable, but reasonable limitations on reverse engineering are typically upheld.

(4) **Coherence with Licensing**: Software vendors often use licensing to supplement trade secret protection. A company that wants stronger protection than trade secret law alone provides can require customers to agree to contractual restrictions as a condition of purchase. This does not contradict trade secret policy; it is consistent with the principle that parties can contract for confidentiality.

In the scenario, if the customer validly accepted the terms of service (and "I Accept" clicking typically constitutes acceptance in jurisdictions recognizing clickwrap agreements), the customer's reverse engineering in breach of that agreement constitutes improper conduct. The software company could pursue both a contract breach claim and a trade secret misappropriation claim.

D. **Incorrect.** Compiled executable code and its underlying algorithms can be trade secrets if they meet the UTSA's three elements (information, independent economic value, reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy). The company's restrictions on reverse engineering and its protection of the code through compilation and licensing demonstrate reasonable efforts. Just because code is compiled (rather than visible in source code form) does not mean it is not a trade secret; in fact, compilation is itself a protective measure.

***

## Question Q1.18 — Misappropriation Through Use of Information Derived Indirectly from Thieves

### 1. Stem
A financial services company develops a proprietary algorithm for predicting market trends and maintains it as a trade secret. A disgruntled programmer steals the source code and sells it to a broker who publishes it on an anonymous online forum. A third-party trading firm downloads the algorithm from the forum, implements it, and begins using it without knowledge of how the code originally circulated. The financial services company discovers the trading firm using its algorithm and sues for misappropriation. The trading firm argues that it did not misappropriate the trade secret because: (1) it did not steal the code (the programmer did), (2) it did not know the code had been stolen, and (3) it acquired it from a public source (the online forum). Which statement best resolves the trading firm's argument?

### 2. Options
A. The trading firm is correct; it cannot be held liable for misappropriation because it did not engage in improper acquisition and had no knowledge of any breach of duty.

B. The trading firm may be held liable for misappropriation under UTSA § 1(2)(b)(2) if evidence shows that it knew or had reason to know that the code was derived from improper acquisition and breach of confidentiality.

C. The trading firm is correct because once a trade secret is published on a public forum, it is no longer a trade secret and is in the public domain; anyone can use publicly available information.

D. The trading firm is liable regardless of its knowledge because trade secret law imposes strict liability on all subsequent users of misappropriated information.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This overlooks UTSA § 1(2)(b), which imposes liability on parties who acquire trade secrets "under circumstances giving rise to a duty to maintain its secrecy or limit its use" and on those who use trade secrets knowing "or [having] reason to know that his knowledge of the trade secret was derived from or through a person who had utilized improper means." The trading firm's lack of direct involvement in theft does not shield it from liability if it had reason to know the source was improper. The "I didn't steal it; someone else did" defense is insufficient. Courts examine whether a reasonable party in the trading firm's position would have suspected improper acquisition.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the UTSA's secondary liability provision. Under UTSA § 1(2)(b)(2), a person is liable for misappropriation if they:

"[D]isclose[d] or use[d] a trade secret of another without express or implied consent by a person who:...At the time of disclosure or use, knew or had reason to know that his knowledge of the trade secret was derived from or through a person who had utilized improper means to acquire it."

The trading firm's liability turns on a subjective/objective knowledge standard: Did it know, or should it have known (had reason to know), that the code came from improper acquisition? Factors courts examine:

(1) **Suspicious Circumstances**: If the code was posted on an anonymous forum with no explanation of its origin, a reasonable party might wonder how it appeared there.

(2) **Red Flags**: If the code bore indicators of proprietary origin (copyright notices, company markings, internal comments), the trading firm should have recognized it as likely misappropriated.

(3) **Obviousness of Impropriety**: If the trading firm knew the algorithm's value and sophistication, it might have reason to suspect the source was improper (no one gives away valuable trade secrets for free).

(4) **Industry Knowledge**: If the trading firm was aware that the financial services company had developed this algorithm, acquiring it from an anonymous source should raise suspicion.

(5) **Due Diligence**: Did the trading firm make any effort to verify the algorithm's provenance or determine its ownership?

In this scenario, if the trading firm could show that it had no reason to suspect impropriety (for example, if the algorithm appeared to be legitimately open-source software), it might escape liability. However, if evidence shows that the trading firm recognized the code's source as suspicious or proprietary, it could be liable.

C. **Incorrect.** While it is true that information in the public domain is not a trade secret, this does not automatically shield subsequent users from liability. The UTSA recognizes "tainted knowledge"—information that, though publicly available, was placed there through improper means. If a trade secret holder's secret is misappropriated and then published, the original misappropriation remains actionable. Subsequent users who know or have reason to know that the publicly available information was derived from improper acquisition can incur liability under § 1(2)(b)(2). This provision prevents parties from laundering stolen information through public disclosure; they cannot download it from a forum and claim immunity merely because it is now public.

D. **Incorrect.** Trade secret law is not based on strict liability. It requires scienter (knowledge or reason to know) that the information was improperly acquired. A party who innocently acquires trade secret information from a public source without any awareness of improper acquisition is not liable. The trading firm would bear the burden of establishing this innocence, but if it could show genuine lack of knowledge or reason to know, it would escape liability. Strict liability would be impractical and would impose unreasonable burdens on parties purchasing or downloading information from public sources.

***

## Question Q1.19 — The Inevitable Disclosure Doctrine and First Amendment Implications

### 1. Stem
A former senior manager of a hardware manufacturer, with intimate knowledge of the company's product roadmap and supply-chain strategy, accepted a position with a competitor. The former manager signed a confidentiality agreement and promised she would not disclose confidential information. However, her new position requires her to make strategic decisions about product planning and supply-chain sourcing—decisions that will necessarily implicate the knowledge she acquired from her prior employer. The hardware manufacturer sought a preliminary injunction preventing her from assuming her new position, claiming "inevitable disclosure" of trade secrets. The former manager argued that enjoining her from working violates her First Amendment right to earn a living in her chosen profession and her liberty interest in pursuing employment. Which statement best resolves the tension between trade secret protection and the former manager's constitutional rights?

### 2. Options
A. The former manager's constitutional rights prevail; trade secret law cannot restrict employment rights, and the "inevitable disclosure" doctrine violates the First Amendment by preventing lawful employment.

B. Trade secret protection prevails; the company's intellectual property rights override the employee's employment rights, and the inevitable disclosure doctrine is constitutional because it protects company interests.

C. The former manager's constitutional rights and trade secret rights exist in tension, but courts balance them by recognizing that: (1) trade secret law does not prevent all employment but only employment in positions where trade secrets will necessarily be used/disclosed; (2) non-compete agreements that prevent competition generally are disfavored, but narrow injunctions preventing misappropriation are appropriate; and (3) the employer's right to protect trade secrets is not preempted by employment rights.

D. The inevitable disclosure doctrine is unconstitutional on its face and cannot be applied to restrict employment under any circumstances because it violates due process by requiring prediction of future conduct.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This overprotects employment rights at the expense of trade secret protection. While employment rights and freedom of movement are important, they do not automatically override trade secret protection. The Supreme Court has recognized that protecting confidential business information serves a legitimate state interest. In *PepsiCo Inc. v. Redmond*, the court acknowledged the tension: trade secret law "should not prevent workers from pursuing their livelihoods when they leave their current positions," but it can restrict employment in specific roles where disclosure is inevitable. The tension is real, but it does not resolve in favor of employment rights categorically.

B. **Incorrect.** This overstates trade secret rights and treats them as categorically superior to employment rights. While trade secret protection is legitimate, courts do not permit blanket restrictions on employment. A company cannot prevent all former employees from working for competitors merely because they possess confidential knowledge. The restriction must be narrowly tailored to the specific threat of inevitable disclosure in the particular role. Moreover, this answer ignores the constitutional concerns that do exist and the fact that employment rights are also protected interests.

C. **Correct.** This answer properly identifies the balancing act that courts perform. The resolution proceeds in steps:

(1) **Trade Secret Rights Are Constitutional**: The company's interest in protecting trade secrets is a legitimate economic and property-based interest. State trade secret laws do not violate the Constitution merely because they restrict some employment opportunities.

(2) **Employment Rights Are Also Protected**: Workers have interests in earning a living and pursuing their chosen profession. However, these rights are not absolute; they do not extend to using stolen or improperly disclosed information.

(3) **Narrow Injunctions Are Appropriate**: The key distinction is between broad restrictions on employment (not permitted) and narrow restrictions targeting inevitable disclosure in specific roles (permitted). An injunction that prevents the manager from working *anywhere* would be disfavored as a disguised non-compete. But an injunction that prevents her from assuming a specific role in supply-chain planning at the competitor, because this role would inevitably implicate the prior employer's trade secrets, is more appropriate.

(4) **Precedent in PepsiCo**: The court in *PepsiCo* acknowledged the tension and held that even assuming the former employee's employment rights were substantial, the preliminary injunction was appropriate because (a) the inevitable disclosure threat was clear and not speculative, and (b) the injunction was narrowly tailored to the specific position that would implicate the trade secrets.

(5) **Comparison to Non-Competes**: Courts are much more skeptical of broad non-compete agreements that restrict all employment in an industry or region. The inevitable disclosure doctrine is more narrowly focused on a specific threat in a specific role, making it more defensible from a constitutional perspective.

D. **Incorrect.** This claims the doctrine is unconstitutional "on its face," but courts have upheld it in multiple jurisdictions. The doctrine does require courts to predict future conduct, but this is not inherently unconstitutional. Many legal standards (e.g., preliminary injunction standards requiring prediction of irreparable harm, or recidivism risk assessments in sentencing) involve predictive judgments. The "fundamental unfairness" of predicting conduct is mitigated by the fact that courts apply the standard only where the likelihood is "high" and "inevitable," not merely speculative.

***

## Question Q1.20 — Synthesizing the UTSA Elements: The "Reasonable Efforts" Paradox and Burden of Proof

### 1. Stem
A biotech startup developed a novel protein sequencing method over three years and treated it as a trade secret. The company implemented NDAs with all employees, maintained the research data in a locked laboratory, and restricted access to two senior scientists. However, the startup was cash-strapped and could not afford advanced cybersecurity measures or physical facility upgrades. The lab shared a building with unaffiliated businesses, the door locks were standard (not biometric), and no surveillance system monitored the lab. After a competitor hired away one of the two scientists (who breached his NDA and disclosed the method), the startup sued for misappropriation. The competitor argues that the startup's minimal physical security and lack of cybersecurity measures mean it failed to take "reasonable efforts," and therefore the method is not a trade secret. During discovery, it emerges that the startup's reasonable efforts were appropriate for a cash-strapped startup but below industry standards for larger, well-funded biotech firms. Which statement best resolves the appropriate standard for "reasonable efforts" in this context?

### 2. Options
A. The reasonable efforts standard is objective and industry-wide; because the company's security measures fell below typical biotech industry standards, it failed to maintain a trade secret regardless of its financial constraints.

B. The reasonable efforts standard is subjective and depends only on the company's own assessment of what is necessary; if the company believed its measures were reasonable, courts should defer to that judgment.

C. The reasonable efforts standard is contextual and accounts for company size, financial resources, industry norms, and the nature of the information; courts examine whether the company's efforts were reasonable *given its particular circumstances*, not whether they matched larger competitors' investments.

D. The reasonable efforts standard is fact-dependent but requires companies to invest in the most current industry-standard security measures; companies cannot rely on budgetary constraints to excuse inadequate protection.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This imposes an objective "industry standard" test that ignores practical reality. Requiring all companies to maintain security measures at the level of well-funded, large firms would make trade secret protection unavailable to startups, nonprofits, and smaller organizations—a consequence contrary to UTSA policy. The UTSA's phrase "reasonable under the circumstances" explicitly invites contextual analysis. Courts recognize that a startup's reasonable efforts differ from a large pharmaceutical company's reasonable efforts. To hold otherwise would create a perverse incentive for entrepreneurs: either spend millions on security to qualify for trade secret protection, or abandon trade secrets and publicize inventions, sacrificing competitive advantage. This would undermine innovation by startups and small businesses.

B. **Incorrect.** The standard is not purely subjective; courts do not defer entirely to a company's self-assessment. If a company's efforts were genuinely inadequate, claiming subjective belief in reasonableness will not shield the information. The question is objective: would a reasonable person in the company's position, facing the same constraints, have taken these measures? However, the company's subjective intent and actual efforts are relevant evidence of how seriously the company treated secrecy.

C. **Correct.** This reflects the consensus of courts interpreting the UTSA's "reasonable under the circumstances" standard. Relevant factors include:

(1) **Company Size and Resources**: A small startup cannot be held to the same security standards as a Fortune 500 company. Courts consider what the company could afford and reasonably implement.

(2) **Industry Norms**: What do similar companies in the field typically do? If other startups in biotech use similar protective measures, this supports reasonableness.

(3) **Information Type and Value**: Highly valuable secrets may warrant greater investments; mundane information may require less.

(4) **Threat Environment**: What threats does the company face? If the industry experiences high rates of employee theft, greater measures are appropriate.

(5) **Practical Effectiveness**: Does the measure actually work? An expensive but ineffective security system may not be "reasonable," while a modest but effective system may be.

(6) **Accessibility of Measures**: Can the company practically implement these measures given its location and operations?

In this scenario, the startup's NDAs, restricted access, and locked lab may constitute reasonable efforts for a cash-strapped biotech startup, even if they fall short of measures employed by well-funded firms. The competitor's argument—that the startup failed to meet industry standards—should be rejected if industry standards for similarly-sized companies are comparable to the startup's measures.

D. **Incorrect.** This imposes an absolute requirement to adopt current industry-standard security, which conflicts with the "reasonable under the circumstances" language and with practical reality. Requiring constant technology upgrades would be prohibitively expensive and would shift the burden of maintaining trade secret protection toward larger, better-resourced companies. It would also create a moving target: as technology advances, companies would face ever-escalating obligations to upgrade. The UTSA does not require this standard; it permits contextual judgment about what is reasonable given the company's situation.

***



## Question Q2.1 — Information Requirement: Drawing the Line Between "Concrete Knowledge" and "General Skill"

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
A manufacturing engineer at Polymer Corp. spent five years developing expertise in advanced polymerization temperature controls that improve product durability. She gained this knowledge through hands-on experience with Polymer Corp.'s proprietary processes and attended company training sessions. The knowledge she developed is partly based on industry standards and partly on Polymer Corp.'s undisclosed techniques. When she leaves to join a competitor, Polymer Corp. sues for trade secret misappropriation, arguing that the engineer is taking away valuable "knowledge" about their process. The engineer's new employer argues that the engineer is simply exercising general professional skill acquired over time. Under trade secret law, does the engineer's accumulated expertise qualify as protectable "information"?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the engineer acquired the knowledge while employed by Polymer Corp. and therefore it belongs to the company as a trade secret.

B. No, because general skills, knowledge, and experience acquired during employment are part of an employee's professional competence and are portable, even if sharpened on the job.

C. Yes, because the engineer's experience with Polymer Corp.'s undisclosed techniques combined with industry knowledge creates a unique skill set that competitors would value.

D. It depends on whether the engineer can articulate specific, defined processes and methods, as opposed to merely recalling general know-how and typical design principles.

### 3. Correct Answer
D

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Trade secret law does not automatically protect all knowledge acquired while employed. Courts distinguish sharply between general skill and experience (which is portable) and specific, protectable information. Simply acquiring knowledge on the job does not transform it into a company trade secret. The critical question is whether the knowledge rises to the level of a defined, concrete method or technique—not merely how it was acquired.

**B. Partially correct but incomplete.** While it is true that general professional skill and accumulated competence are portable and not protectable as trade secrets, this option oversimplifies. The real issue is the line between "general" and "specific." An employee cannot be prevented from using general industry principles, but if the engineer took with her specific, detailed processes or methods—distinct from routine skill—those could qualify as information. This option fails to account for scenarios where an engineer does take away concrete, protectable content.

**C. Incorrect.** This answer conflates two different concepts. The fact that a skill set is "valuable" or "unique" does not automatically make it a trade secret. Trade secret law requires more than value—it requires that the information be (1) sufficiently concrete to be defined with specificity, (2) not generally known or readily ascertainable, and (3) qualifying as "information" under the statute. The UTSA defines "information" broadly but does not include mere general expertise or unique skill combinations. See *Altavion* (distinguishing between broad conceptual ideas and concrete, defined design concepts).

**D. Correct.** This captures the essential legal test. Trade secret law protects *defined knowledge*—specific formulas, process steps, methods, techniques—not the general skill or judgment that employees develop. Under Restatement § 757 and the UTSA, courts ask: Can the employee articulate concrete details about the method or process that go beyond what an industry professional would know? If so, and if it is kept secret and has economic value, it may qualify. If the employee merely recalls typical design principles or general approaches, no protection attaches. The portable nature of general skill is well-established in cases like *PepsiCo* (distinguishing between Redmond's general business acumen and specific strategic plans) and reinforced in the casebook materials discussing the distinction between "ideas" and "information."

***

## Question Q2.2 — Information Requirement: Can Abstract Concepts Qualify as Trade Secrets?

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
DataTech Corp. has developed what it considers a revolutionary approach to data clustering. The concept can be summarized as: "Use machine learning to group customer profiles by predictive spending behavior rather than demographic categories alone." When an employee leaves to join a competitor, DataTech argues that this "general idea" is a trade secret. The employee argues the concept is too abstract—it is merely a high-level strategic concept, not concrete information. DataTech responds that it spent two years and $2 million validating the concept through research, and it has not disclosed the underlying details to anyone outside a small group of employees under NDAs. How should a court analyze whether this "idea" qualifies as protectable information?

### 2. Options
A. The concept qualifies as protectable information because DataTech invested significant time and money developing it, and this demonstrates independent economic value.

B. The concept does not qualify as protectable information because it is merely a business idea or strategy, and trade secret law protects only technical innovations, formulas, and processes—not abstract concepts.

C. The court must examine whether DataTech can articulate the concept with sufficient particularity to separate it from general knowledge in the field, and whether the concept is defined with enough specificity that competitors would need to independently develop the same insights or reverse engineer it.

D. The concept qualifies as information if DataTech can show it is not generally known; the level of abstraction or concreteness is irrelevant as long as the information is confidential.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While investment and economic value are relevant factors in showing that information has "independent economic value," they are not sufficient on their own to qualify something as protectable "information." A company might invest heavily in developing a general business idea that remains too abstract to qualify. The UTSA requires that information be capable of being protected—that is, sufficiently defined and concrete. The investment inquiry is secondary to the threshold question of whether the content qualifies as "information" at all. See *Altavion* (rejecting overly broad concepts and requiring concrete, defined design concepts to qualify as information).

**B. Incorrect.** This option is too restrictive. Trade secret law protects not only technical innovations but also business methods, strategies, compilations, and processes. The UTSA's definition of "information" is deliberately broad and includes "formulas, patterns, compilations, program, device, method, technique, or process." However, the statute does require that the information be concrete enough to be distinguished from mere ideas or general principles. The distinction drawn here between "ideas" and "technical innovations" is a false dichotomy; what matters is whether the information is specific enough to be identified and defined.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the proper legal analysis. Under *Altavion*, the key inquiry is whether the claimant can articulate the alleged secret with sufficient particularity to separate it from general knowledge and to allow the defendant to understand "at least the boundaries within which the secret lies." If DataTech can define its data-clustering approach in concrete terms—for example, specifying the particular machine learning algorithms used, the specific behavioral metrics weighted in the model, the validation thresholds, the proprietary training data sets, or the precise weighting of spending-behavior inputs—then those concrete elements may qualify as protectable information. If, however, DataTech can only articulate the concept as "use behavioral clustering instead of demographics," then it is too abstract. The question is not whether the idea is a "strategy" versus "technology," but whether it is sufficiently defined. See *Altavion* on the distinction between protectable design concepts and unprotectable general concepts.

**D. Incorrect.** Secrecy alone is not sufficient. The UTSA requires that information meet multiple tests, including that it qualify as "information" in the first place. Abstract or overly general concepts do not become protectable simply by being kept confidential. If a concept is so vague that it is indistinguishable from general industry knowledge or principles that any skilled professional would develop, confidentiality does not transform it. This answer fails to account for the threshold requirement that the information be sufficiently concrete and defined.

***

## Question Q2.3 — Independent Economic Value: Value Derived From Secrecy vs. Value in General

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
TechSoft Inc. has developed a customer acquisition software tool. The tool uses a well-known algorithm (based on published academic research) combined with TechSoft's proprietary database of 500,000 customer profiles and behavioral data. The tool generates leads at a 3% conversion rate, compared to industry average of 1.5%, giving TechSoft a significant competitive advantage. TechSoft has not patented the tool and keeps the entire system confidential. A competitor learns of the tool and begins reverse-engineering it, starting with analyzing TechSoft's publicly available marketing materials and customer samples. TechSoft sues for trade secret misappropriation. The competitor argues that because the algorithm is published and the customer data is "just" business information, there is no trade secret. TechSoft argues the tool is valuable *because* competitors do not know about it. How should a court analyze the "independent economic value" element?

### 2. Options
A. The tool qualifies as having independent economic value because TechSoft would lose its competitive advantage if the tool became known, and competitors would immediately adopt it.

B. The tool does not have independent economic value protectable as a trade secret because the core algorithm is publicly published, and general business value derived merely from keeping something secret is insufficient.

C. The court must examine whether the *specific combination and implementation* of known elements provides economic value that competitors cannot replicate through legitimate means (reverse engineering, independent development, or using published sources), and whether that value flows from the confidentiality of the integration.

D. The tool has independent economic value if TechSoft can show it spent significant resources developing it and that disclosure would harm TechSoft's market position.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect, though it contains an element of truth.** While it is true that loss of competitive advantage would result from disclosure, this is not the correct test for "independent economic value." This formulation confuses the concept of "value dependent on secrecy" with "any competitive harm from disclosure." Under the UTSA and Restatement § 757, the question is not merely whether disclosure would harm the business, but whether the information derives *independent economic value* *because it is not generally known*. The distinction is subtle but critical: a competitor's published price list has immediate harm potential if disclosed, but it may not qualify as a trade secret if it is easily discerned from public market data. See the casebook discussion of what makes information "valuable precisely because it is secret."

**B. Incorrect.** While this option correctly identifies that published algorithms do not themselves constitute trade secrets, it oversimplifies the analysis. Trade secret law recognizes that a combination of public and confidential elements can constitute protectable information. If TechSoft's specific integration and implementation of the published algorithm with its proprietary customer database creates synergistic value that competitors cannot easily replicate through legitimate reverse engineering, that combination may qualify as a trade secret. The casebook (citing cases like *Hertz v. Luzenac* and *Compulife*) establishes that a novel combination of public-domain elements can be protectable if the combination is not readily ascertainable and has economic value. Simply because one component is public does not destroy the secrecy claim for the whole.

**C. Correct.** This is the legally precise test. Under the UTSA, "independent economic value" means the information must provide a competitive advantage specifically because others do not know about it (or cannot easily discover it). The court must ask: (1) Does the *specific implementation* of known components provide value that competitors could not readily replicate through legitimate means? (2) Would knowledge of this specific implementation give competitors an unfair jump? (3) Does the secrecy itself—rather than the inherent utility of the algorithm—drive the economic advantage? If TechSoft can show that the specific database, the particular weighting of variables, the integration architecture, or the validation thresholds are not readily ascertainable from analyzing TechSoft's outputs alone, then the compilation qualifies. The fact that the core algorithm is published is irrelevant if the implementation is sufficiently unique and not readily derivable. See *Altavion* (holding that a combination of known design concepts can be a protectable trade secret if the combination and its implementation are not readily ascertainable) and the casebook discussion distinguishing between general industry knowledge and specific proprietary knowledge.

**D. Incorrect.** Investment and market harm are factors that may support a claim, but they are not the same as "independent economic value." Many companies invest heavily in public domain information—a market research database, a compilation of published data—without creating protectable trade secrets. The "independent economic value" test is not satisfied merely by showing expense or market harm; it requires showing that the value flows from the information being secret (not generally known) and that competitors would gain substantial advantage from the secret.

***

## Question Q2.4 — "Not Generally Known": Testing the Boundaries of Relative Secrecy

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
PharmaCorp manufactures a generic drug and has developed a manufacturing process that combines five industry-standard chemical steps in a specific sequence and with specific temperature-control tolerances. The process is not patented. Two competitors independently have developed substantially similar processes. Five other competitors in the industry use similar sequences. However, PharmaCorp has never disclosed the specific tolerances, and the precise sequence of steps is not published in any accessible source. PharmaCorp requires all employees to sign NDAs, restricts access to the process documents, and maintains the details in a locked archive. Three years ago, a published industry whitepaper described the general concept of the five-step sequence, but did not detail PharmaCorp's specific implementation or tolerances. Is PharmaCorp's process "generally known" within the meaning of the UTSA?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because two other competitors have independently developed similar processes, demonstrating that the process is known or readily ascertainable by others in the industry.

B. No, because PharmaCorp has taken reasonable measures to maintain secrecy (NDAs, access restrictions), and the specific implementation details and tolerances have not been publicly disclosed.

C. No, simply because some competitors use similar approaches does not mean that PharmaCorp's specific tolerances and implementation are "generally known"—the question is whether the particular details are widespread or easily accessible, not whether the general concept exists elsewhere.

D. Yes, because the general concept was published in a whitepaper three years ago, and therefore the core knowledge is in the public domain.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Independent development by competitors does not defeat trade secret status. The UTSA explicitly provides that independently developed information and reverse-engineered information are not misappropriated. However, independent development by others does not render PharmaCorp's information "generally known" in the relevant sense. "Generally known" means not known by competitors in the relevant field due to public disclosure, industry publication, or widespread practice. That a small number of competitors stumbled upon similar approaches through independent means does not make PharmaCorp's specific implementation generally known. See *Winston Research Corp. v. 3M* (holding that independent development does not constitute misappropriation) and the UTSA's carve-out for proper means of acquisition. The question here is not whether misappropriation occurred, but whether the information was "generally known" in the first place.

**B. Partially correct but incomplete.** While PharmaCorp's reasonable efforts at maintaining secrecy support the conclusion that the information is not "generally known," this option misses the substantive inquiry. A company can maintain an NDA and still lose trade secret status if the information is nonetheless widely known through other channels (publication, industry conference presentations, regulatory disclosures, etc.). The focus should be on the *actual state of knowledge* in the industry, not merely on PharmaCorp's efforts. That said, PharmaCorp's restrictive practices are evidence supporting the conclusion that the specific tolerances are not generally known.

**C. Correct.** This is the proper test. "Generally known" does not mean "known to some competitors" or "known elsewhere" in absolute terms; it means widely known or publicly accessible to those who could benefit from the information. If PharmaCorp's specific tolerances and implementation sequence have not been disclosed to the industry at large—through patents, publications, regulatory filings, industry conferences, or widespread practice—then they are not "generally known" merely because a handful of competitors have independently reinvented similar (but not identical) approaches. The fact that others use "similar" processes does not mean PharmaCorp's particular tolerances are generally known. The whitepaper disclosed the general five-step concept but not PharmaCorp's specific implementation, which distinguishes this case from one where the precise details were published. See *PepsiCo* (finding PCNA's pricing strategy not generally known despite industry knowledge of general pricing concepts) and the Board of Trade case (holding that information distributed to a limited group under confidentiality conditions remains protectable even if some people know it).

**D. Incorrect.** Publication of a general concept does not automatically destroy trade secret status for the specific implementation. The UTSA and case law distinguish between general knowledge (the idea that five steps can work) and specific knowledge (PharmaCorp's particular tolerances and sequencing). If the whitepaper did not disclose PharmaCorp's specific tolerances or the precise sequence PharmaCorp uses, then the fact that the general concept is in the public domain does not render those specific details "generally known." See *Altavion* (distinguishing between general DST concepts disclosed to investors and the specific design concepts that remained secret).

***

## Question Q2.5 — "Not Readily Ascertainable": The Standard of Difficulty and Proper Means

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
SensorTech Corp. manufactures advanced motion sensors and has developed a proprietary calibration algorithm that enhances sensitivity and reduces false positives. The algorithm is implemented in the firmware of SensorTech's commercial sensor devices, which are sold on the open market to customers. The algorithm combines known statistical methods (published in academic literature) with proprietary weighting factors and thresholds that SensorTech discovered through six years of testing. A competitor purchases SensorTech's sensor, disassembles it, and attempts to reverse-engineer the firmware. The competitor can analyze the algorithm's structure and the general approach but cannot determine the exact weighting factors and threshold values without extensive further testing (estimated at 18 months and $500,000 in engineering costs). SensorTech claims the algorithm is a trade secret because it is not "readily ascertainable." Is this claim likely to succeed?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the specific weighting factors and thresholds are not visible in the disassembled firmware and would require substantial time and money to reverse-engineer.

B. No, because the product is sold on the open market and the competitor can legally purchase and reverse-engineer it, making the algorithm "readily ascertainable" under trade secret law.

C. Yes, because trade secret law prohibits reverse engineering of commercial products, and therefore the competitor's efforts to disassemble the sensor are improper means of acquisition.

D. It depends on whether the cost and effort required to reverse-engineer (18 months, $500,000) are considered "trivial" or "routine" within the industry—if such efforts are substantial and beyond normal competitive investigation, the algorithm may remain protectable.

### 3. Correct Answer
D

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Partially correct but too categorical.** The fact that specific values are not immediately visible does not automatically establish that the algorithm is not "readily ascertainable." While the difficulty of discovering the values is relevant, the legal test requires analyzing whether *legitimate means* could reasonably uncover the information. The problem with this option is that it conflates "difficult to reverse-engineer" with "not readily ascertainable." If the effort is within the range of what a skilled competitor would ordinarily undertake (even if costly), the information may be considered readily ascertainable. See the discussion in the casebook of the "difficulty/cost" standard for ascertainability.

**B. Incorrect in its conclusion, though it identifies a key principle.** While it is true that reverse engineering of commercially available products is a *proper means* of acquisition (and therefore, if the competitor uses reverse engineering, there is no misappropriation), this does not automatically mean the algorithm is "readily ascertainable." A product can be openly sold while still containing trade secrets. For example, the competitor's ability to *access* the product for reverse engineering (through purchase) is proper, but the question is whether the *specific information* they seek is readily derivable from that access. If 18 months and $500,000 of engineering effort are required, the information may still be protectable even though reverse engineering is a proper means. See *E.I. du Pont de Nemours & Co. v. Christopher* and the casebook discussion: proper means include reverse engineering, but only if the reverse engineering effort is not extraordinary.

**C. Incorrect.** Reverse engineering is explicitly a *proper means* under the UTSA and Restatement § 757. The statute does not prohibit reverse engineering; in fact, it defines "improper means" to *exclude* reverse engineering and independent derivation. The competitor's disassembly and analysis, if done without breach of contract or deception, is lawful. The question is not whether reverse engineering is allowed, but whether, despite the competitor's ability to reverse-engineer, the specific information remains "not readily ascertainable" through that proper means.

**D. Correct.** This captures the proper legal analysis. Trade secret law does not give owners an absolute monopoly; it protects against improper means. However, even when proper means (reverse engineering) are available, the UTSA protects information that is not "readily ascertainable" through those proper means. The standard depends on the *practical difficulty and cost* for a skilled competitor to acquire the same information through legitimate investigation. If the effort required is routine or trivial—for example, if the weighting factors could be inferred from a few simple tests—then the information is readily ascertainable. If the effort is extraordinary or beyond what a typical competitor would undertake—18 months, $500,000, specialized testing facilities—then the information may remain protectable even if the competitor *could* eventually derive it through legitimate means. The casebook emphasizes that the "readily ascertainable" test is contextual and depends on industry norms and capabilities. However, SensorTech faces a difficult burden here: if the competitor can eventually derive the values through reverse engineering (even if it takes 18 months), courts may ultimately hold the information is "readily ascertainable," and any competitor would not be liable for misappropriation if they reverse-engineered rather than stole. See *Bonito Boats, Inc. v. Thunder Craft Boats, Inc.* (holding that absent contractual restrictions, reverse engineering is permissible).

***

## Question Q2.6 — Compilation Secrets: Novel Combinations of Public Elements

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
MarketData Corp. operates a subscription service that compiles and curates publicly available information about real estate prices, housing inventory, neighborhood trends, and economic indicators into a single, integrated database with custom search and analysis tools. Each individual data point in the database is obtained from public sources: government databases (MLS listings, tax records, census data), news articles, and economic reports. MarketData's value proposition is the *specific combination* and *organization* of these public sources, along with proprietary algorithms for weighting and analyzing the data to predict neighborhood trends. None of these sources individually are secret, but MarketData claims that the database, as a compilation and integrated tool, is a trade secret. MarketData restricts access, requires confidentiality agreements, and has not disclosed its weighting algorithms or data organization methodology. A former employee leaves and attempts to recreate a similar database by gathering the same public sources, but without using MarketData's original database. Is MarketData's database likely protectable as a trade secret?

### 2. Options
A. No, because each individual component is from public sources, trade secret law does not protect compilations based on publicly available information.

B. Yes, because MarketData has taken measures to keep the compilation secret, and the former employee's attempt to recreate it shows that it has economic value.

C. It depends on whether MarketData can show that the specific selection, organization, weighting methodology, and combination of public data creates economic value not readily ascertainable from those individual public sources, and whether a competitor would require significant time and effort to independently recreate the compilation.

D. No, because a former employee who independently recreates the database from public sources has not misappropriated anything, and therefore the source material cannot be a trade secret.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** This is an overreaching interpretation of trade secret law. While individual public elements are not protectable, trade secret law explicitly recognizes "compilations" as a form of protectable information. The UTSA and Restatement § 757 both identify "compilation" as an example of protectable information. The fact that a compilation is built from public sources does not automatically disqualify it. If the *selection, arrangement, and organization* of those public elements is not generally known and provides competitive advantage, the compilation can be a trade secret. See *Altavion* (establishing that a combination of known design concepts can be protectable if the specific combination is not readily ascertainable).

**B. Insufficient.** While secrecy and economic value are necessary elements, they are not sufficient on their own. Many companies take measures to keep public information secret; a mere phone book would not become a trade secret simply by placing it under lock and key. The question is whether the compilation itself meets the UTSA test: (1) Is it information? Yes, a compilation is explicitly covered. (2) Does it have independent economic value from not being generally known? That depends on whether the specific combination and arrangement provide value that competitors cannot readily derive from the same public sources. (3) Are reasonable efforts being made to maintain secrecy? Yes, in this scenario. But element (2) is the real issue.

**C. Correct.** This is the proper legal test for compilation secrets. Under *Altavion* and the casebook materials on compilation secrets (citing cases like *Compulife* and references in the textbook), a compilation can be protectable even if its components are in the public domain, provided that: (a) the *specific selection, arrangement, and combination* provide competitive value that is not generally known in the industry, and (b) a competitor would require significant independent effort to recreate the compilation from the same sources. In this case, if MarketData's weighting methodology, its particular selection of which data points to include, its organizational structure, and its algorithmic approach are not publicly disclosed and not readily inferable from analyzing MarketData's outputs, then the compilation may qualify. The test is not whether each component is secret, but whether the *integration and methodology* are proprietary and not readily ascertainable. A competitor could theoretically gather the same public sources, but if it would take them months or years to independently derive the same weighting scheme, organizational structure, and analytical approach, then the compilation remains protectable.

**D. Incorrect.** This option confuses the concepts of misappropriation and trade secret status. Whether the former employee's independent recreation constitutes misappropriation is a separate question from whether the source material is a trade secret. If the employee used only public sources and properly acquired information, there is no misappropriation. However, this does not mean MarketData's database is not a trade secret. The database remains a trade secret if it satisfies the statutory test. The former employee's inability to misappropriate it does not eliminate its status as a protected trade secret; it simply means the former employee did not commit tort liability through improper means. Competitors can build their own databases from public sources without liability, but the original compilation can still be a trade secret. See *Bonito Boats* (reverse engineering is proper, but the underlying information may still be a trade secret).

***

## Question Q2.7 — Reasonable Efforts to Maintain Secrecy: NDAs Alone Insufficient

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
FinanceFlow Corp. has developed proprietary software for algorithmic trading. The company requires all employees to sign nondisclosure agreements that explicitly state the software is confidential and subject to trade secret protection. However, FinanceFlow's security practices are inconsistent. While key technical specifications are kept in a locked server, FinanceFlow regularly demonstrates its software to prospective investors, venture capital firms, and potential clients without requiring them to sign NDAs—only verbal confidentiality "reminders" are given. FinanceFlow also publishes case studies on its website describing the software's general approach and capabilities. Additionally, several employees discuss the software's features at industry conferences, and FinanceFlow's marketing materials highlight the proprietary algorithms in promotional videos. When a former employee takes the source code to a competitor, FinanceFlow sues for trade secret misappropriation. The competitor argues that FinanceFlow failed to take reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy. How should a court analyze this claim?

### 2. Options
A. FinanceFlow has taken reasonable efforts because it required employee NDAs, maintained the source code in a locked server, and made verbal confidentiality statements to investors.

B. FinanceFlow has not taken reasonable efforts because it disclosed the software's general capabilities and approach in marketing materials and at industry conferences, showing inconsistent treatment of the information.

C. The court should examine the *totality of circumstances* to determine whether FinanceFlow's actual practices reasonably maintained secrecy—weighing the restrictive measures (NDAs, server locks) against the extensive public disclosures and inconsistent confidentiality protocols for outside parties, and considering whether FinanceFlow's own conduct undermines the claim that it treated the information as confidential.

D. FinanceFlow has taken reasonable efforts as a matter of law, because the company used multiple security methods (NDAs, server locks, verbal statements) and required employees to maintain confidentiality.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While the measures identified—NDAs, locked servers, verbal confidentiality reminders—are positive factors, they are not sufficient on their own. More importantly, they must be viewed in the context of FinanceFlow's *overall conduct*. A company cannot take some security steps while simultaneously engaging in practices that undermine secrecy. The fact that FinanceFlow requires employee NDAs is undermined by its practice of demonstrating the software to outsiders without adequate written confidentiality protections. See *nClosures Inc. v. Block & Co.* (holding that an NDA alone is insufficient if a company's actual conduct demonstrates that the information is not being treated as confidential).
