The contradiction between locked-server security and public-facing disclosures signals to a court that FinanceFlow did not truly regard the information as confidential.

**B. Incorrect in its reasoning, though it reaches a defensible conclusion.** The problem with this option is that it treats all public disclosures equally. Not all disclosures destroy trade secret status; what matters is whether the disclosures are controlled and accompanied by confidentiality agreements. For example, FinanceFlow's case studies describing "general approach and capabilities" might be permissible disclosures if they do not reveal the specific source code or algorithms. The real issue is the *inconsistency*: FinanceFlow requires strict NDAs from employees but gives verbal-only confidentiality statements to investors and allows employees to discuss details at conferences. This signals a company that does not consistently treat information as confidential.

**C. Correct.** This is the proper legal approach, consistent with *nClosures* and the casebook's teaching on reasonable efforts. Courts examine the totality of circumstances to determine whether a company's *actual practices* reasonably maintained secrecy. The analysis requires balancing: (1) the restrictive measures FinanceFlow took (NDAs, server security); (2) the public disclosures FinanceFlow made (case studies, conference discussions, marketing videos, investor demonstrations); and (3) the consistency or inconsistency of these practices. FinanceFlow's requirement of employee NDAs is positive, but its public-facing disclosures and verbal-only confidentiality statements to outside parties suggest the company did not consistently regard the information as confidential. A court would likely find that FinanceFlow failed to take reasonable efforts because the company's own conduct undermined its secrecy claims. The key is not whether FinanceFlow took *any* security measures, but whether the company's practices were *consistent* with the view that the information was confidential. See the casebook discussion: "Reasonable efforts do not require absolute lockdown, but they do require a consistent story" that the information is treated as confidential. FinanceFlow's mixed signals—strict security in some areas, casual disclosure in others—would likely tip the balance against finding reasonable efforts.

**D. Incorrect.** This option treats "reasonable efforts" as a box-checking exercise. Trade secret law does not award protection as a matter of law simply because a company used multiple security measures. The court must examine whether those measures, in light of the company's actual practices, reasonably maintained secrecy. A company cannot claim the benefit of reasonable-efforts protection if its own conduct—like public disclosures, employee conference presentations, or unwritten confidentiality statements to investors—undermines the secrecy claim.

***

## Question Q2.8 — Inevitable Disclosure and Threatened Misappropriation

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
SalesMetrics Inc. develops proprietary sales forecasting algorithms and market analysis tools. Sarah Kim, a Senior Vice President at SalesMetrics, has deep knowledge of SalesMetrics's three-year strategic plan, detailed customer pricing strategies, new product launches planned for 2026, and R&D roadmaps. Kim signs a confidentiality agreement and an employment agreement containing a non-compete clause. Without warning, Kim announces she is joining TechRival Corp. as Chief Marketing Officer, where she will have direct responsibility for product strategy, pricing, and go-to-market decisions for TechRival's competing forecasting platform. SalesMetrics immediately learns that Kim and TechRival are setting up a "firewall" (an ethical wall preventing Kim from directly accessing or disclosing SalesMetrics confidential information) and that TechRival will require Kim to sign a supplemental NDA reiterating that she will not use or disclose SalesMetrics information. SalesMetrics sues for a preliminary injunction based on "inevitable disclosure"—the theory that Kim's intimate knowledge of SalesMetrics's strategy will *inevitably* be used in her new role, even if unintentionally. Can SalesMetrics obtain an injunction?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because Kim has access to sensitive strategic information and will be in a competing role, making disclosure inevitable as a practical matter.

B. No, because Kim has signed an NDA with TechRival and has agreed to a firewall arrangement, and courts cannot enjoin employees from working for competitors merely based on their knowledge of confidential information.

C. It depends on whether SalesMetrics can show that Kim's new role will "necessarily" and "inevitably" require her to rely on SalesMetrics's trade secrets—that is, whether there is a "high degree of probability of inevitable and immediate use of trade secrets" that makes it impractical for Kim to perform her job without relying on confidential information.

D. No, because inevitable disclosure is a doctrine applicable only in patent cases, not trade secret cases.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While the factors identified—access to sensitive information and a competing role—are relevant, they are not sufficient to support an injunction on their own. Courts recognize the tension between protecting trade secrets and allowing employee mobility. Simply having access to information or being in a competing role does not automatically justify enjoining an employee from taking the new job. The question is whether the employee's new role would make it practically *impossible* to avoid using the trade secrets. See *PepsiCo v. Redmond* (distinguishing between general knowledge or skills and the kind of specific, detailed information that would make inevitable disclosure predictable). Many employees change employers within an industry; not all such moves justify pre-emptive injunctions.

**B. Incorrect in its reasoning, though it captures one important principle.** While it is true that courts are cautious about enjoining employee mobility and that confidentiality agreements and firewalls are relevant factors, this option overstates the protection for employees. Courts *can* enjoin threatened misappropriation, including based on inevitable disclosure, if the circumstances demonstrate a genuine threat. However, the mere existence of an NDA and a firewall arrangement is not automatically dispositive; courts examine whether these protections are realistic given the circumstances. See *PepsiCo* (granting a preliminary injunction despite the defendant's promises not to disclose information). The question is not whether injunctions are ever appropriate, but whether the specific circumstances here show a realistic threat.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the legal standard established in *PepsiCo v. Redmond* and reinforced in the casebook. The test for inevitable disclosure is whether there is a "high degree of probability of inevitable and immediate use of trade secrets." Courts look at: (1) whether the employee's new role will necessarily require decisions or actions that would *require* the use of the trade secrets, (2) whether there is a factual basis for believing the employee cannot compartmentalize the information, and (3) whether the employee's conduct before departure shows bad faith or deception. In this scenario, SalesMetrics might have a strong case if it can show that TechRival's competitive platform directly competes with SalesMetrics's offerings in areas where Kim has detailed knowledge of SalesMetrics's strategy. For example, if TechRival's new CMO role requires decisions on pricing strategy, product positioning, or launch timing for a competing product, and SalesMetrics can show that TechRival's strategy decisions would directly implicate the confidential information Kim possesses, then inevitable disclosure might be found. However, SalesMetrics must show more than mere opportunity for disclosure; it must show practical inevitability. The firewall and supplemental NDA are relevant but not determinative. See *PepsiCo* (court noted that assurances from the defendant are "less than comforting" when the employee will necessarily be making decisions that would invoke confidential knowledge). If SalesMetrics can demonstrate that Redmond's new responsibilities would require him to make decisions that necessarily implicate PCNA's trade secrets, that would support an injunction, despite the firewall.

**D. Incorrect.** The inevitable-disclosure doctrine applies to trade secret law, not just patent law. The UTSA explicitly permits injunctions against "threatened misappropriation," and courts have applied this language to support preliminary injunctions based on inevitable disclosure. See *PepsiCo*.

***

## Question Q2.9 — Distinguishing Between "General Concept" and "Trade Secret Implementation"

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
Chemical Corp. has invented a novel industrial catalyst that significantly improves the efficiency of a specific chemical reaction used in manufacturing. Chemical Corp. disclosed the general concept of the catalyst to three potential licensing partners during confidential negotiations (under NDAs). Each potential partner learned that: (1) ChemicalCorp is developing an efficiency-enhancing catalyst; (2) it works by modifying the reaction temperature and pressure; (3) preliminary tests show 30% improvement. However, ChemicalCorp did not disclose the specific molecular composition of the catalyst, the exact proportions of its components, the precise temperature and pressure ranges required, the catalytic mechanism, or ChemicalCorp's validation data. All three potential partners declined the licensing offer. One former ChemicalCorp employee later joins a competitor and shares the general concept (which he heard about during a company meeting open to the research team) but not the specific technical details. The competitor independently develops a catalyst based on similar principles and achieves comparable efficiency gains. ChemicalCorp sues for trade secret misappropriation, arguing that it disclosed the general concept only to potential licensees under NDA, while keeping the specific implementation secret. Does ChemicalCorp have a protectable trade secret?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because ChemicalCorp disclosed only the general concept to the licensing partners, not the specific implementation details, and the general concept was disclosed only to a small group under NDAs.

B. No, because ChemicalCorp disclosed the general concept (catalyst for efficiency improvement via temperature and pressure modification) to potential partners, making the core idea not "generally known" but sufficiently known to lose protection.

C. It depends on whether the general concept itself (efficiency-enhancing catalyst modifying temperature and pressure) is distinguishable from the trade-secret implementation details (specific composition, proportions, temperature ranges, validation data), and whether the disclosure to licensing partners was limited to the general concept without revealing implementation details.

D. No, because a competitor can independently develop a catalyst with similar principles without liability, and this independent development proves the general concept is not a trade secret.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect in its reasoning, though it may reach the right conclusion.** This option assumes that because the general concept was disclosed only to three licensees under NDAs, it remains secret. However, this conflates "limited disclosure" with "not generally known." The question is whether the general concept itself has become known outside the small group of licensees. If three potential licensees knew the concept, and if the concept itself (independent of the implementation details) is not difficult to reverse-engineer from commercial products, then the general concept may not be protectable as a trade secret. What matters is whether each *layer* of the information qualifies separately. See *Altavion* (distinguishing between general ideas disclosed to investors and specific design details kept secret).

**B. Partially correct but imprecise.** This option correctly identifies that disclosure of the general concept to outside parties (licensees) affects protectability, but it conflates the general concept with the implementation. The proper analysis requires *separately* evaluating each layer: (1) Is the general concept (catalyst improves efficiency via T/P modification) itself protectable? Probably not, if it has been disclosed to three parties. (2) Are the specific implementation details (molecular composition, proportions, exact ranges, mechanism) protectable? Possibly yes, if they have not been disclosed and are not readily ascertainable. The option fails to maintain this critical distinction.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the *Altavion* approach to layered information. Courts must examine whether the general idea and the specific implementation are *separate* layers of information, each of which must qualify independently. In this scenario: (1) The general concept (efficiency-enhancing catalyst via T/P modification) may not be protectable because it was disclosed to three licensing partners. However, that disclosure may not destroy protection for the *specific implementation details*. (2) If the specific molecular composition, proportions, temperature ranges, and mechanism are not disclosed to the licensees and are not readily ascertainable from analyzing ChemicalCorp's products, then those details may remain protectable trade secrets even if the general concept is not. This is consistent with *Altavion*, which found that while a general DST concept was disclosed to investors, the specific design concepts underlying the implementation could still be protectable trade secrets. The key question is: Would a competitor who knew the general concept still need to undertake substantial independent research to discover ChemicalCorp's specific implementation? If yes, the implementation may be protectable. See the casebook discussion distinguishing between the "forest" (general concept) and the "trees" (specific implementation).

**D. Incorrect, though it identifies a principle.** While it is true that independent development does not constitute misappropriation, this does not mean the underlying information is not a trade secret. A secret can remain a trade secret even if a competitor independently discovers it; the competitor simply has not committed tort liability. The question here is whether ChemicalCorp's specific implementation details are trade secrets, not whether the competitor's independent development is permissible (it is). If ChemicalCorp's specific composition, proportions, and validation data are not readily ascertainable and have not been publicly disclosed (even though the general concept was disclosed to licensees), then those details remain protectable as trade secrets—the competitor's independent development is simply a defense to any misappropriation claim based on improper acquisition.

***

## Question Q2.10 — Loss of Secrecy Through Accidental Disclosure

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
TechVenture Inc. has developed proprietary code for a machine learning model used in medical diagnostics. The code is protected by strict access controls, employee NDAs, and confidentiality markings. A junior programmer at TechVenture accidentally uploads the source code to a public GitHub repository without authorization. The error is caught and corrected within 24 hours, but GitHub's caching and archived copies mean the code remains accessible to anyone who knew to look for it during that 24-hour window. A competitor downloads the code during that window. Three weeks later, TechVenture discovers the breach. TechVenture sues the competitor for trade secret misappropriation, claiming the brief public exposure does not destroy the trade secret status of the code. Is TechVenture likely to succeed?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the code was publicly accessible for only 24 hours, and TechVenture can show it took reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy and quickly remedied the breach.

B. No, because once the code was publicly accessible, even briefly, it became "generally known" and lost its trade secret status irreversibly.

C. It depends on whether TechVenture can show that: (1) the accidental disclosure was beyond the company's reasonable control, (2) the company acted promptly to mitigate the disclosure after discovering the breach, and (3) the practical effect of the brief exposure was limited (for example, many people may not have known to look for it, or downstream dissemination was minimal).

D. No, because TechVenture's failure to implement technical controls to prevent accidental uploads to public repositories demonstrates that its security was not "reasonable under the circumstances."

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect in its reasoning.** While it is true that brief exposure and quick remediation are relevant factors, they are not sufficient to guarantee that trade secret status is preserved. The question is not whether TechVenture took reasonable efforts *before* the breach, but whether it can show that the *accidental* nature of the disclosure and the *limited practical effect* of the brief exposure allow the information to retain trade secret status despite the exposure. Simply having a short window (24 hours) does not automatically preserve secrecy.

**B. Incorrect.** While public disclosure generally destroys trade secret status, trade secret law recognizes that limited, brief, accidental, or geographically restricted disclosures may not permanently destroy secrecy if the information does not become truly "generally known" in the sense of being widely disseminated and accessible to competitors. The UTSA defines a trade secret in part by reference to information "not generally known," not information "never exposed." If the information remained largely inaccessible despite the technical exposure (for example, few people knew to look for it in that specific GitHub repository), it might retain protection. Additionally, some jurisdictions recognize that information may regain trade secret status after accidental disclosure if the owner takes prompt action to prevent further dissemination and if the information never truly became "generally known" in practical terms.

**C. Correct.** This reflects a nuanced approach to accidental disclosure, consistent with the UTSA and case law principles. The analysis requires examining: (1) Whether the disclosure was truly accidental and beyond the company's reasonable control (a junior programmer's upload error falls into this category, though TechVenture's failure to implement technical controls preventing unauthorized uploads might cut against this). (2) Whether TechVenture acted promptly to mitigate the breach (discovering it within 24 hours and taking it down quickly shows reasonable response). (3) Whether the practical effect of the disclosure was limited (for example, if the GitHub repository was not widely indexed, or if few people had reason to know to access it, the information may not have truly become "generally known" despite technical accessibility). Additionally, courts may consider whether the competitor's acquisition of the code through the accidental disclosure constitutes "knowing" acquisition from improper means, even if the disclosure was brief. The key insight is that "generally known" is not purely a function of technical accessibility; it is about practical, functional knowledge in the industry. See *UTSA* provisions and case law recognizing that brief or accidental disclosures may not destroy trade secret status if they do not result in widespread, practical knowledge among competitors.

**D. Incorrect.** While a failure to implement reasonable technical controls could cut against TechVenture's claim that it exercised "reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy," it does not automatically destroy trade secret status. The question is whether TechVenture's security measures, taken as a whole, were reasonable. A company might have strong access controls and employee NDAs but still fail to prevent all accidental uploads. Modern trade secret law recognizes that no security system is perfect, and "reasonable" does not mean "perfect." However, TechVenture's failure to implement upload-prevention controls might support an argument that its overall security was not reasonable. This would be a closer call than option D suggests, and the outcome would depend on the totality of TechVenture's security practices.

***

## Question Q2.11 — Reasonable Efforts to Maintain Secrecy: Scope of Distribution

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
BioLabs Inc. has developed a proprietary genetic testing protocol that is significantly more accurate and cost-effective than industry-standard methods. BioLabs keeps the protocol confidential and restricts access to a team of 12 senior scientists, all of whom sign NDAs. However, BioLabs also operates a licensed laboratory in seven different cities, and the protocol is used by technicians in all seven locations (total of 40 technicians, each with varying levels of access to the protocol). BioLabs provides training on the protocol to all technicians but does not require the technicians to sign NDAs (only a general company confidentiality policy covers them). The technicians have access to the protocol documentation stored in a shared drive on BioLabs's internal network (with basic password protection). Has BioLabs taken "reasonable efforts" to maintain secrecy?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the protocol is kept within BioLabs's internal network with password protection, and access is restricted to employees who have agreed to confidentiality obligations through the general company policy.

B. No, because the protocol is not sufficiently restricted—40 technicians across seven locations have access, and not all of them have signed individual NDAs.

C. Yes, if BioLabs can show that all 40 technicians are bound by employment confidentiality obligations (through the general company policy), that access is limited to those with a need to know, and that BioLabs has implemented technical controls (password protection) and training to reinforce confidentiality.

D. No, because reasonable efforts require that all individuals with access to a trade secret sign explicit, individual NDAs; a general company confidentiality policy is insufficient to meet the statutory standard of "reasonable efforts."

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect in its reasoning, though it may be close to correct.** This option is too categorical and fails to engage with the real issue: whether distributing the protocol to 40 technicians across seven locations, while relying on a general company confidentiality policy rather than individual NDAs, constitutes "reasonable" efforts. While internal networks with password protection are relevant, they are not, by themselves, sufficient to demonstrate reasonableness when so many people have access.

**B. Incorrect.** This option wrongly assumes that individual NDAs are *required* for all recipients. While individual NDAs are a best practice, trade secret law does not mandate them. The UTSA requires only "reasonable efforts," not a specific form of protection. If BioLabs can show that all 40 technicians are bound by enforceable confidentiality obligations through their employment contracts and company policy, and that access is on a need-to-know basis with technical and procedural safeguards, the absence of individual NDAs may not defeat the reasonableness showing. However, relying solely on a general company policy (rather than explicit protocols for this specific sensitive information) may be risky.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the proper analysis under the UTSA's "reasonable efforts" standard. The test is not whether specific procedures are followed, but whether the *totality of measures* is reasonable given the circumstances. In this scenario, BioLabs would likely satisfy the standard if it can demonstrate: (1) All 40 technicians are bound by confidentiality obligations (either individual NDAs or enforceable employment-based confidentiality clauses in the company policy). (2) Access is limited to a legitimate "need-to-know" (technicians who actually use the protocol for testing). (3) Technical safeguards are in place (password-protected access, internal network restrictions). (4) Training and internal communications reinforce the confidential nature of the protocol. (5) The company takes prompt action against any suspected leaks or breaches. The fact that there are 40 technicians across seven locations is not, by itself, unreasonable—large companies routinely maintain trade secrets across multiple locations and employee groups. What matters is whether the company's *actual practices* demonstrate that the information is being treated as confidential. See the casebook discussion: "Reasonable efforts do not require absolute secrecy; they require efforts proportionate to the importance of the information and the size and nature of the organization." The fact that BioLabs uses the protocol in its operations (thereby involving 40 people) does not defeat the reasonableness showing if BioLabs implements appropriate safeguards.

**D. Incorrect.** This overstates the legal requirement. Trade secret law does not mandate individual NDAs for every person with access; it requires "reasonable efforts under the circumstances." A large company operating across multiple locations might reasonably rely on a combination of: employment-based confidentiality obligations in company policy, technical access controls, need-to-know restrictions, training, and monitoring. Individual NDAs are valuable but not mandatory in all contexts. A rigidly applied rule requiring individual NDAs would be impractical for large organizations and would not reflect the flexible standard the UTSA establishes.

***

## Question Q2.12 — Customer Lists: Identifying When Compilations Are Protectable

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
SalesForce Corp. maintains a customer database containing 5,000 customers for industrial cleaning products. The customer names, basic contact information, and industry classifications are not confidential—a competitor could identify these through public business directories, trade publications, industry databases, and online research. However, SalesForce's database also includes: (1) each customer's contract terms, pricing, and volume commitments (negotiated individually); (2) each customer's specific product preferences and usage patterns; (3) the history of each customer's interactions with SalesForce (including service calls, complaints, and preferences); and (4) SalesForce's assessment of each customer's likelihood to switch to a competitor. SalesForce restricts access to this database to sales and account management staff and requires confidentiality agreements. A former salesperson leaves to join a competitor and brings a spreadsheet containing all 5,000 customers with their contract terms, pricing, and SalesForce's competitive-vulnerability assessments. Is the customer database (or the spreadsheet containing the customer information) a protectable trade secret?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because SalesForce has restricted access and required confidentiality agreements, and customer lists are traditionally protectable as trade secrets.

B. No, because the customer names and contact information are publicly available, and therefore the database cannot be a trade secret.

C. It depends on whether the compilation as a whole (the specific selection, arrangement, and non-public data about each customer—including pricing, terms, preferences, and competitive assessments) provides economic value not readily ascertainable from public sources, and whether a competitor would incur substantial effort to independently develop equivalent information.

D. It depends on whether the customers themselves agreed to keep their identity and dealings with SalesForce confidential; without customer consent, the database cannot be a trade secret.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While it is true that customer lists can be protectable and that SalesForce's restrictive practices are relevant, this option is too categorical. Not all customer lists are protectable trade secrets. A list of names and phone numbers that are publicly available would not qualify. The answer depends on the nature and completeness of the information about each customer. See *PepsiCo* (recognizing that customer lists can be trade secrets, but only if they contain information not readily ascertainable from public sources).

**B. Incorrect.** This is an overstatement. The fact that customer *names* are publicly available does not mean that a *compilation* of customers combined with confidential information about their contracts, preferences, and competitive vulnerabilities is public. A compilation can be protectable even if some of its components are public. See *Altavion* (combination of known design concepts can be protectable) and the casebook discussion of compilation secrets. The database's value comes not from the customer names alone, but from the integration of the customer list with specific contract terms, pricing, preferences, and competitive assessments.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the proper legal analysis. A customer database or compilation can be a trade secret even if the basic customer names are publicly available, if the compilation includes non-public information about each customer. In this scenario, SalesForce's database includes: (1) contract terms and pricing (non-public, individually negotiated). (2) Product preferences and usage patterns (non-public, developed through relationship history). (3) Service history and customer interactions (non-public, proprietary to SalesForce). (4) Competitive vulnerability assessments (non-public, developed by SalesForce's analysis). A competitor who knows the customer names would still need to undertake substantial work to independently discover each customer's specific terms, preferences, usage patterns, and vulnerabilities. If SalesForce can show that this specific compilation—customers combined with their associated confidential information—is not readily ascertainable from public sources and has substantial economic value (because it enables targeted sales strategies and negotiations), then the compilation is protectable. See *PepsiCo* (affirming that customer lists combined with confidential contract terms and pricing information can be trade secrets) and the casebook's discussion of compilation secrets. The former salesperson's transfer of not just the names but the associated pricing, terms, and competitive assessments strongly supports trade secret status for the compilation.

**D. Incorrect.** Trade secret protection does not depend on customer consent or confidentiality. The trade secret owner (SalesForce) is the party who must maintain the secrecy, not the customers. Customers do not need to agree to keep their own identity secret; rather, they expect businesses to keep their contract terms, pricing, and dealings confidential. Customer lists have long been recognized as potentially protectable trade secrets of the business (the seller), not the customers. See *PepsiCo*.

***

## Question Q2.13 — "Economic Value from Secrecy" vs. "Inherent Utility"

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
DesignStudio Inc. has developed a proprietary interior design methodology combining color psychology, spatial analysis, and client psychology insights. The methodology is based partly on published research (available in design textbooks and articles), partly on industry-standard design principles (known to professional designers), and partly on DesignStudio's own original research and client experience data. DesignStudio has compiled this into a systematic framework that allows new designers to achieve consistent, high-quality results with 20% less client revision than industry average. The methodology has inherent utility—it would be valuable even if widely known, because it improves designer performance and client satisfaction. However, it is also valuable *because* competitors do not use it, which gives DesignStudio a competitive edge. Is DesignStudio's methodology protectable as a trade secret based on "independent economic value"?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the methodology has inherent utility and is valuable in the market, and competitors do not currently use it.

B. No, because the methodology is based partly on published research and industry-standard principles, the "independent economic value" element is not satisfied.

C. It depends on whether the methodology's value flows *primarily from the fact that competitors do not know about it* or whether the methodology would retain significant value even if widely disclosed; if the value is *dependent on secrecy*, it qualifies as having independent economic value.

D. Yes, because DesignStudio can point to specific, measurable benefits (20% fewer revisions), which demonstrates that the methodology has independent economic value beyond mere secrecy.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** This option conflates "valuable in the market" with "independent economic value from secrecy." The fact that something is valuable and that competitors do not yet use it does not satisfy the legal test for trade secret protection. Many valuable ideas and methods are not trade secrets. For example, a publicly known management technique might be valuable, but it would not be a trade secret merely because a particular competitor has not yet adopted it. See the casebook discussion: "Independent economic value means the value comes *because it is secret*, not merely that it is useful."

**B. Incorrect.** While it is true that methodologies based partly on published principles may face heightened scrutiny, the fact that some elements are publicly known does not automatically preclude protection. What matters is whether the *specific integration and application* of those elements provides value that flows from confidentiality. A synthesis of published principles can be a trade secret if the synthesis itself is not generally known and if competitors would gain advantage from learning it. See *Altavion* and the discussion of compilation secrets. The legal question is not whether all components are novel, but whether the combination and specific implementation are.

**C. Correct.** This captures the critical distinction at the heart of "independent economic value from not being generally known." The test is not whether the methodology is useful—it clearly is—but whether its value *depends on secrecy*. If DesignStudio's methodology would retain substantial value even if widely published and adopted by competitors (because inherent utility and efficacy provide value), then it might not meet the "independent economic value from secrecy" test in its strictest form. However, if the primary source of DesignStudio's competitive advantage is that competitors do not know about the methodology, then the value *is* dependent on secrecy. The legal distinction is subtle: A universally known management technique has "independent economic value" (value in execution), but it is not a *trade secret* because the economic value does not flow from secrecy. By contrast, if DesignStudio's competitive advantage would evaporate if the methodology became known (because all competitors would adopt it and eliminate DesignStudio's edge), then the economic value flows from secrecy and satisfies the test. The casebook emphasizes this distinction: "Independent economic value" under the UTSA means value that would be lost if the information were disclosed. See the discussion in Chapter 1 and the *Religious Technology Center* case on nonprofit contexts.

**D. Partially correct but incomplete.** While measurable benefits (20% fewer revisions) demonstrate utility and are relevant to showing economic value, they do not, by themselves, satisfy the "independent economic value from secrecy" test. A competitor who learns DesignStudio's methodology would gain the same 20% benefit. This shows that the methodology has utility independent of secrecy. The question is whether DesignStudio's competitive advantage depends on secrecy or on inherent superiority of the method. If the methodology's benefits are primarily from its quality (which would persist even if known), the value is not *dependent on* secrecy. However, if DesignStudio's competitive advantage comes from being the only firm using this methodology (such that adoption by competitors would eliminate the advantage), then the value does flow from secrecy.

***

## Question Q2.14 — Identification and Particularity of Trade Secrets in Litigation

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
AnalyticsCorp sues TechRival for trade secret misappropriation. In its complaint, AnalyticsCorp alleges that TechRival misappropriated "AnalyticsCorp's proprietary analytics algorithms and methodologies used to analyze customer behavior and predict market trends." AnalyticsCorp further describes this as "a collection of algorithms, data structures, and analytical techniques that AnalyticsCorp has developed through years of research and that competitors do not use." When TechRival moves to dismiss for failure to identify trade secrets with sufficient particularity (as required by case law on trade secret identification), AnalyticsCorp responds by pointing to a confidential document titled "Core Analytics Methodology" that describes various algorithmic approaches in general terms but does not identify specific mathematical formulas, data inputs, or implementation details. TechRival argues that AnalyticsCorp has failed to identify its trade secrets with the required "reasonable particularity." Must AnalyticsCorp identify each trade secret separately, or is a general description of a "collection of algorithms" sufficient?

### 2. Options
A. AnalyticsCorp's general description is sufficient, because the complaint adequately conveys the nature of the confidential information and provides TechRival with notice of the general subject matter.

B. AnalyticsCorp must separately identify each specific algorithm, data structure, and technique as a distinct trade secret, with mathematical formulas, data inputs, and implementation details for each.

C. AnalyticsCorp must identify its trade secrets with "reasonable particularity"—sufficient to separate them from general knowledge in the field and to permit TechRival to understand at least the boundaries of each alleged secret—but need not identify every specific implementation detail or formula at the pleading stage; discovery may clarify the boundaries.

D. The requirement of identifying trade secrets with particularity applies only after discovery; at the pleading stage, AnalyticsCorp may use general descriptions.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** A general description of "analytics algorithms and methodologies" is too vague to satisfy the legal standard. Case law (see *Altavion*) requires that trade secrets be identified with sufficient particularity to separate them from general knowledge and to allow the defendant to understand "at least the boundaries" of each alleged secret. A description that could apply to hundreds of possible algorithms and methodologies fails this test. TechRival would not be able to mount a meaningful defense if it does not know which specific methodologies or algorithms AnalyticsCorp claims are secret.

**B. Incorrect in requiring excessive detail.** While separate identification of distinct trade secrets is required, courts do not demand that parties identify every specific formula or implementation detail at the pleading stage. That level of detail may emerge through discovery. However, each trade secret must be identified with enough particularity to be distinguishable from other claimed secrets and from general industry knowledge. This option imposes an unreasonably high burden at the pleading stage.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the legal standard for pre-discovery identification of trade secrets, as established by cases like *Altavion* and referenced in the casebook. The requirement is "reasonable particularity"—not absolute specificity, but enough detail that: (1) The defendant can distinguish the alleged secret from general knowledge in the field. (2) The defendant understands the boundaries of the alleged secret. (3) The defendant can frame a meaningful defense. This might require AnalyticsCorp to identify, at the pleading stage, specific algorithmic categories or functional descriptions (e.g., "a proprietary algorithm for predicting customer lifetime value based on behavioral clustering using the following input variables: [list]" or "a data structure implementing a novel approach to feature weighting in neural networks"). AnalyticsCorp need not provide proprietary formulas or exact source code at the pleading stage, but it must provide enough detail that a reasonable competitor could understand the scope of what is claimed to be secret. Discovery can then clarify boundaries and reveal details.

**D. Incorrect.** The requirement to identify trade secrets with particularity applies before discovery begins. Courts (see *Altavion*) require that a plaintiff identify trade secrets "prior to commencing discovery." The purpose is to prevent plaintiffs from using the discovery process to fish for secrets or to mount overly broad claims. The identification requirement is a gating issue, not something that can be deferred until after discovery.

***

## Question Q2.15 — Reverse Engineering vs. "Easily Completed" Tasks

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
LabTech Corp. sells advanced laboratory equipment that includes proprietary software controlling the equipment's performance. The software is embedded in compiled, object code (not source code) and is stored in a read-only memory chip inside the equipment. A competitor purchases one of LabTech's devices and attempts to extract the software code by reading the memory chip directly, a process that requires specialized equipment costing $3,000 and approximately two hours of technical work by an experienced engineer. Once extracted, the code can be analyzed. The competitor successfully extracts and analyzes the code. LabTech argues this is improper acquisition (not "reverse engineering" but "theft") and seeks trade secret protection. The competitor argues this is legitimate reverse engineering of a commercially available product. Under trade secret law, is the competitor's extraction and analysis of the code a "proper means" of acquiring information, or improper means?

### 2. Options
A. The extraction is improper means (essentially theft) because the competitor is circumventing technical security measures (the embedded read-only memory) to access the code, not reverse engineering through legitimate market testing.

B. The extraction is proper reverse engineering because the competitor purchased the product on the open market and has the legal right to examine what it bought; the $3,000 in equipment and technical effort are ordinary costs of reverse engineering.

C. It depends on whether the competitor's extraction method is considered "routine" or "trivial" within the industry; if memory chip reading is a standard, readily available practice, it is likely proper reverse engineering, but if it requires specialized expertise or equipment beyond typical industry practice, it may be improper means.

D. The extraction cannot be improper means because LabTech sold the product on the open market without contractual restrictions, and trade secret law does not permit sellers to control how buyers analyze purchased products.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** The fact that the competitor circumvents technical security measures (the embedded read-only memory) does not automatically make the acquisition "improper." Courts distinguish between contractual circumvention (violating a license restriction) and technical circumvention (opening up a device). Reverse engineering often involves circumventing technical protections. See *Bonito Boats* (holding that reverse engineering of a publicly sold product is generally proper, even if it requires effort to disassemble and analyze). However, some methods of circumvention can be improper; the question depends on industry norms and the nature of the circumvention.

**B. Incorrect in treating all reverse engineering as equally "proper."** While purchasers do have the right to examine what they buy, trade secret law does not treat all methods of examination as equally proper. If the competitor obtained the code through breaking a contractual restriction (e.g., by violating a license agreement prohibiting disassembly), that would be improper despite the product being on the open market. Additionally, some methods of extraction can be so specialized or unauthorized that they cross the line into improper means. The question is not simply whether the competitor is entitled to buy the product, but whether the *method* of extraction is consistent with industry norms and proper reverse engineering.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the proper legal analysis. While reverse engineering of commercially available products is generally a proper means of acquiring information, the *method* of reverse engineering can vary. If memory chip reading and code extraction are considered routine, standard practices in the industry (like disassembling a device, testing its performance, or analyzing a purchased copy of software), then the competitor's actions constitute legitimate reverse engineering. However, if the method requires specialized, non-standard techniques or equipment not typically used for reverse engineering in the industry, or if it involves circumventing security measures in ways that go beyond normal disassembly, courts may find it improper. The standard is contextual: What would a skilled competitor in the industry normally do to understand a competitor's product? If the competitor's actions are within industry norms, they are proper. If they go beyond ordinary competitive investigation, they may be improper. See the casebook discussion of reverse engineering and the principle that "proper means" includes standard competitive investigation. In this case, if reading embedded memory chips is a standard practice in the laboratory equipment industry, the competitor's action is likely proper. If it requires unusual, specialized expertise or techniques, it might be improper.

**D. Incorrect.** While it is true that LabTech's sale of the product on the open market generally permits reverse engineering, this does not mean that *all* methods of analyzing the product are permissible. If LabTech's product includes a contractual license restricting disassembly (e.g., "License granted only for use; any disassembly or reverse engineering is prohibited"), then circumventing that license to extract the code could be improper, even though LabTech sold the product. Additionally, certain methods of extraction can be improper even without a contract. The question is not whether LabTech retained rights by selling the product, but whether the competitor's specific method of extraction is consistent with proper reverse engineering.

***

## Question Q2.16 — Trade Secrets in Employment Termination Context: Head Start Injunctions

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
ServicePro Corp. discovers that a former employee, James Chen, has taken proprietary client files, pricing strategies, and service delivery procedures when he left to form a competing business. The information is clearly a trade secret—ServicePro took reasonable security measures, the information is not generally known, and competitors would gain substantial advantage from it. ServicePro proves that Chen acquired the information through breach of his employment agreement and nondisclosure agreement. However, Chen's new company is already operational and has been serving customers for 2 months using information derived from ServicePro's files. ServicePro seeks a permanent injunction barring Chen from using the misappropriated information indefinitely. Chen argues that a permanent injunction is too harsh; he proposes instead a "head start" injunction that would bar him from using ServicePro's information for a limited period (e.g., 18 months), allowing him time to independently develop the services and data he would have developed had he not used the trade secrets. How should a court analyze this request?

### 2. Options
A. The court should grant the permanent injunction, because Chen's breach was willful and the trade secrets were clearly protected, and any lesser remedy would permit him to profit from his wrongdoing.

B. The court may impose a "head start" injunction instead of a permanent one if it determines that Chen's independent efforts could reasonably produce equivalent information within the specified period, and if a permanent injunction would be disproportionately harsh given the circumstances.

C. The court must choose between a permanent injunction (if Chen's breach was willful) or no relief (if Chen's use was minimal); intermediate relief like a "head start" injunction is not available under trade secret law.

D. The court should decline to enjoin Chen at all, because he has already operated for 2 months without being stopped, which shows that any competitive harm has already occurred and cannot be remedied by future injunction.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect in being too categorical.** While permanent injunctions are often appropriate in trade secret cases, particularly when the breach was willful, courts have discretion to craft remedies proportionate to the circumstances. A permanent injunction that effectively prevents the defendant from ever operating in the field might be disproportionate if the defendant could reasonably develop equivalent information through independent effort within a defined period. Courts balance the need to protect the trade secret holder against the interest in permitting reasonable competition. See *American Can Co. v. Mansukhani* (awarding a permanent injunction, but with discussion of possible "head start" relief in other contexts). The casebook notes that courts have discretion to "transform a strict ban into a reasonable royalty arrangement" in some circumstances.

**B. Correct.** This reflects the principle of proportionate equitable relief in trade secret cases. Courts have discretion to impose "head start" injunctions that prevent the defendant from using misappropriated information for a defined period equal to the approximate time and effort it would have taken the defendant to independently develop equivalent information. The logic is: The defendant should not profit from the misappropriation, but once the defendant has had time to independently develop what it would have developed lawfully, the injunction lifts. For example, if it would have taken Chen 18 months to independently develop the same service delivery procedures and client database through legitimate research, the court might impose an 18-month injunction rather than a permanent one. This balances ServicePro's interest in preventing profit from the misappropriation against Chen's ability to compete on equal footing after the "head start" period. The court may also consider: (1) The willfulness of the breach (more willful conduct may warrant longer injunctions). (2) The time and cost that would have been required for independent development. (3) Whether the defendant has already gotten a commercial advantage that cannot be fully remedied by the injunction period. See the UTSA § 1836(b)(3)(A), which provides that a court "may grant an injunction on such terms as it deems reasonable" and may consider fashioning remedies that are proportionate.

**C. Incorrect.** Trade secret law permits intermediate forms of relief, including "head start" injunctions, royalty arrangements, and other tailored remedies. The UTSA explicitly gives courts discretion to craft appropriate relief beyond a simple all-or-nothing choice. The casebook notes that courts have flexibility in fashioning remedies.

**D. Incorrect.** The fact that time has passed and Chen has already competed does not eliminate the basis for injunctive relief. The court must still consider whether further use of the misappropriated information would cause additional harm. Additionally, future injunctive relief is intended to prevent ongoing harm, not merely to remedy past harm. The 2-month head start Chen already obtained is one factor the court might consider in calculating the length of any injunction, but it does not eliminate the availability of prospective relief.

***

## Question Q2.17 — Scope of Confidentiality: Information at the Margins

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
FashionTech Inc. develops proprietary sizing algorithms for online apparel sales. The core algorithm (which calculates body measurements from digital photos using machine learning) is clearly a trade secret. However, FashionTech also maintains information about: (1) general industry benchmarks for fabric shrinkage rates (publicly available from textile trade associations); (2) FashionTech's specific customer satisfaction data on how its sizing performs versus competitors (internal proprietary data); (3) historical fit preferences by demographic group (compiled by FashionTech from customer survey data and anonymized transaction data); and (4) FashionTech's pricing model for sizing consultations (markup percentages and cost allocations). When a former employee leaves and provides a competitor with a document labeled "Sizing System Overview" that includes discussions of all four categories above, FashionTech claims the employee misappropriated multiple trade secrets. The competitor argues that some of the information is too tangential or insufficiently proprietary to qualify as trade secrets. Which of the information categories above is FashionTech most likely to have difficulty establishing as a trade secret?

### 2. Options
A. The general industry benchmarks for fabric shrinkage (category 1), because information publicly available from trade associations is "generally known" and therefore not protectable.

B. FashionTech's customer satisfaction data (category 2), because customer opinion data is subjective and may not have "independent economic value."

C. The historical fit preferences by demographic group (category 3), because this information, while proprietary, is based on aggregated customer responses that individual customers might not regard as confidential.

D. FashionTech's pricing model (category 4), because pricing information is subject to public market forces and therefore inherently cannot have value from secrecy.

### 3. Correct Answer
A

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Correct.** General industry benchmarks for fabric shrinkage, if they are publicly available from textile trade associations or published industry sources, are "generally known" and cannot be protected as trade secrets. Information that is widely available to others in the industry lacks the "not generally known" element. FashionTech cannot claim ownership of publicly available information merely by including it in a proprietary document. This category of information is unambiguously not protectable. See *UTSA* definition and the casebook discussion of "generally known" information.

**B. Incorrect.** FashionTech's customer satisfaction data is proprietary to FashionTech—it is not publicly available and has independent economic value. Data comparing FashionTech's sizing performance to competitors would have substantial value to competitors and is likely not readily ascertainable. While customer opinion data is inherently subjective, subjective data can still be a trade secret if it is not generally known and has competitive value. For example, market research data, customer preference studies, and satisfaction metrics are routinely protected as trade secrets.

**C. Incorrect.** While the data is based on aggregated customer responses, FashionTech's compilation and analysis of fit preferences by demographic group is proprietary. The fact that the underlying data comes from customers does not defeat FashionTech's ability to protect the *compilation and analysis*. Demographic preference data compiled by a business is valuable, not generally known, and protectable. See the compilation secrets discussion; a compilation of customer data has long been recognized as potentially protectable. The question is not whether individual customers consider their responses confidential, but whether the business has compiled and analyzed the data in ways that are not generally known and would be valuable to competitors. FashionTech likely can protect this.

**D. Incorrect.** While pricing information is subject to market forces, confidential pricing methodologies and cost allocations can be trade secrets. A company's internal pricing model—how it calculates markups, allocates costs, and sets prices for different customer segments—is proprietary information. Competitors would benefit from learning FashionTech's pricing strategy. Pricing information is protectable if it is not generally known and provides competitive advantage. See *PepsiCo* (recognizing that pricing strategies can be trade secrets).

***

## Question Q2.18 — Temporal Issue: When Does Information Become a Trade Secret?

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
CloudServices Inc. is developing a new data compression algorithm. The algorithm is in the research phase; the company has not yet completed testing, has not deployed it in any commercial product, and has not shared it with customers or partners. However, CloudServices has shared the algorithm with two internal research teams, required them to sign confidentiality agreements, and maintains the algorithm in a secured research repository. Before CloudServices completes development, a former researcher leaves and discloses the algorithm to a competitor. CloudServices sues for trade secret misappropriation. The competitor argues that because the algorithm is still under development, not yet deployed, and not yet providing competitive advantage in the marketplace, it cannot be a "trade secret" under the UTSA. Is the competitor's argument correct?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because trade secret protection applies only to information that is currently deployed and generating competitive advantage; information still in development is merely potential intellectual property, not protectable as a trade secret.

B. No, because the UTSA explicitly protects information that derives "independent economic value, actual or potential," and CloudServices has taken reasonable measures to maintain secrecy of the algorithm.

C. It depends on whether the algorithm, despite being in development, has achieved sufficient specificity and definition that competitors would gain tangible advantage from learning it, and whether CloudServices can show that the research and development effort would be substantially accelerated by access to the algorithm.

D. No, as a matter of law, because the UTSA protects information with "potential" economic value, and an algorithm being developed necessarily has potential economic value.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** The UTSA's definition explicitly includes information deriving "actual or potential" economic value. An algorithm still in development can have "potential" economic value if it is likely to provide competitive advantage once deployed. The fact that the advantage is not yet realized does not disqualify the information. Many trade secret claims involve information in development phases—early-stage research, prototype designs, etc. See the casebook and statutory language.

**B. Partially correct but too categorical.** While it is true that the UTSA includes "potential" economic value, this alone does not automatically grant trade secret status to all information in development. The question is whether the potential value is genuine and reasonably foreseeable. If CloudServices's algorithm is merely speculative or has not progressed beyond theoretical discussion, it may not qualify. However, if the algorithm has been developed to a point where it would have tangible, foreseeable value to competitors, the "potential" language likely applies. This option is correct in principle but fails to engage with the threshold question of whether the algorithm has progressed far enough to have genuine "potential" economic value.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the proper legal analysis. The inquiry is whether: (1) The algorithm has achieved sufficient specificity and technical definition that competitors would gain tangible advantage from access. (2) The algorithm is not merely conceptual or theoretical, but has progressed to a stage where its development or deployment is reasonably foreseeable. (3) CloudServices can demonstrate that the research effort required to independently develop equivalent algorithms would be substantial—if yes, then competitors would gain advantage by accessing the algorithm. If the algorithm is in early-stage research with uncertain value or utility, it may not qualify as a trade secret. But if CloudServices can show that the algorithm has been substantially developed, that its technical specifications are defined, and that competitors would save significant time and money by accessing it, then the algorithm likely has "potential" economic value sufficient to qualify as a trade secret. The casebook recognizes that "potential" economic value is sufficient; the question is whether that potential is real and substantial, not merely theoretical.

**D. Incorrect.** This option oversimplifies the "potential economic value" language. Not every algorithm in development has trade secret protection simply because it *might* someday be valuable. The information must have genuine potential for providing economic advantage; it cannot be purely speculative or undeveloped. The question is not whether the word "potential" appears in the statute, but whether the specific information has real, foreseeable potential economic value.

***

## Question Q2.19 — Distinguishing Confidential Information from Trade Secrets

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
RetailCorp Inc. maintains a confidential internal document titled "2025-2027 Strategic Business Plan" that includes market expansion goals, planned store locations, staffing increases, and capital expenditure estimates. The document is marked confidential, is not publicly available, and is distributed only to senior executives under confidentiality agreements. A competitor learns of the strategic plan through an employee leak and uses this information to open stores in locations RetailCorp had planned to enter. RetailCorp sues for misappropriation of a trade secret. The competitor argues that while the strategic plan may be confidential business information, it is not a "trade secret" under the UTSA because a strategic plan is not information that typically qualifies for trade secret protection—it is merely "business strategy," not a formula, process, technique, or tangible method. Is the competitor's argument correct?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the UTSA defines trade secrets as formulas, patterns, compilations, programs, devices, methods, techniques, or processes, and a strategic business plan does not fit these categories.

B. No, because a strategic business plan that is not generally known can constitute protectable information if it provides independent economic value and is subject to reasonable confidentiality measures.

C. It depends on whether the plan includes specific, concrete information (e.g., particular store locations, specific demographic analysis, targeted revenue numbers, detailed market entry strategies) that go beyond general strategic "ideas," and whether this concrete information has not been disclosed to the public or competitors.

D. No, strategic business plans are always protectable as trade secrets regardless of their specificity, because all strategic information is confidential by nature.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While it is true that the UTSA lists specific categories (formulas, patterns, compilations, programs, devices, methods, techniques, or processes), the statute explicitly states these are examples ("including"), not an exhaustive list. The definition of "trade secret" is broad and includes "information" generally. A strategic business plan could potentially fall within "compilation" (a compilation of strategic information and analysis) or simply within the broader category of "information." The question is not whether the plan fits a specific statutory category, but whether it qualifies as "information" that meets the UTSA's definition. See *Altavion* (interpreting "information" broadly to include design concepts and strategic ideas if sufficiently concrete and specific).

**B. Partially correct but incomplete.** While a strategic plan could potentially be protectable as a trade secret, this option fails to engage with the threshold question of specificity. Not all strategic information is protectable; very general or speculative strategic ideas may not qualify. The critical analysis requires examining the *specificity and concreteness* of the plan. See *Altavion* and the casebook discussion: abstract or broad strategic concepts may not be protectable, but specific, detailed strategic plans can be.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the proper legal analysis. A strategic business plan can be a protectable trade secret if it moves beyond abstract strategy and includes concrete, specific information. For example: (1) Particular store locations identified for expansion (concrete, not readily ascertainable, valuable to competitors). (2) Specific market analysis and demographic data supporting the locations (concrete, proprietary analysis). (3) Detailed financial projections and revenue targets for each planned location (concrete, valuable to competitors). (4) Specific timing and sequencing of store openings (concrete, strategically valuable). If RetailCorp's plan includes this level of specificity and concreteness, it is more likely to qualify as a trade secret. If the plan is merely general statements like "expand into the Northeast market" or "increase stores by 20%," it is less likely to qualify as protectable "information" because it is too abstract. The analysis mirrors *Altavion*: concrete, detailed plans and strategies can be protectable; abstract concepts are not. In this scenario, because the competitor learned of specific store locations and used that information, RetailCorp may be able to establish that the plan included concrete, proprietary information that qualifies as a trade secret.

**D. Incorrect.** Strategic business plans are not automatically protectable as trade secrets. The analysis requires examining whether the plan includes concrete, specific information that meets the UTSA definition. Some strategic plans are too abstract to qualify; others, with sufficient specificity and detail, can qualify. See the analysis in option C.

***

## Question Q2.20 — Multiple Misappropriators and "Knowledge" of Misappropriation

(MCQ)

### 1. Stem
PharmaInnovate Corp. maintains proprietary formulas for drug compounds as trade secrets. A researcher, Dr. David Park, working at PharmaInnovate obtains the formulas and sends them to an anonymous online forum frequented by pharmaceutical scientists. Dr. Park does not directly contact or identify a specific competitor. However, within days, a competitor—CompetitorRx Inc.—downloads the formulas from the online forum and immediately begins using them in its own drug development. CompetitorRx's CEO, who authorized the download, received an email tipping her off to the forum post. The CEO did not know the formulas came from a PharmaInnovate employee or that they were PharmaInnovate trade secrets. CompetitorRx took reasonable steps to verify that the information was in the public domain before using it. PharmaInnovate sues both Dr. Park and CompetitorRx for trade secret misappropriation. CompetitorRx argues it is not liable because it did not "know or have reason to know" that the formulas were trade secrets. Is CompetitorRx liable?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, CompetitorRx is liable because it downloaded and used information that it should have known was confidential, given the nature of pharmaceutical formulas and the competitive context.

B. No, CompetitorRx is not liable because it had no actual knowledge that the formulas were trade secrets, and its reasonable verification steps demonstrate good faith.

C. Yes, CompetitorRx is liable under the UTSA if Dr. Park used improper means (disclosure without authorization) and CompetitorRx either (1) knew or had reason to know the information was derived from improper means, or (2) had reason to know it was a trade secret based on the circumstances.

D. No, CompetitorRx is not liable because information published on a public online forum is no longer confidential, regardless of how it was placed there.

### 3. Correct Answer
C

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect in being too categorical.** While the nature of pharmaceutical formulas (inherently confidential in the industry) is relevant to what CompetitorRx "should have known," this option does not fully engage with the UTSA's statutory language. The question is whether CompetitorRx had "reason to know" the formulas were trade secrets—a fact-specific inquiry, not a categorical rule based on industry norms. CompetitorRx's good-faith verification efforts are relevant to whether it had "reason to know."

**B. Incorrect.** The UTSA uses a "reason to know" standard, not merely "actual knowledge." CompetitorRx could be liable if it should have suspected the formulas were trade secrets. Additionally, the key question for CompetitorRx's liability (if Dr. Park used improper means) is whether CompetitorRx knew or had reason to know the formulas were derived through improper means, not merely whether it had knowledge of their original trade secret status. The UTSA § 1(2)(2) provides liability for one who discloses or uses another's trade secret "at the time of disclosure or use, knew or had reason to know that his knowledge of the trade secret was derived from or through a person who had utilized improper means to acquire it." A tip-off about a forum posting, combined with the nature of pharmaceutical formulas, might provide CompetitorRx with "reason to know" that something was amiss.

**C. Correct.** This reflects the statutory language of the UTSA and case law on downstream misappropriation. Under UTSA § 1(2), a person who discloses or uses another's trade secret without consent is liable if, at the time of use: (1) "knew or had reason to know that his knowledge of the trade secret was derived from or through a person who had utilized improper means to acquire it" [alternative ground for liability]; or (2) "knew or had reason to know that it was a trade secret and that knowledge of it had been acquired by accident or mistake." CompetitorRx's liability depends on: (1) Whether CompetitorRx had reason to know that Dr. Park's disclosure to the forum was improper (i.e., that the information was obtained or disclosed in breach of trust or through misappropriation). A pharmaceutical researcher posting detailed formulas to an anonymous forum is suspicious, and CompetitorRx might be charged with "reason to know" that something improper occurred. (2) Whether CompetitorRx knew or should have known the formulas were trade secrets. The nature of pharmaceutical formulas and the context suggest CompetitorRx should have inquired further. The UTSA does hold downstream recipients liable if they know or have reason to know of the improper acquisition. See UTSA § 1(2)(2)(B) and (C). In this scenario, CompetitorRx's liability turns on whether it had "reason to know"—a fact-specific inquiry considering: Was the posting anonymous? Was the source suspicious? Did CompetitorRx make reasonable inquiries? Should pharmaceutical formulas be treated with skepticism when found on a public forum? These factors would determine CompetitorRx's liability.

**D. Incorrect.** The mere fact that information was published on a public forum does not automatically destroy trade secret status or eliminate liability for downstream recipients. If the publication was improper (unauthorized disclosure by someone under a duty to maintain confidentiality, like Dr. Park), the information can still be a trade secret, and those who receive it with knowledge or reason to know of the improper acquisition can be liable. See the UTSA's recognition of "misappropriation" occurring through improper means, including breach of trust. The question is not whether the information reached a public forum, but how it got there and what CompetitorRx knew or should have known about its origins.



# CHAPTER 3 – MISAPPROPRIATION OF TRADE SECRETS

***

## Question Q3.1 — Improper Means and Commercial Morality

### 1. Stem
TechCorp hired an investigator to photograph Rivalsys's factory from public roads during construction of a new manufacturing facility. The photographs revealed structural features of Rivalsys's proprietary semiconductor fabrication process. The investigator did not trespass, did not violate aviation regulations, and took photos from publicly accessible areas. Rivalsys has brought a trade secret misappropriation claim. Rivalsys argues that the photographs constituted improper means under the Restatement of Torts § 757(a) and state trade secret law. TechCorp defends on the ground that no trespass occurred and that all observations were from public space.

### 2. Options
A. TechCorp's defense likely succeeds because photography from public space falls outside the statutory definition of "improper means" regardless of the investigator's intent.
B. TechCorp's conduct may constitute improper means if it circumvented Rivalsys's reasonable security precautions in a manner that violates commercial norms and reasonable expectations of privacy during construction.
C. The mere fact that TechCorp gathered information about a rival's process means TechCorp necessarily committed improper acquisition unless it independently reverse-engineered the final product.
D. Rivalsys forfeited protection because construction-phase visibility of the facility indicates the trade secret holder failed to take adequate precautions.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While the statute defines improper means by example (theft, bribery, misrepresentation, espionage), courts apply a broader principle: improper means are those that fall below generally accepted standards of commercial morality and reasonable conduct. The Fifth Circuit in *E.I. du Pont de Nemours v. Christopher* explicitly held that aerial photography of a plant under construction, though technically lawful in terms of trespass, can be improper means if it circumvents the owner's reasonable security efforts and violates commercial norms. The absence of physical intrusion does not shield conduct that subverts commercial ethics.

**B. Correct.** Under *Christopher*, the touchstone is whether the means fall below reasonable commercial standards, not whether they involve technical illegality or trespass. The court emphasized that trade secret holders need only guard against intrusions that violate reasonable commercial norms, not all possible intrusions. Here, photographing an operating construction site where manufacturing details are visible goes beyond mere product observation or reverse engineering and reflects deliberate circumvention of interim secrecy measures. The investigator's knowledge of why the photos were sought and the deliberateness of the surveillance matter.

**C. Incorrect.** Trade secret law does not prohibit all knowledge of a rival's process. It prohibits improper acquisition, use, or disclosure. Independent reverse engineering of a finished product remains lawful. Here, however, the facts suggest deliberate surveillance during construction, not legitimate analysis of a completed, commercially available product.

**D. Incorrect.** *Christopher* itself rejected this argument. The court recognized that requiring an impenetrable fortress during construction would be unreasonable. The standard is not whether information was visible but whether the owner took reasonable precautions appropriate to the circumstances. A partially open construction site with interim security measures demonstrates reasonable effort under the circumstances.

***

## Question Q3.2 — Distinction Between Improper Means and Lawful Access

### 1. Stem
An engineer employed by PatentCo legitimately attended a trade show where ProducerCorp displayed a prototype of a new electrical connector. The display was open to the industry and competitors. The engineer examined the prototype closely, took photographs (permitted under the trade show rules), assembled notes to understand component relationships, and later, back at PatentCo, used that analysis to develop a competing product with similar structural features but independent refinements. ProducerCorp now claims trade secret misappropriation based on the engineer's acquisition of information at the trade show.

### 2. Options
A. The engineer's conduct clearly constitutes improper acquisition because she analyzed a prototype belonging to a competitor without ProducerCorp's explicit permission for that level of detail.
B. The engineer's conduct is likely improper because attendees at trade shows owe an implicit duty of confidence to all exhibitors regarding displayed products.
C. The engineer's conduct likely falls within proper means (reverse engineering of a lawfully obtained product) unless ProducerCorp can show it had contractual restrictions, had clearly marked the prototype as confidential with access limitations, or had other reasonable precautions against detailed analysis.
D. The engineer may be liable if PatentCo later uses the information to market a directly competing product, even if the development process itself was independent.

### 3. Correct Answer
**C**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Reverse engineering of a lawfully obtained product—including examining a publicly displayed prototype—is expressly recognized as proper means under trade secret law (*Bonito Boats, Inc. v. Thunder Craft Boats*). The engineer need not obtain explicit permission to examine a product at a public event. The key question is not whether permission was requested but whether the product was lawfully obtained and whether the reverse engineering occurred without breach of confidence or circumvention of access controls.

**B. Incorrect.** No automatic duty of confidence arises merely from attendance at a trade show. An implied confidential relationship arises only from the context and nature of the disclosure. Voluntary public display of a product creates a lesser expectation of confidentiality than, for example, disclosure to a prospective licensee under an evaluation agreement.
