Trade shows are expressly designed for industry observation and competitive intelligence gathering.

**C. Correct.** Lawful reverse engineering of a purchased or publicly displayed product is a fundamental exception to trade secret protection. If ProducerCorp wished to prevent detailed analysis, it could have (1) restricted access to authorized personnel only, (2) required written evaluation agreements with confidentiality provisions, (3) used contractual limitations such as shrink-wrap licenses, or (4) employed physical security (e.g., potting compounds in electronics). The mere display at a trade show, without additional protective measures, indicates acceptance of industry inspection. The engineer's subsequent use of the analysis is lawful absent a breach of a confidentiality duty.

**D. Incorrect.** The lawfulness of the reverse engineering process does not change based on how PatentCo later markets the competing product. Once properly reverse-engineered information enters the public domain of the competitor's knowledge, that competitor may use it commercially. ProducerCorp's remedy lies in patent protection, not trade secret law.

***

## Question Q3.3 — Implied Confidential Relationship in Evaluation Context

### 1. Stem
DesignStudio submitted a digital authentication concept to TechGiant Corporation for evaluation in connection with a possible joint venture. DesignStudio and TechGiant did not sign a formal nondisclosure agreement (NDA), but the submission was marked "confidential" and DesignStudio's email covering letter stated: "We are submitting this for your evaluation as part of our business discussion. We expect you to treat this information as confidential." TechGiant reviewed the concept, declined the joint venture, and three months later filed a patent application on core elements of DesignStudio's concept. DesignStudio now sues for trade secret misappropriation. TechGiant argues that without a written NDA, no confidential relationship existed.

### 2. Options
A. TechGiant's argument succeeds because trade secret law, by statute, requires a written confidentiality agreement to establish a legally binding duty.
B. An implied confidential relationship may arise from the context and nature of the submission, even without a formal written NDA, if the circumstances indicate the parties intended the information to be received in confidence.
C. Because TechGiant did not explicitly agree in writing to keep the information confidential, it owed no duty, and DesignStudio's claim must fail regardless of the email or the "confidential" marking.
D. The confidential marking on the submission document is insufficient by itself to create a duty; DesignStudio must prove TechGiant's subjective intent to accept the information in confidence.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Neither the UTSA nor state common law requires a written NDA to establish a confidential relationship. Both statutes and case law recognize that a confidential relationship may arise "by operation of law from the affiliations of the parties and the context in which the disclosures are offered" (*Burten v. Milton Bradley*). When disclosure is made to a prospective business partner (licensee, joint venturer, or purchaser) specifically to enable evaluation, the circumstances ordinarily give rise to an implied duty of confidence.

**B. Correct.** Under *Burten v. Milton Bradley*, an implied confidential relationship arises where "facts demonstrate that a disclosure was made in order to promote a specific relationship, e.g., disclosure to a prospective purchaser to enable him to appraise the value of the secret." Here, DesignStudio's email explicitly communicated an expectation of confidence, the submission was marked confidential, and the disclosure was made in the context of a specific business opportunity (joint venture evaluation). Courts infer the parties' intent from these objective circumstances. The absence of a formal NDA does not defeat the implication when the surrounding facts show the parties understood the information to be confidential.

**C. Incorrect.** Courts do not require explicit written agreement to recognize a duty of confidence in evaluation contexts. The "Disclosure Record" form in *Burten*, which was signed and contained general language about not establishing a relationship, was found ambiguous and insufficient to waive tort liability for misappropriation. Here, TechGiant's conduct is stronger evidence of implied acceptance of a confidence duty: it received information marked confidential, it was told by the disclosing party that confidence was expected, and it accepted the information for evaluation purposes.

**D. Incorrect.** While subjective intent matters, courts infer intent from objective circumstances and the nature of the relationship and disclosure. DesignStudio need not prove what TechGiant subjectively thought; it must show that a reasonable party in TechGiant's position, receiving marked-confidential information in an evaluation context with explicit statements of expectation, would understand a confidence duty. Here, those circumstances are satisfied.

***

## Question Q3.4 — Disclaiming Confidential Relationship Through Ambiguous Language

### 1. Stem
Indie Inventors submitted a novel toy concept to MegaToys Corp. with a disclosure form. The form stated: "Submission is voluntary and no relationship is implied from our review. We assume no obligation to consider or license the idea. Inventor retains all rights under patent law." The form also contained a clause authorizing MegaToys to "make copies for company records." Indie Inventors marked its submission "confidential," but MegaToys's form did not explicitly state "we will not treat this as confidential" or contain language such as "this submission is not received in confidence." One year later, Indie Inventors discovered that MegaToys had launched a product with design elements similar to the submitted concept. Indie Inventors seeks damages for misappropriation of trade secrets.

### 2. Options
A. The disclaimer language in MegaToys's form conclusively waives any implied confidential relationship as a matter of law, and Indie Inventors cannot recover.
B. Despite the general disclaimer language, Indie Inventors may still establish an implied confidential relationship if a jury finds the language was ambiguous, if other provisions (like the reproduction clause) suggest MegaToys understood it would handle the submission carefully, or if the context indicates confidence was expected.
C. The fact that Indie Inventors marked the submission "confidential" creates an explicit contractual waiver that overrides any implied confidential relationship that might otherwise arise.
D. MegaToys waived all obligations to Indie Inventors by signing the disclosure form, but Indie Inventors can sue for patent infringement because the form explicitly preserved patent rights.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** This answer reflects the position rejected in *Burten v. Milton Bradley*. The court held that ambiguous or general disclaimer language—language that disclaims a general "relationship" without explicitly addressing confidentiality or tort liability—does not, as a matter of law, waive the tort claim for trade secret misappropriation. The court drew a critical distinction between explicit waivers (e.g., "this submission is not received in confidence") and general disclaimers about the existence of a business relationship. Here, MegaToys's form does not explicitly address whether a confidential relationship exists; it addresses only whether a contractual relationship or obligation to review exists.

**B. Correct.** Under *Burten*, the ambiguity of the language is critical. The court found that a jury could reasonably interpret "no relationship" to mean "no contractual relationship during the review process" rather than "no duty of confidence regarding tortious misuse." Additionally, the presence of the "reproduction for company records" clause, which limiting MegaToys's right to copy only to record-keeping purposes, could suggest an implied promise of careful handling and confidentiality in use. Finally, the context—disclosure for evaluation of a specific business opportunity—ordinarily raises an expectation of confidence. A jury question therefore exists.

**C. Incorrect.** Indie Inventors's marking does not create a contractual waiver; rather, it evidences Indie Inventors's intent that the information be treated as confidential. The question is whether the parties' conduct and agreement as a whole show they intended to disclaim the duty. MegaToys's marking does not contradict Indie Inventors's marking; it must be interpreted in the broader context of the disclosure agreement, and ambiguous language will not override the presumption that disclosure in an evaluation context carries an implied duty.

**D. Incorrect.** While the form may preserve patent law rights, that preservation does not negate the trade secret claim. Trade secret and patent law coexist (*Kewanee Oil v. Bicron*). The form's language regarding patent rights is legally separate from whether a confidential relationship exists and whether tort liability for misappropriation can attach.

***

## Question Q3.5 — Third-Party Liability Under "Reason to Know" Standard

### 1. Stem
FormerManager worked for SupplierCo and left to join CompetitorCorp. On her first day at CompetitorCorp, FormerManager was hired into a business development role with significant decision-making authority over pricing, product strategy, and supplier partnerships. A week into employment, the CEO of CompetitorCorp asked FormerManager a general question about industry pricing trends. CompetitorCorp did not explicitly ask FormerManager to disclose SupplierCo's confidential pricing information, and CompetitorCorp's employee handbook contains a policy against using information from competitors in breach of duty. However, CompetitorCorp never asked FormerManager to detail her prior employment, never inquired whether she had confidentiality obligations, and did not restrict her access to sensitive decision-making meetings. Two months later, CompetitorCorp underbid SupplierCo on a major contract using pricing strategy information that matches SupplierCo's confidential pricing architecture. SupplierCo alleges that CompetitorCorp knew or had reason to know that FormerManager would rely on confidential information in her new role.

### 2. Options
A. CompetitorCorp is not liable because it never explicitly solicited the confidential information and its handbook policy demonstrates it did not knowingly encourage misappropriation.
B. CompetitorCorp may be liable if, under the circumstances (FormerManager's senior role, the context of her hiring, CompetitorCorp's knowledge of her background, and the nature of her decision-making authority), a reasonable company should have foreseen that she would rely on prior confidential knowledge, even absent explicit discussion.
C. CompetitorCorp's liability depends entirely on whether FormerManager herself acted with knowledge that she was violating her duty to SupplierCo; if she did not consciously remember specific confidential information, she cannot have breached her duty.
D. CompetitorCorp has reason to know of misappropriation only if SupplierCo can prove that CompetitorCorp specifically instructed FormerManager to exploit her prior knowledge.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Under *PepsiCo, Inc. v. Redmond*, liability for knowing receipt does not require explicit solicitation or request. The UTSA § 1(2) imposes liability on a person who knew or *had reason to know* that knowledge was derived from improper means. The "reason to know" standard is objective: would a reasonable company in CompetitorCorp's position, knowing that it hired a senior-level manager directly from a competitor into a high-authority role, foresee that confidential knowledge would inevitably influence decision-making? The handbook policy, while evidence of intent to comply, does not insulate CompetitorCorp if it recklessly hired into a position where misuse was foreseeable.

**B. Correct.** *PepsiCo* established that a recipient company may be liable for "inevitable disclosure" if (1) the employee possesses intimate knowledge of the prior employer's trade secrets, (2) the new position provides access to decision-making in competitive areas, and (3) the threat of misuse is substantial and foreseeable. Here, CompetitorCorp hired FormerManager into business development—a role that inherently influences pricing strategy—without obtaining assurances, investigation, or confidentiality agreements regarding her prior employment. CompetitorCorp should have (or did) have reason to know that pricing decisions in her domain would likely be informed by knowledge of SupplierCo's confidential pricing, given the inevitability of relying on ingrained professional experience. The failure to segregate her from sensitive decisions or to seek legal guidance constitutes constructive knowledge of the misappropriation risk.

**C. Incorrect.** *Inevitable disclosure* liability does not depend on the employee's conscious recollection of specific secrets or conscious breach. The theory recognizes that once confidential knowledge is acquired, it becomes part of the employee's professional toolkit and will inevitably influence future decisions, even unconsciously. CompetitorCorp's liability is based on its reason to know that this psychological reality would lead to misuse, not on FormerManager's subjective state of mind.

**D. Incorrect.** The statute does not require specific instruction or solicitation. Willful blindness—hiring a high-level manager from a competitor into a pricing or strategy role without inquiry or safeguards—satisfies the "reason to know" standard. CompetitorCorp's failure to investigate, to restrict access, or to seek legal guidance is probative of constructive knowledge.

***

## Question Q3.6 — Use vs. Disclosure in Breach of Confidentiality Relationship

### 1. Stem
DataCorp disclosed its proprietary customer database (including client identity, purchase history, and pricing paid) to IntegrationVendor under a Master Service Agreement (MSA). The MSA stated: "Vendor agrees to maintain confidentiality of all Customer Data and to use it solely for the purpose of integrating it with Vendor's reporting systems for DataCorp's benefit. Vendor shall not disclose Customer Data to any third party without written permission." IntegrationVendor did not disclose the database publicly or to competitors. However, IntegrationVendor used the customer data internally to inform its own sales strategy, targeted its own product offerings to DataCorp's high-value clients based on their purchase history, and modified its service pricing to undercut DataCorp's margins on similar clients. DataCorp discovered this use and sued IntegrationVendor for trade secret misappropriation. IntegrationVendor argues that since there was no disclosure to a third party, no misappropriation occurred.

### 2. Options
A. IntegrationVendor is correct because the UTSA defines misappropriation as disclosure or use without consent, and IntegrationVendor's internal use for its own purposes is not actionable unless the information was first disclosed.
B. IntegrationVendor's argument conflates disclosure and use; improper use of a lawfully acquired trade secret can constitute misappropriation independently of whether the secret was further disseminated.
C. Misappropriation requires proof of both use and disclosure; if there is use but no disclosure to others, the trade secret holder can seek only equitable relief (injunction), not damages.
D. IntegrationVendor's internal use of Customer Data is outside the scope of trade secret law because the company did not sell or commercially exploit the data as a standalone product.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** This answer fundamentally misreads the statute. The UTSA § 1(2)(b) provides liability for "[d]isclosure or use of a trade secret of another without express or implied consent." The "or" is disjunctive: either disclosure without consent *or* use without consent can constitute misappropriation. They are independent violations. The statute does not require disclosure for use-based claims. *3M Co. v. Pribyl* confirms that an employee may be liable for misappropriation by using confidential processes acquired under a duty of confidence, even if the employee did not disclose the information to others.

**B. Correct.** Under *3M v. Pribyl*, an employee left a manufacturer and joined a competitor, taking knowledge of confidential manufacturing processes. The court held the employee liable for misappropriation based on internal use of those processes in the new company's operations, even though the employee did not disclose the specific process details to others or to the public. The violation lay in applying the confidential knowledge to derive commercial advantage beyond the scope of authorized use. Similarly, here, IntegrationVendor's authorized use was limited to "integration" and "reporting" for DataCorp's benefit. Using the data to target DataCorp's clients and undercut its pricing strategy falls outside that scope and constitutes improper use.

**C. Incorrect.** Misappropriation through improper use does not require disclosure, and damages are fully available. The trade secret holder need not prove any loss of the secret's public status; it need only prove that the defendant derived value through unauthorized use in breach of a duty. Damages can be measured by lost profits, unjust enrichment, or a reasonable royalty, all available regardless of disclosure.

**D. Incorrect.** Trade secret protection is not limited to information sold as a standalone product. The law protects information used internally by an organization to generate competitive advantage. DataCorp's customer database is protected as a trade secret by virtue of its confidentiality and economic value, and the defendant's unauthorized internal use in breach of the MSA scope constitutes actionable misappropriation.

***

## Question Q3.7 — Accidental Disclosure and Continuing Duty

### 1. Stem
ArchitectureFirm's project manager accidentally sent an email containing detailed specifications for a new commercial building design (including cost estimates, structural innovations, and client pricing) to the wrong address: they sent it to BuildingContractor's email instead of to a colleague at ArchitectureFirm. BuildingContractor received the email, realized it was confidential, but did not immediately return or delete it. Instead, BuildingContractor reviewed the specifications and retained them in its computer for two weeks before ArchitectureFirm discovered the error and demanded return of all copies. BuildingContractor had not yet used the information or shared it with competitors. ArchitectureFirm asserts a trade secret misappropriation claim based on BuildingContractor's knowing retention of accidentally disclosed information. BuildingContractor argues that since it did not affirmatively acquire the information through improper means (it was simply received in error), no misappropriation can have occurred.

### 2. Options
A. BuildingContractor is liable for misappropriation because it received the information via misdirected email, which is functionally equivalent to theft.
B. BuildingContractor may be liable under the UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) if, at the time it received the email, it knew or had reason to know the information was a trade secret that had been acquired by accident or mistake, and BuildingContractor failed to promptly delete or return it before a material change of position.
C. BuildingContractor cannot be liable because the statutory language specifically protects accidental disclosures and permits the recipient to retain accidentally received information indefinitely without liability.
D. BuildingContractor's liability depends solely on whether it later used or disclosed the information; mere retention after accidental receipt does not, standing alone, constitute misappropriation.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** The statute does not treat accidental receipt as equivalent to theft. Rather, UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) creates a distinct category: liability arises when a person acquires a trade secret by accident or mistake and, before a material change of position, knew or should have known it was a trade secret and that knowledge was improperly acquired. This provision recognizes that innocent recipients of misdirected information have limited duties. However, those duties do not extend indefinitely or permit indefinite retention.

**B. Correct.** UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) specifically addresses accidental disclosure. It imposes liability on a person who (1) knew or had reason to know the information was a trade secret, (2) knew or had reason to know it was acquired by accident or mistake, and (3) before a material change of position, failed to take reasonable steps to avoid benefiting from the information. Here, BuildingContractor received a misdirected email clearly marked confidential, containing detailed project specifications. A reasonable party would know the information was valuable and confidential and that the email was sent in error. The two-week retention without deletion, return, or segregation from systems, before any use occurs, suggests BuildingContractor did not promptly disassociate itself from the misdirected information. BuildingContractor's failure to act within a reasonable time to delete or return the information before a material change of position (such as incorporation of the information into decision-making or memory) could constitute misappropriation under this subsection.

**C. Incorrect.** The statute does not permit indefinite retention. Quite the opposite: § 1(2)(b)(3) creates liability precisely where the recipient fails to promptly destroy or return accidentally acquired trade secrets. The provision is a limitation on liability, not a license to retain.

**D. Incorrect.** Use or disclosure is not required for liability under § 1(2)(b)(3). The statute imposes liability for the knowing retention and failure to promptly disassociate from accidentally acquired trade secrets, independent of whether they are ever used or disclosed. The rationale is that even retention can constitute a form of benefiting from the secret and failure to act on notice that the disclosure was mistaken.

***

## Question Q3.8 — Improper Acquisition Through Inducement to Breach Duty

### 1. Stem
ConsultantX worked for ResearchCorp under a Non-Disclosure Agreement (NDA) and had access to ResearchCorp's confidential formulation for a novel pharmaceutical compound. ConsultantX's employment was scheduled to end in six months. PharmaCo, a competitor, contacted ConsultantX and offered a substantial consulting fee ($500,000) contingent on ConsultantX providing detailed documentation of the formulation and the research methodology within 30 days of leaving ResearchCorp. ConsultantX accepted the offer and, two weeks before her departure, copied the formulation documents, encrypted them, and transferred them to an external hard drive. On her last day, she delivered the hard drive to PharmaCo. PharmaCo knew that ConsultantX was bound by an NDA and knew that the information was confidential; PharmaCo's recruiting officer explicitly told the consulting firm recruiter that PharmaCo was "interested in obtaining key proprietary information from ResearchCorp." ResearchCorp has sued both ConsultantX and PharmaCo for trade secret misappropriation.

### 2. Options
A. PharmaCo is not liable for misappropriation because ConsultantX, not PharmaCo, physically acquired and transferred the documents, and PharmaCo merely received the information.
B. PharmaCo may be liable for improper acquisition if it can be shown that PharmaCo induced ConsultantX to breach her NDA and that PharmaCo knew or had reason to know the information was a trade secret acquired through breach of a confidentiality duty.
C. PharmaCo's explicit knowledge that ConsultantX was bound by an NDA and its offer of substantial compensation specifically contingent on obtaining the formulation absolves PharmaCo of liability because the inducement was transparent and not fraudulent.
D. PharmaCo's liability depends entirely on whether the formulation could have been independently developed or reverse-engineered; if the compound could theoretically have been discovered through legitimate R&D, PharmaCo did not use improper means.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** The statute does not limit liability to the person who physically takes the secret. UTSA § 1(2)(b)(2) imposes liability on a person who discloses or uses a trade secret "without express or implied consent" if that person knew or had reason to know the knowledge "was derived from or through a person who had utilized improper means to acquire it" or from a person who owed a duty to maintain confidentiality. The statute specifically contemplates liability for downstream recipients. More broadly, common law trade secret doctrine recognizes that a party who induces another to breach a confidentiality duty is itself liable for improper acquisition or inducement of breach. PharmaCo's offering of inducement is itself improper conduct, not merely passive receipt.

**B. Correct.** PharmaCo engaged in improper acquisition by inducement. The UTSA defines improper means to include "inducement of a breach of a duty to maintain secrecy" (§ 1(1)). Here, PharmaCo (1) knew ConsultantX was bound by an NDA (stated objective), (2) offered substantial, targeted compensation contingent on obtaining the confidential information, and (3) explicitly instructed the recruiter that PharmaCo wanted "key proprietary information." This is textbook inducement to breach. PharmaCo's knowledge of the NDA, combined with its targeted offer and the explicit link between compensation and disclosure, demonstrates both improper means and knowledge that the acquisition would violate a duty. PharmaCo is liable as a co-actor in the misappropriation scheme.

**C. Incorrect.** Transparency does not cure improper means. Indeed, PharmaCo's explicit acknowledgment that it seeks information from ResearchCorp and its knowledge of the NDA strengthen the case for improper acquisition through inducement. The Restatement of Torts examples include bribery and deliberate inducement to breach confidence. PharmaCo's offer, contingent on the breach, is a form of bribery for the breach of duty. Improper means cannot be laundered by transparency; if anything, transparency demonstrates consciousness of wrongdoing.

**D. Incorrect.** The independent development defense applies to parties who reverse-engineer or conduct their own R&D. It does not apply to parties who induce breach of confidence to obtain a shortcut. PharmaCo did not invest in independent research; it induced ConsultantX to steal. The availability of an alternative path to knowledge does not validate improper means used to acquire a shortcut.

***

## Question Q3.9 — Confidential Information Within Employer-Employee Relationship

### 1. Stem
SeniorDev worked as a software architect for TechFirm for eight years, with primary responsibility for designing the internal architecture of TechFirm's proprietary data analytics platform. The platform's architectural design, while not formally documented as a "trade secret," was not publicly disclosed and was known only to senior engineering staff at TechFirm. SeniorDev never signed a non-compete, non-solicitation, or NDA. However, TechFirm's employee handbook stated: "Employees may not disclose or use confidential information about our products or technologies without authorization." Three months after leaving TechFirm, SeniorDev joined CompetitorSoft and was tasked with designing a new data analytics product. SeniorDev applied the same architectural principles and system design patterns to CompetitorSoft's platform, resulting in a product with similar structural organization and efficiency. SeniorDev did not copy any documents, did not have access to TechFirm's code, and used only her own knowledge and skill. TechFirm alleges misappropriation of trade secrets.

### 2. Options
A. SeniorDev is not liable because she did not copy any documents, did not breach any signed agreement, and applied only general knowledge and skill.
B. SeniorDev is liable because she was employed by TechFirm, learned the architecture on the job, and therefore owed a perpetual duty not to compete using that knowledge.
C. SeniorDev may be liable if TechFirm can prove that the specific architectural design was a protectable trade secret (not just a general principle) and that SeniorDev's use at CompetitorSoft violated an implied duty of confidence arising from the employment relationship and the handbook policy.
D. SeniorDev cannot be liable because architectural principles and design patterns, once learned, become part of the employee's general skill set and cannot be protected by trade secret law.

### 3. Correct Answer
**C**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** This answer oversimplifies. While SeniorDev did not copy documents and signed no agreement, trade secret law protects against misappropriation through use of knowledge acquired under a duty of confidence, even without physical copying or a signed agreement. *3M v. Pribyl* held an employee liable for misappropriation by applying confidential manufacturing processes learned on the job, even absent any document copying. The absence of a written agreement does not negate an implied duty arising from the employment context and the employer's handbook policy.

**B. Incorrect.** Trade secret law does not create a perpetual non-compete for employees. *PepsiCo v. Redmond* explicitly cautioned that "trade secret law does not provide a reserve clause for solicitous employers." Employees are free to move to competitors and to use general knowledge, skill, and experience. TechFirm's claim is not that SeniorDev cannot work in the industry but that she cannot exploit specific, protectable trade secrets—a narrower claim that requires proof that the architecture is a trade secret, not just useful knowledge.

**C. Correct.** Under *Burten v. Milton Bradley*, an implied confidential relationship arises in the employment context. The combination of (1) the employment relationship, (2) SeniorDev's access to confidential technical information as part of her role, (3) TechFirm's handbook policy indicating that confidential information is not to be disclosed or used without authorization, and (4) the fact that the architectural design was unknown outside the company creates a reasonable basis for inferring a duty of confidence. However, TechFirm must also establish that the specific architectural design (not merely general principles) qualifies as a trade secret: it derives independent economic value from secrecy, is not readily ascertainable by proper means, and is subject to reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy. If TechFirm can establish both the trade secret status and SeniorDev's use of that specific information (as opposed to generalized skill), liability may follow.

**D. Incorrect.** While general knowledge and skill are not protected, specific trade secrets—including proprietary system designs—can be protected if they are not readily ascertainable and are subject to reasonable protection efforts. The question is not whether knowledge was acquired through employment (which is expected) but whether the knowledge relates to a specific, protectable trade secret and whether it was used beyond the scope of authorized employment in breach of a duty of confidence.

***

## Question Q3.10 — Reverse Engineering as Proper Means vs. Misappropriation

### 1. Stem
FastMotors purchased a limited-edition high-performance engine from LuxuryCars, a competitor. FastMotors' engineers spent six weeks carefully disassembling the engine, measuring components, analyzing materials, and documenting the manufacturing process. They did not access any internal LuxuryCars documents, did not bribe any LuxuryCars employee, and did not violate any contractual restriction (the engine was sold without a license agreement or reverse-engineering clause). Based on their analysis, FastMotors' engineers developed a process to manufacture a similar high-performance engine with comparable specifications. LuxuryCars sued for trade secret misappropriation, arguing that the engine's structural design and optimization principles are trade secrets developed through years of R&D investment.

### 2. Options
A. FastMotors is clearly liable because it deliberately copied LuxuryCars' design and derived the benefit of LuxuryCars' R&D investment.
B. FastMotors' conduct is protected as proper means (reverse engineering of a lawfully obtained product) unless LuxuryCars can show that the engine was licensed or sold with contractual restrictions against reverse engineering, or that physical security measures (potting, welding) prevented access.
C. FastMotors is liable if its reverse-engineered product performs nearly identically to LuxuryCars' engine, which suggests copying rather than independent development.
D. FastMotors' reverse engineering is improper because the engine components embody LuxuryCars' protectable trade secrets, and LuxuryCars can prevent analysis of secrets embedded in sold products by relying on trade secret law alone.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Trade secret law does not prohibit copying of lawfully obtained products. The entire point of reverse engineering as a proper means is that a party may study, analyze, and replicate a product once it has been lawfully purchased or acquired. The fact that LuxuryCars invested time and money in R&D does not give it a trade secret monopoly over a design that is embedded in a sold product and can be analyzed through lawful means. This is precisely why trade secret holders may choose patents (which block reverse engineering) rather than secrecy.

**B. Correct.** Under *Bonito Boats, Inc. v. Thunder Craft Boats*, reverse engineering of a lawfully obtained product is a recognized proper means of acquiring knowledge and is not misappropriation, absent contractual or physical restrictions. Here, FastMotors (1) lawfully purchased the product, (2) conducted physical analysis without breaching any contract, (3) did not solicit confidential information or breach a duty, and (4) invested its own labor in the analysis. LuxuryCars' remedy, if it wished to protect the engine design beyond 20 years, was to enforce a restrictive sale agreement (e.g., "for use only, not reverse engineering"), to use physical security (e.g., potting or welding), or to apply for patent protection. The mere embedding of a trade secret in a sold product does not extend trade secret protection when the product is openly sold without restrictions.

**C. Incorrect.** Performance similarity is not evidence of copying in the trade secret context (though it might be in patent cases). Once reverse-engineered information is lawfully derived from product analysis, the defendant may use it commercially, and the resulting product may be identical or nearly identical. LuxuryCars' recourse is not to show similarity but to prove that FastMotors breached a contractual or fiduciary duty, engaged in deception, or relied on improperly acquired information—none of which are alleged here.

**D. Incorrect.** Trade secret law cannot prevent analysis of products in the public domain (i.e., sold products). *Bonito Boats* and the Restatement principles make clear that an owner cannot hide behind trade secret law to prevent reverse engineering of a product it has willingly sold in the open market. LuxuryCars' choice to sell the engine without restrictive covenants or patents indicates acceptance of the risk of reverse engineering.

***

## Question Q3.11 — "Reason to Know" in Hiring a Competitor's Executive

### 1. Stem
MediaCorp's Chief Marketing Officer (CMO) left to join BroadcastCo, a direct competitor, as Vice President of Strategic Partnerships. At MediaCorp, the CMO had exclusive access to MediaCorp's annual strategic plan, which included confidential revenue projections, planned product launches, advertising budgets, and partnership targets for the coming three years. The CMO had signed a comprehensive confidentiality agreement. BroadcastCo's CEO personally recruited the CMO and was aware of her role at MediaCorp. At no point did BroadcastCo inquire whether the CMO had confidentiality obligations, restrict her access to strategic planning meetings, or obtain her written assurance that she would not use MediaCorp information. Within four months of the CMO's hire, BroadcastCo launched a partnership strategy that directly anticipated MediaCorp's planned partnerships and undercut MediaCorp's revenue projections in those areas.

### 2. Options
A. BroadcastCo has no liability because it did not explicitly ask the CMO to disclose MediaCorp's information and did not instruct her to violate her NDA.
B. BroadcastCo may have reason to know of threatened misappropriation if the CMO's senior position, her known access to high-level strategic information, and BroadcastCo's failure to segregate her from strategic planning meetings or obtain confidentiality assurances create a high probability that she would rely on MediaCorp's plans.
C. BroadcastCo is liable only if the CMO herself admits that she consciously relied on MediaCorp's strategic plan in her new role.
D. BroadcastCo has reason to know only if it hired multiple executives from MediaCorp in quick succession or if it can be shown that the CEO specifically instructed the CMO to breach her duty.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** *PepsiCo v. Redmond* established that a recipient company can be liable for "inevitable disclosure" (threatened misappropriation) even absent explicit request or instruction. The liability arises from the company's "reason to know" that the employee will inevitably rely on trade secrets in the new role. Here, BroadcastCo's CEO recruited the CMO specifically because of her role at MediaCorp, and BroadcastCo did nothing to prevent misuse (no inquiry, no segregation, no written assurance). This constructive knowledge satisfies the "reason to know" standard.

**B. Correct.** *PepsiCo* held that a company has reason to know of inevitable disclosure when (1) the employee possesses intimate knowledge of the former employer's trade secrets (here, the strategic plan is precisely that), (2) the new position is directly competitive and provides input into areas where the secrets would be useful (here, BroadcastCo's Strategic Partnerships role directly overlaps with MediaCorp's strategic initiatives), and (3) the company fails to take reasonable precautions such as investigative inquiry, segregation, or written assurance that confidential information will not be used. BroadcastCo's failure to inquire about the NDA or restrictions, despite personally recruiting the CMO into a role where her MediaCorp knowledge would be directly applicable, demonstrates reason to know of the threat. The fact that BroadcastCo's subsequent strategy anticipated MediaCorp's plans may further support the inference that the CMO's prior knowledge was inevitable influence.

**C. Incorrect.** The CMO's conscious recollection or admission is not necessary to establish misappropriation. *PepsiCo* recognizes that confidential knowledge becomes part of an employee's professional judgment and will inevitably influence future decisions, even unconsciously. BroadcastCo's liability is based on the foreseeability that the CMO would rely on ingrained professional knowledge, not on her subjective admissions.

**D. Incorrect.** The statute and case law do not require multiple hires or explicit instruction to establish reason to know. The objective circumstances—the CMO's position and access to trade secrets, her recruitment into a competitive role, BroadcastCo's knowledge of the competitive overlap, and the company's failure to investigate or restrict access—satisfy the reason to know standard in isolation. Hiring multiple competitors' executives would strengthen the case but is not required.

***

## Question Q3.12 — Scope of Authorized Use and Breach Through Exceeding Boundaries

### 1. Stem
SoftwareCo licensed its proprietary accounting software to AccountingFirm under a Software License Agreement (SLA). The SLA permitted AccountingFirm to "use the Software solely for the purpose of processing accounting transactions for AccountingFirm's own internal operations. AccountingFirm shall not use the Software for the benefit of third parties, shall not share the Software with customers, and shall not create derivative products based on the Software." AccountingFirm's engineers, over time, became intimately familiar with the Software's code architecture, algorithms, and data structures (which SoftwareCo considers trade secrets). AccountingFirm then created a modified version of the Software and began offering it as a standalone service to AccountingFirm's corporate clients as "AccountingFirm's Custom Accounting Solution." AccountingFirm did not copy SoftwareCo's source code directly but applied the same algorithmic principles and architectural patterns to develop its own product. SoftwareCo discovered the unauthorized product and sued for misappropriation of trade secrets.

### 2. Options
A. AccountingFirm is not liable because it wrote its own code and did not copy SoftwareCo's source files or violate the source code license terms.
B. AccountingFirm may be liable for misappropriation if it used knowledge of the Software's trade secrets (code architecture, algorithms) acquired under the authorized internal-use license in a manner that exceeded the scope of authorization (commercial offering to third parties).
C. AccountingFirm is liable only if SoftwareCo can prove that AccountingFirm's product performs identically to the original Software or uses the exact same algorithm code.
D. The SLA is a contract and does not govern trade secret liability; even if AccountingFirm exceeded its license scope, it is not liable for trade secret misappropriation unless it violated a separate confidentiality duty.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Trade secret misappropriation is not limited to direct copying of source code. Under *Altavion v. Konica Minolta*, a company may be liable for using trade secret principles and concepts acquired under a lawful disclosure for purposes beyond the scope of the original authorization. Here, the SLA explicitly limited use to internal accounting operations, not to derivative product development for third parties. While AccountingFirm wrote new code, it applied protectable trade secrets (the algorithmic approach, architectural patterns) in a manner that violated the use restriction.

**B. Correct.** *3M v. Pribyl* and *Altavion v. Konica Minolta* establish that misappropriation occurs when someone with lawful access to information uses it in a way that violates the authorized scope or breaches an implied duty. Here, (1) AccountingFirm's authorized use was limited to internal operations; (2) AccountingFirm's employees acquired intimate knowledge of SoftwareCo's protectable trade secrets (architectural and algorithmic design); (3) AccountingFirm then used that knowledge to create a competing product for commercial sale, exceeding the authorized scope; and (4) this exceeded use breached both the license restriction and the implied duty of confidence that accompanies disclosure of trade secrets. The fact that AccountingFirm wrote its own code does not immunize the use of the underlying principles; the question is not whether the code was copied but whether the knowledge derived from the authorized disclosure was used beyond authorization.

**C. Incorrect.** Identical performance or code is not required. The misappropriation lies in the use of specific, nonpublic principles and patterns acquired through the licensed relationship in a manner that violates the scope of authorization. Even a somewhat different final product that embodies the same architectural or algorithmic insights can constitute misappropriation if the insights were derived from a trade secret source and used beyond the authorized scope.

**D. Incorrect.** While the SLA is a contract, trade secret law and contract law overlap in this context. An implied confidentiality duty arises from the disclosure of trade secrets, independent of a written confidentiality clause. The SLA's use restrictions create an objective boundary of authorized use; exceeding that boundary while relying on knowledge of trade secrets can constitute both breach of contract and trade secret misappropriation. SoftwareCo need not prove a separate written confidentiality provision; the disclosure in a restricted license context implies a duty to use the information only within the authorized scope.

***

## Question Q3.13 — Distinctions Between Idea Submission and Trade Secret Misappropriation

### 1. Stem
IndieDesigner submitted a product concept to MassProducer for evaluation as part of a potential manufacturing partnership. The concept was an innovative modular furniture system. IndieDesigner did not sign an NDA, and MassProducer's standard submission form (which IndieDesigner signed) stated: "Submission is voluntary. Company assumes no obligation to pursue the idea or to compensate Submitter. Submitter retains all patent rights." IndieDesigner also stated verbally at the meeting that he expected "fair treatment" and hoped MassProducer would "respect" his intellectual effort if it decided to develop the concept. MassProducer's product development team reviewed the concept, declined to partner, and two years later launched a modular furniture line that incorporated several core design features of IndieDesigner's submission. IndieDesigner sued, alleging trade secret misappropriation. MassProducer argues the concept was merely an idea, not a protectable trade secret, and that IndieDesigner's submission form waived all claims.

### 2. Options
A. IndieDesigner is barred from recovery because the form explicitly waived all obligations and MassProducer assumed no duty to Submitter.
B. IndieDesigner's claim may proceed if the modular furniture system involved specific, nonpublic technical details or design specifications (beyond general ideas), and if the circumstances of the submission (marked confidential, expected fair treatment, context of business discussion) could support an implied confidential relationship, despite the form's general waiver language.
C. IndieDesigner's claim fails because general product ideas are not trade secrets; only detailed technical processes and algorithms are protected by trade secret law.
D. MassProducer is liable because it used IndieDesigner's concept two years after the submission, which demonstrates intent to misappropriate and delay.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While *Burten v. Milton Bradley* teaches that an explicit waiver of a confidential relationship can bar recovery, the waiver language here is general and does not explicitly disclaim confidentiality in tort (as opposed to contract). The form addresses contractual obligations (no obligation to pursue, no compensation owed, patent rights retained) but does not explicitly state "we do not receive this in confidence" or "confidentiality is not owed." Courts interpreting ambiguous forms will allow a jury to consider context. The fact that IndieDesigner stated expectation of "fair treatment" and "respect" at the meeting could be evidence that the parties understood a confidence duty despite the form.

**B. Correct.** *Burten* establishes that ambiguous disclaimer language does not necessarily bar a confidential relationship claim. Courts look to the context, including (1) whether the submission was made in the context of a specific business opportunity (partnership), (2) whether the submitter communicated an expectation of confidentiality, and (3) whether additional language (like the patent clause or a reproduction restriction) suggests care and limitation of use. Here, those factors are present. Critically, IndieDesigner must establish that the modular furniture system involved more than a general idea—it must have involved specific design details, construction specifications, or innovative techniques not commonly known or readily ascertainable in the industry. If the submission included such specifications and they were marked confidential or referenced as confidential by IndieDesigner, a court may find that an implied confidential relationship could be inferred, and MassProducer's incorporation of those specifics into its product could constitute misappropriation, notwithstanding the ambiguous form language.

**C. Incorrect.** While general ideas are not protectable trade secrets, the law recognizes that specific technical details, design specifications, and nonpublic methodologies embodied in product concepts can be trade secrets. The Restatement gives examples including patterns, devices, methods, techniques, and processes—which can apply to detailed product designs. The question is whether the submission went beyond vaguer ideation to specific, nonpublic technical information.

**D. Incorrect.** A two-year delay does not, standing alone, demonstrate misappropriation or establish a temporal link sufficient for liability. MassProducer could have developed its product independently or through different means in the interim. The temporal gap may be evidence of copying but is not determinative. IndieDesigner must still prove that MassProducer used specific trade secrets or a confidential relationship breach, not merely that a similar product was eventually developed.

***

## Question Q3.14 — Employee Noncompetition vs. Trade Secret Restrictions

### 1. Stem
JuniorEngineer worked for DefenseContractor for three years in the missile guidance systems division, with access to classified and proprietary technical specifications regarding inertial navigation algorithms. DefenseContractor's handbook stated: "Employees shall not disclose or misuse proprietary technical information." However, JuniorEngineer signed no written NDA and no non-compete agreement. JuniorEngineer left DefenseContractor and joined AerospaceRival, a competitor, in an identical role working on similar navigation systems. JuniorEngineer did not take any documents, but she applied the same algorithmic and system design approaches to AerospaceRival's products. DefenseContractor sued to enjoin JuniorEngineer from working for AerospaceRival and claimed trade secret misappropriation. JuniorEngineer argues that absent a written non-compete or NDA, she is free to work where she wishes and to apply her general knowledge and skill.

### 2. Options
A. DefenseContractor's argument fails because it did not require a written NDA or non-compete; those documents are necessary to establish a duty of confidentiality regarding trade secrets.
B. DefenseContractor may succeed in proving misappropriation if the algorithmic and system design approaches are protectable trade secrets, DefenseContractor took reasonable steps to maintain secrecy, and JuniorEngineer's use in the same field likely involves reliance on those secrets, even absent a written NDA.
C. Trade secret law prohibits any employee from working for a competitor in the same field once the employee has learned confidential information, regardless of written agreements.
D. DefenseContractor must choose between seeking an injunction against JuniorEngineer working or seeking damages for misappropriation; it cannot pursue both remedies simultaneously.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While written agreements provide clearer evidence of intent to maintain confidentiality, they are not prerequisites to an implied confidential relationship. *Burten v. Milton Bradley* and trade secret doctrine recognize that implied duties of confidence arise from employment relationships, particularly where the employee has access to trade secrets and the employer communicates (through handbook policies or otherwise) that the information is confidential. A written NDA strengthens the case but is not essential.

**B. Correct.** Under *3M v. Pribyl* and *PepsiCo v. Redmond*, an implied duty of confidentiality can arise in the employment context. Here, (1) DefenseContractor's handbook policy indicates that proprietary technical information is not to be disclosed or misused, creating notice of a confidentiality expectation; (2) the algorithmic and design approaches are likely protectable trade secrets (nonpublic, valuable because of secrecy, known only to the division's engineers); (3) JuniorEngineer's role at AerospaceRival is substantially similar and would require her to address the same technical challenges; and (4) therefore, reliance on the navigation approaches she learned at DefenseContractor is likely or inevitable. This satisfies the elements for an implied duty and threatened misappropriation. The absence of a written NDA does not foreclose liability if the other elements are established.

**C. Incorrect.** Trade secret law does not prohibit all employee movement to competitors. *PepsiCo* explicitly stated that "trade secret law does not provide a reserve clause for solicitous employers." Employees may move and may use general skill and knowledge. The law restricts use of specific, protectable trade secrets in breach of a duty of confidence, not employee mobility itself. Here, the question is whether the navigation algorithms constitute trade secrets and whether their use was in breach of a duty—not whether JuniorEngineer can work for a competitor.

**D. Incorrect.** DefenseContractor may pursue both injunctive relief and damages. An injunction is often sought when misappropriation is ongoing or threatened, and damages are awarded for harm caused. The remedies are complementary, not exclusive.

***

## Question Q3.15 — Business Context and Implied Duty in Vendor Relationships

### 1. Stem
ToolCo is a parts supplier. Equipment Manufacturer contracted with ToolCo to manufacture specialized die-cast aluminum components according to Equipment Manufacturer's proprietary design specifications. The specifications included Equipment Manufacturer's process flows, material compositions, and quality control parameters—all considered trade secrets by Equipment Manufacturer. The purchase order did not include an NDA or confidentiality clause, but the drawings were marked "CONFIDENTIAL." ToolCo manufactured the components successfully. Six months later, ToolCo began offering identical components to Equipment Manufacturer's competitors at a lower price, using the specifications and manufacturing process it had learned while producing for Equipment Manufacturer. Equipment Manufacturer sued for misappropriation of trade secrets. ToolCo argues that its status as a vendor, not an employee, means no confidentiality duty arose from the purchase order alone.

### 2. Options
A. ToolCo is correct; confidentiality duties only arise in employment relationships, not in vendor or supplier relationships.
B. An implied confidential relationship may arise between a supplier and manufacturer in the context of a collaborative manufacturing arrangement, particularly where specifications are marked confidential and the supplier receives proprietary process information necessary to perform the contract.
C. ToolCo is liable for misappropriation only if Equipment Manufacturer can prove that ToolCo specifically intended to breach a duty at the time of contracting.
D. ToolCo's ability to manufacture identical components for competitors demonstrates that the specifications are not truly trade secrets because they have become part of standard industry knowledge.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** Confidential relationships are not limited to employment contexts. *Burten v. Milton Bradley* and the Restatement recognize that confidential relationships typically arise in business relationships between "purchasers and suppliers" and "prospective licensees and licensors." A vendor relationship that involves disclosure of proprietary specifications and processes is precisely the context in which courts infer a confidential relationship.

**B. Correct.** Courts recognize that a confidential relationship may be implied in supplier/vendor contexts when (1) the supplier receives proprietary, marked-confidential information necessary to perform the contract, (2) the information is nonpublic and not part of standard industry practice, and (3) the nature of the disclosure (custom specifications rather than off-the-shelf services) suggests an expectation of confidentiality. Here, ToolCo received marked-confidential manufacturing specifications and proprietary process flows as part of a collaborative relationship. The fact that no written NDA was included does not defeat the implied relationship, particularly given the confidential markings and the custom nature of the work. ToolCo's subsequent use of that information to compete directly with Equipment Manufacturer and to serve Equipment Manufacturer's competitors breaches the implied duty.

**C. Incorrect.** *Inevitable disclosure* and misappropriation liability do not depend on subjective intent at the time of contracting. They depend on objective circumstances—what the parties understood and what would be foreseeable to a reasonable party. ToolCo's state of mind regarding whether a duty existed is not relevant if the circumstances objectively establish one.

**D. Incorrect.** The fact that ToolCo can now manufacture identical components does not negate the trade secret status or establish misappropriation. Trade secrets often lose protection once independently derived by lawful means. However, ToolCo did not independently derive the specifications; it learned them through a confidential supplier relationship. The confidentiality duty persisted after the contract ended, and ToolCo's use of the specifications to compete directly, without new authorization, constitutes a breach and misappropriation.

***

## Question Q3.16 — Inevitable Disclosure and Burden of Proof at Preliminary Injunction Stage

### 1. Stem
StrategyOfficer held a VP-level position at ShoppingMall Corp, where she had access to ShoppingMall's confidential financial forecasts, tenant recruitment strategy, and lease negotiation playbooks. ShoppingMall did not have her sign an NDA. StrategyOfficer resigned and accepted a position with CompetitorDeveloper, a company developing a competing shopping complex in the same metro market. At the preliminary injunction hearing, ShoppingMall alleged that StrategyOfficer would "inevitably disclose" its trade secrets in her new role. CompetitorDeveloper's counsel argued that the threat of disclosure is speculative and insufficient; CompetitorDeveloper said it has a policy against misuse and that StrategyOfficer promised she would not use ShoppingMall information. The district court denied the preliminary injunction, finding that ShoppingMall had not established a likelihood of success on its inevitable disclosure theory because the threat was merely speculative.

### 2. Options
A. The district court was correct; inevitable disclosure is too speculative a theory and courts should require proof of actual disclosure before granting an injunction.
B. The district court may have erred if ShoppingMall presented specific evidence that StrategyOfficer's position at CompetitorDeveloper directly overlaps with her prior role, that she possesses intimate knowledge of ShoppingMall's trade secrets in the relevant areas, and that CompetitorDeveloper's prior employee hiring or conduct suggests a pattern of seeking insider knowledge—facts that would support a finding of likelihood of success on a threat of misappropriation.
C. ShoppingMall would prevail if it could prove that CompetitorDeveloper's promised use restrictions are insufficiently protective, but absent proof of bad faith in CompetitorDeveloper's hiring, the threat remains speculative.
D. The policy against misuse and StrategyOfficer's promise render the inevitable disclosure theory inapplicable; no preliminary injunction can issue based on threatened misappropriation if the defendant has taken contractual protective measures.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** *PepsiCo v. Redmond* established that courts may enjoin threatened (not yet actualized) misappropriation if the threat is substantial and probable, not merely speculative. The statute itself permits injunctions against "actual or threatened misappropriation." The whole point of a preliminary injunction is to prevent harm before it occurs. Courts do not require proof of actual disclosure if the circumstances create a likelihood of threatened misappropriation.

**B. Correct.** *PepsiCo* provides the framework: a plaintiff establishes threatened misappropriation by showing (1) the defendant possesses intimate knowledge of the plaintiff's trade secrets, (2) the defendant's new position places her in a competitive role where the secrets would be directly useful, (3) the circumstances (including hiring of a direct competitor's insider, overlap of responsibilities, and absence of segregation measures) create a high probability that the trade secrets will inevitably influence the defendant's decisions. Here, if ShoppingMall presented evidence that (i) StrategyOfficer had high-level access to confidential tenant, financial, and negotiation strategies, (ii) her new role as VP at a competing developer in the same market would directly benefit from that knowledge, (iii) CompetitorDeveloper hired her specifically because of her market knowledge, and (iv) CompetitorDeveloper took no precautions to segregate her from strategy decisions, that would establish a likelihood of success on a threatened misappropriation claim. CompetitorDeveloper's bare policy against misuse and StrategyOfficer's promise (which *PepsiCo* found insufficient given her deception in that case) would not necessarily defeat the injunction.

**C. Incorrect.** The inquiry is not limited to whether CompetitorDeveloper's restrictions are sufficiently protective. Under *PepsiCo*, the court focuses on the likelihood that the employee will inevitably rely on trade secrets in the new role, regardless of contractual promises. An employee's promise to compartmentalize is often found insufficient when the new role inherently requires reliance on the prior knowledge. Additionally, evidence of hiring patterns (seeking insiders) is relevant to show consciousness of wrongdoing or at least constructive knowledge.

**D. Incorrect.** Contractual protective measures may be relevant to the injunction balancing, but they do not render the inevitable disclosure theory inapplicable. *PepsiCo* contemplates situations where the defendant company has a confidentiality policy and the employee promises compliance, yet an injunction was still warranted based on the inevitability of reliance. The district court must weigh whether those measures are sufficient to reduce the threat to an acceptable level, but they do not automatically defeat an otherwise well-founded inevitable disclosure claim.

***

## Question Q3.17 — Accidental Disclosure and Promptness of Return/Destruction

### 1. Stem
ConsumerLabs sent an email containing its confidential market research data (including customer survey responses and statistical analysis) to TestCorp's general company email address (wrong recipient) instead of to a specific contact at TestCorp. The email was marked "CONFIDENTIAL" and the subject line read "Market Research - DO NOT DISTRIBUTE." TestCorp's IT department received the email and, within its normal email review process, flagged it as potentially containing sensitive information. A week passed. TestCorp did not return the email or confirm deletion, but instead, one of TestCorp's analysts accessed the data, reviewed the market research, and provided a summary to TestCorp's marketing team, which used it to inform TestCorp's competitive strategy. ConsumerLabs discovered the disclosure a month later and sued TestCorp for misappropriation. TestCorp argues that it did not affirmatively acquire the information through improper means; it was merely a passive recipient of a misdirected email.

### 2. Options
A. TestCorp is not liable because it received the information by mistake and cannot be held responsible for an email that was sent to the wrong address.
B. TestCorp may be liable under UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) if it knew or had reason to know the information was a trade secret acquired by accident, but failed before a material change of position (such as accessing and using the data) to delete the information or promptly notify ConsumerLabs and request instructions.
C. TestCorp's liability depends on whether it could have foreseen that the email was intended for a specific person; if it could have reasonably determined the intended recipient, it has a duty to do so but is not liable for accessing the information.
D. TestCorp incurs no liability under trade secret law for accidental disclosures; those are governed by privacy law or data breach notification requirements, not by the UTSA.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) provides a limited safe harbor for recipients of accidentally acquired information, the safe harbor is not absolute. The statute imposes a duty: if the recipient knew or had reason to know the information was a trade secret acquired by accident and had not materially changed position, the recipient must refrain from using or disclosing it and must promptly notify the discloser or take steps to avoid benefiting from the mistake. Passive receipt followed by active use (as occurred here) can constitute misappropriation.

**B. Correct.** UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) states: "[liability attaches] before a material change of his position, [if he] knew or had reason to know that it was a trade secret and that knowledge of it had been acquired by accident or mistake." Here, (1) TestCorp knew or should have known the email was confidential (it was marked CONFIDENTIAL and flagged by IT), (2) it should have understood that misdirection to a general company email indicated accidental disclosure, (3) TestCorp did not promptly delete or return the information, and (4) instead, TestCorp underwent a material change of position by accessing the data, analyzing it, and incorporating it into business decisions. By that time, TestCorp can no longer claim the safe harbor. The statute contemplates that an innocent recipient has a narrow window to disassociate from accidentally received information; TestCorp's failure to do so and its deliberate analysis and use constitute misappropriation.

**C. Incorrect.** While identifying and contacting the intended recipient would have been a prudent step, the statute does not condition liability on failure to identify the recipient. The duty arises simply upon knowing or having reason to know that the information is a trade secret acquired by accident; the recipient must promptly refrain from using it and should notify the discloser or seek instructions. TestCorp's failure to take any of those steps, combined with its deliberate access and use, crosses the line into misappropriation.

**D. Incorrect.** Accidental disclosure is specifically governed by UTSA § 1(2)(b)(3) and is not displaced by privacy or data breach law. Trade secret law provides the remedy for misappropriation of accidentally disclosed trade secrets, and that section creates a limited safe harbor for innocent recipients who promptly disassociate themselves from the information.

***

## Question Q3.18 — Scope of Duty in Joint Venture Context

### 1. Stem
MediDevice A and MediDevice B entered into a joint venture to co-develop a novel surgical implant. Under the Joint Venture Agreement, MediDevice A disclosed its proprietary polymer formulation, sterilization techniques, and biocompatibility testing protocols—all confidential trade secrets. The agreement specified: "Each party shall maintain all shared information in strict confidence and shall use such information solely for the purpose of the joint venture. Upon termination of the venture, each party shall return or destroy all shared information and shall not exploit the joint venture knowledge for independent product development for two years." After the joint venture ended (amicably), MediDevice B began developing a competing implant using the same polymer formulation and sterilization techniques it had learned during the venture. MediDevice B argues that since the venture is dissolved and MediDevice B is no longer bound by the non-use restriction, it is free to use the information.

### 2. Options
A. MediDevice B is correct; once the joint venture terminates, any non-use restrictions end, and MediDevice B may use the information freely.
B. MediDevice B may be liable for misappropriation if the post-venture non-use clause is enforceable as written, or if trade secret law imposes a continuing duty not to exploit confidential information acquired during the venture in direct competition, even absent the contractual restriction.
C. MediDevice B's use of the polymer formulation for a competing implant is permissible because MediDevice B developed its own product; it did not copy the physical product or MediDevice A's documents.
D. MediDevice B incurs liability only if it explicitly promised never to use the information after the venture ended.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While contractual non-use clauses may have explicit durations, trade secret law imposes a continuing duty of confidence with respect to information that qualifies as a trade secret. The termination of the joint venture does not automatically terminate the trade secret status of the information; it remains a trade secret if it continues to derive value from secrecy and if the original owner maintains reasonable efforts to preserve secrecy. MediDevice A's use of the information in the venture was authorized, but MediDevice B's independent use after termination, in direct competition with MediDevice A, constitutes a breach of the implied duty to maintain confidentiality regarding the trade secrets.

**B. Correct.** Under *Altavion v. Konica Minolta*, use of confidential information acquired in a business relationship (in that case, an evaluation; here, a joint venture) for purposes outside the scope of the relationship can constitute misappropriation. Here, (1) MediDevice B acquired the polymer formulation and sterilization techniques as a trade secret within the authorized venture context, (2) the venture agreement expressly limited use to the joint venture purpose, (3) the agreement also imposed a post-termination non-use restriction (which may or may not be separately enforceable), and (4) MediDevice B's use of that information to independently develop a competing product exceeds the authorized scope. Additionally, trade secret law imposes a continuing duty of confidence: once information is communicated as a trade secret in confidence, the recipient owes a duty not to use it beyond the authorized scope, even after the relationship ends. MediDevice B may be liable under either the contractual non-use clause (if enforceable) or under the trade secret misappropriation doctrine (based on the duty of confidence arising from the joint venture context).

**C. Incorrect.** Trade secret misappropriation is not limited to copying of products or documents. *Altavion* and *3M v. Pribyl* establish that using trade secrets—including formulations, processes, and techniques—in a manner that exceeds the scope of authorized disclosure constitutes misappropriation, regardless of whether the user independently wrote code or designed a product shell. Here, MediDevice B's use of the specific polymer formulation and sterilization techniques is actionable.

**D. Incorrect.** An explicit promise is not required. An implied duty arises from the joint venture context and the disclosure of information as confidential for a limited purpose. MediDevice B's use for an independent competitive purpose breaches that implied duty, and the trade secret remains protected despite the venture's termination.

***

## Question Q3.19 — Scope of Reverse Engineering Restriction and Enforceability

### 1. Stem
EngineerCorp sold a specialized test instrument to AnalyticsCorp. The instrument included printed circuit boards and firmware. EngineerCorp's end-user license agreement (EULA) included the clause: "Licensee shall not reverse engineer, disassemble, or create derivative works based on this instrument. Any violation shall be subject to liquidated damages of $50,000 and attorney's fees." AnalyticsCorp's engineers, in an attempt to understand the instrument's functionality to better integrate it with their systems, carefully disassembled the circuit board, photographed components, analyzed signal flows, and documented the design. They did not extract or decompile the firmware. They used their analysis to design a compatibility adapter for their own systems (not a product offered for sale). EngineerCorp discovered the reverse engineering and sued for breach of the EULA and trade secret misappropriation. AnalyticsCorp argues that the EULA clause is unenforceable because reverse engineering is a lawful right under trade secret law.

### 2. Options
A. AnalyticsCorp is correct; trade secret law permits reverse engineering of lawfully obtained products, and contractual restrictions on reverse engineering are unenforceable as against public policy.
B. The enforceability of the reverse-engineering restriction depends on the reasonableness of the restriction, the consideration given for the license, and whether the restriction is commercially reasonable in the context; if enforceable, violation may support breach of contract liability, and if the design constitutes a trade secret, misappropriation liability may also apply.
C. EngineerCorp can enforce the reverse-engineering restriction only if it also applied physical security measures (potting, encryption) to prevent analysis; bare contractual language is insufficient.
D. AnalyticsCorp's internal use of the analysis for a compatibility adapter, rather than for a competing product, insulates AnalyticsCorp from trade secret liability but not from breach of contract liability.

### 3. Correct Answer
**B**

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
**A. Incorrect.** While reverse engineering of publicly sold products is permitted under trade secret law, the rule is not absolute. Parties may contractually restrict reverse engineering through license agreements, and courts generally enforce such restrictions provided they are not unconscionable or contrary to specific statutory law (such as the Digital Millennium Copyright Act in certain contexts). The Restatement recognizes that a trade secret owner may use contractual restrictions to prevent reverse engineering. A plain prohibition on reverse engineering in an EULA, supported by consideration (the right to use the instrument), is likely enforceable as a matter of contract law.

**B. Correct.** Courts balance multiple considerations: (1) reverse engineering of lawfully obtained products is a recognized proper means under trade secret law, but (2) contractual restrictions on that right are enforceable if they are reasonable, supported by consideration, and not unconscionable; (3) if the license restricts reverse engineering and the licensee violates it, breach of contract liability attaches; (4) separately, if the instrument's design qualifies as a trade secret and the licensee's reverse engineering extracts that secret in breach of a confidentiality duty arising from the licensed relationship, trade secret misappropriation may also apply. Here, the restriction appears reasonable (AnalyticsCorp knew it was agreeing to refrain from reverse engineering as a condition of use), it is supported by the license to use the instrument, and the liquidated damages clause is not unconscionable. Both breach of contract and potentially misappropriation claims are viable.

**C. Incorrect.** Physical security measures may strengthen a case for trade secret protection and for reasonableness of a reverse-engineering restriction, but they are not prerequisites. The EULA itself can serve as a contractual mechanism to maintain secrecy. Courts have recognized that contractual language can suffice to establish an expectation of confidentiality and to preclude reverse engineering, independent of physical security.

**D. Incorrect.** Whether AnalyticsCorp's use is internal or commercial may affect damages calculations but does not determine liability. A breach of the EULA restriction occurs upon reverse engineering, regardless of subsequent use. Additionally, misappropriation liability is not limited to competing products; unauthorized use of trade secrets in any context in breach of a duty can be actionable. AnalyticsCorp's internal adapter development may have reduced damages, but it does not eliminate liability.

***

## Question Q3.20 — Third-Party Recipient's Awareness and Constructive Knowledge

### 1. Stem
SalesRep worked for TelecoCorp and had access to TelecoCorp's confidential customer list, pricing models, and sales strategies. SalesRep left TelecoCorp to join CompetitorPhone, accepting a position in business development. At CompetitorPhone, nobody asked SalesRep to sign a confidentiality agreement or to disclose his prior employment restrictions. CompetitorPhone's CEO hired him specifically because of his industry experience and knowledge of TelecoCorp's customer base. Within the first month, SalesRep suggested that CompetitorPhone target specific high-value customers from TelecoCorp's list (customers he knew from his prior role), and CompetitorPhone executed that strategy. TelecoCorp discovered the poaching and sued CompetitorPhone for misappropriation, alleging that CompetitorPhone had reason to know SalesRep was using confidential information. CompetitorPhone argues that it did not explicitly ask SalesRep to breach his duty and that SalesRep may have learned the customer names through lawful industry channels.

### 2. Options
A. CompetitorPhone is not liable because it did not solicit or instruct SalesRep to use TelecoCorp's information, and SalesRep may have learned the information through lawful means.
B. CompetitorPhone may be liable if it hired SalesRep specifically because of his knowledge of TelecoCorp's customers, did not require him to verify that he had no confidentiality restrictions, and did not segregate him from sales targeting decisions—circumstances that would support a finding that CompetitorPhone had reason to know he would rely on his prior employment knowledge.
