ManufacturerA's success depends on whether the NDA is interpreted to survive SupplierB's acquisition and to restrict ConglomerateC's use; if the contract does not address acquisition or assignment, courts must infer intent, and the absence of anti-subcontracting language may weigh against finding that downstream recipients were intended to be bound.

C. ManufacturerA will recover against both SupplierB and ConglomerateC because the "Confidential" marking imposes an independent duty on all recipients regardless of the contract.

D. ManufacturerA will lose because SupplierB's acquisition by ConglomerateC constituted a change of control that automatically released SupplierB from its confidentiality obligations.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** While ConglomerateC may not be a direct party to the NDA, it could still be liable for misappropriation if it knowingly received confidential information under circumstances giving rise to a duty of confidence. However, the enforceability against ConglomerateC depends on how the NDA is interpreted and whether downstream recipients are bound.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the complexity of supply chain trade secret protection. Courts examine: (1) whether the NDA survives acquisition (typically yes, unless the agreement provides otherwise); (2) what the parties intended regarding subcontracting and downstream recipients; (3) whether the absence of explicit anti-subcontracting language suggests the parties intended to permit assignment to successors. ManufacturerA is disadvantaged by failing to explicitly address acquisition scenarios and failing to include anti-subcontracting language. However, the "Confidential" marking and the nature of the information suggest an intent to limit disclosure. ConglomerateC's transfer to its competing division is particularly problematic because it represents a clear conflict of interest. ManufacturerA likely has a stronger claim against SupplierB (for failing to restrict downstream use) than against ConglomerateC (which is a successor and may not have had clear notice of the confidentiality obligations).

C. **Incorrect.** A "Confidential" marking helps establish intent, but it does not create an automatic duty on all recipients independent of the circumstances. The analysis requires examination of the relationship between the parties and their knowledge of the confidential nature of the information.

D. **Incorrect.** Acquisition does not automatically release parties from confidentiality obligations. In fact, successors typically step into the shoes of the acquired company and assume its obligations. However, the successor may have defenses if it had no notice of the confidential nature of the information.

---

## Question Q5.12 — Auction or Pitch Disclosure Without NDA

### 1. Stem
StartupX is seeking venture capital funding and presents its business plan, technology roadmap, and proprietary customer acquisition algorithm to ten different venture capital firms during a "demo day" pitch competition. StartupX distributes a written executive summary marked "Confidential" to each firm but does not require any of the VCs to sign an NDA beforehand, relying on industry norms and the firms' stated commitment to confidentiality. One VC firm, BadActorVC, later discloses StartupX's algorithm to CompetitorY, a company in which BadActorVC has a significant investment. StartupX discovers this and sues BadActorVC for misappropriation. BadActorVC argues that the absence of a signed NDA defeats StartupX's claim. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. StartupX will succeed because the "Confidential" marking on the executive summary creates a contractual confidentiality agreement by implication.

B. StartupX may succeed if it can establish an implied confidential relationship based on industry custom (that VCs receiving pitch materials expect to maintain confidentiality) and the marked confidentiality of the materials, but the absence of a signed NDA is a significant weakening factor, particularly if StartupX could have but failed to require NDAs.

C. StartupX will lose because the business community norm is that VCs receive pitch materials without confidentiality obligations, and trade secret law does not override industry custom.

D. StartupX will succeed regardless of the NDA because disclosure to investors is considered a protected class of disclosure under the UTSA/DTSA.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** A "Confidential" marking does not, by itself, create a binding confidentiality agreement. Contractual formation requires mutual assent, which is typically evidenced by a signed agreement. However, the marking is evidence supporting an implied confidential relationship.

B. **Correct.** This reflects a balanced approach to investor pitch disclosures. Courts recognize that in the venture capital context, there is an expectation of confidentiality for marked materials, and industry custom supports an implied confidential relationship. However, StartupX's failure to require signed NDAs is a significant weakness. Courts distinguish between: (1) disclosures to professional investors in a formal pitch context (where implied confidentiality may exist despite the absence of a signed NDA), and (2) indiscriminate public disclosures (where no confidentiality can be implied). If the ten VCs are sophisticated investors who routinely handle confidential information, and if StartupX marked the materials "Confidential," a court may find an implied duty even without a signed agreement. However, StartupX's position is much stronger if it had required NDAs, and the absence of NDAs may be dispositive depending on the jurisdiction and the nature of the materials.

C. **Incorrect.** While industry custom is relevant, it does not eliminate the possibility of an implied confidential relationship. Moreover, the venture capital industry's custom is that marked materials are received in confidence, even absent signed NDAs.

D. **Incorrect.** There is no special "protected class" for investor disclosures under the UTSA/DTSA. Investor disclosures are analyzed under the same standards as other business disclosures. StartupX must establish reasonable efforts and an implied or express duty of confidence.

---

## Question Q5.13 — Inadequate Audit Clauses and Vendor Oversight

### 1. Stem
PharmaCorp contracts with DataVendor to process and analyze clinical trial data. The contract includes a standard confidentiality clause but provides only that PharmaCorp may "audit compliance upon reasonable notice." DataVendor is a large, publicly held company, and PharmaCorp's contract officer assumes that DataVendor's compliance program is robust and decides not to conduct any audits during the three-year contract period. Unknown to PharmaCorp, DataVendor's data security team has been storing the clinical trial data on unsecured servers with outdated encryption, and DataVendor's access controls have not been updated in five years. A disgruntled DataVendor employee exfiltrates the clinical trial data and sells it to a CompetitorCorp. When PharmaCorp discovers the breach, it sues both DataVendor and CompetitorCorp for trade secret misappropriation. CompetitorCorp argues that DataVendor's negligence in maintaining security destroyed the trade secret status, and that PharmaCorp's failure to audit DataVendor proves PharmaCorp did not take reasonable efforts. How should the court analyze this?

### 2. Options
A. PharmaCorp will lose because its failure to exercise the audit right demonstrates that it did not take reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy.

B. PharmaCorp may still succeed against both defendants if it took reasonable precautions before disclosing to DataVendor (e.g., classifying the data, requiring confidentiality agreements, requiring certain technical safeguards), but PharmaCorp's failure to audit despite having the right to do so is a factor that cuts against the showing of reasonable efforts, and DataVendor's inadequate security is a separate breach that may limit PharmaCorp's overall claim.

C. PharmaCorp will definitely succeed against CompetitorCorp despite DataVendor's negligence, because CompetitorCorp is the improper means actor and DataVendor is merely negligent.

D. The trade secret was destroyed the moment DataVendor's systems were breached, so PharmaCorp has no claim regardless of prior reasonable efforts.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** The failure to exercise an audit right is a factor, but it is not dispositive. PharmaCorp must have taken reasonable precautions before disclosure. If PharmaCorp carefully selected DataVendor, required contractual confidentiality and security standards, and had no reason to suspect DataVendor's inadequate practices, the failure to audit may not defeat the claim. However, the failure to audit is a weakness.

B. **Correct.** This reflects a nuanced analysis. PharmaCorp's overall showing of reasonable efforts depends on what it did before disclosure, not solely on post-disclosure oversight. If PharmaCorp required DataVendor to meet certain security standards and to certify compliance, PharmaCorp's failure to audit is less damaging. However, if PharmaCorp simply handed off the data to DataVendor with minimal oversight, the failure to audit is more problematic. DataVendor's inadequate security is a separate issue: DataVendor may be liable for breach of contract and for violation of an implied duty of confidence. CompetitorCorp's liability depends on whether it acquired the information through improper means (e.g., knowing that DataVendor obtained it through breach). The exfiltrating employee clearly used improper means, and CompetitorCorp's knowledge of that fact is probative.

C. **Incorrect.** CompetitorCorp's liability depends on whether CompetitorCorp knew or had reason to know that the information was a trade secret and that it was obtained through improper means. If CompetitorCorp had no reason to know of DataVendor's breach, or if CompetitorCorp believed it was purchasing legitimately obtained data, liability is less clear. However, the fact that the employee was a DataVendor employee and was selling the data on the black market suggests knowledge of improper means.

D. **Incorrect.** The mere fact of a breach does not extinguish all claims. PharmaCorp may still recover damages for the misappropriation, even though ongoing protection of the information is no longer available.

---

## Question Q5.14 — Clean Room Failure and Cross-Project Contamination

### 1. Stem
SoftwareCorp discloses proprietary source code to EngineerVendor under a detailed development services agreement. The agreement includes comprehensive clean room procedures: (1) only five named engineers may access the code; (2) any engineer working on competing projects must use a physically segregated workstation with a "Chinese wall"; (3) code access is logged; (4) no engineer may work on code for CompetitorZ (SoftwareCorp's primary competitor) within one year of handling SoftwareCorp's code. Three months into the engagement, EngineerVendor assigns Engineer Johnson, who has access to SoftwareCorp's code, to work on a CompetitorZ project in the same physical facility (though at a different workstation). Johnson uses code architecture principles from SoftwareCorp's codebase to optimize CompetitorZ's project. SoftwareCorp discovers this and seeks a preliminary injunction to prevent further work on the CompetitorZ project and to bar Johnson from any work on competing projects. EngineerVendor argues that the clean room procedures are unenforceable restrictions on employee assignments. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. The injunction will be denied because the clean room procedures are unreasonable restrictions on employee mobility and business operations.

B. The injunction will likely be granted because the comprehensive nature of the clean room procedures, combined with the clear breach (assigning Johnson to a CompetitorZ project in violation of the one-year restriction), demonstrates that EngineerVendor violated the agreement and threatens the misappropriation of trade secrets.

C. The injunction will be granted only against CompetitorZ, not against EngineerVendor, because CompetitorZ is the ultimate competitor benefiting from the breach.

D. The injunction will be denied if SoftwareCorp cannot prove that Johnson actually used SoftwareCorp's code in the CompetitorZ project.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Clean room procedures and "Chinese wall" provisions are well-established in the software development and professional services industries. They are not unreasonable restrictions; they are standard risk-mitigation tools. Courts routinely enforce assignment restrictions tied to confidentiality and trade secret protection.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the doctrine of specific contractual performance. The agreement's clean room procedures are detailed, reasonable, and directly tied to trade secret protection. The one-year restriction on working with competitors is a particularized safeguard that courts regularly enforce. EngineerVendor's assignment of Johnson to the CompetitorZ project directly violates this restriction. SoftwareCorp need not prove that Johnson actually used specific code lines; the violation of the clean room procedure and the structural risk of cross-contamination (Johnson's knowledge of SoftwareCorp's code architecture combined with his work on CompetitorZ's project) is sufficient to establish likelihood of misappropriation. A preliminary injunction is the appropriate remedy to prevent further contamination.

C. **Incorrect.** While CompetitorZ may be a secondary beneficiary, the injunction should be directed at the primary violator (EngineerVendor) to halt the breach of the development services agreement and prevent further misappropriation risk.

D. **Incorrect.** Under the UTSA/DTSA, courts recognize that actual misappropriation and threatened misappropriation are both enjoinable. SoftwareCorp need not prove actual use; the threat of misappropriation, evidenced by the breach of the clean room procedures, is sufficient.

---

## Question Q5.15 — Duty of Confidence in Licensing Negotiations

### 1. Stem
LicenseCo, a technology licensor, enters into licensing discussions with ProspectCorp, a potential licensee in an adjacent market. During negotiations, LicenseCo discloses detailed technical specifications, performance benchmarks, and pricing models for LicenseCo's core product, but the parties have not yet signed a license agreement. LicenseCo does not require ProspectCorp to sign an NDA, relying instead on ProspectCorp's verbal assurance that it will maintain confidentiality "as is customary in our industry." After six weeks, the licensing deal falls through due to pricing disputes. Three months later, LicenseCo learns that ProspectCorp has incorporated LicenseCo's performance benchmarks into its own product marketing materials and has shared LicenseCo's pricing model with two industry competitors. LicenseCo sues for misappropriation of trade secrets. ProspectCorp argues that without a signed NDA, no confidential relationship was formed, and the information was disclosed without restrictions. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. LicenseCo will succeed because licensing negotiations inherently create an implied confidential relationship, and ProspectCorp's incorporation of the benchmarks into its own marketing materials is per se misappropriation.

B. LicenseCo may succeed if it can establish that an implied confidential relationship arose from the licensing negotiation context, but the absence of a signed NDA, combined with ProspectCorp's uncontrolled sharing with competitors, suggests that LicenseCo may not have taken sufficient reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy, and ProspectCorp's reliance on industry custom to justify its disclosure may be reasonable.

C. LicenseCo will lose because the information was disclosed during negotiations before any binding agreement was reached, and trade secret law does not protect pre-contractual disclosures.

D. LicenseCo will prevail if it can show that ProspectCorp's pricing model and performance benchmarks are, in fact, trade secrets independent of whether ProspectCorp took unauthorized steps.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** While licensing negotiations may give rise to an implied confidential relationship, this is not automatic. The context supports such an inference (the parties are negotiating in good faith, the disclosure is technical in nature), but the absence of a written agreement and the vague reliance on "industry custom" weaken the inference.

B. **Correct.** Licensing negotiations do often support an implied confidential relationship, particularly when technical information is disclosed. However, LicenseCo's failure to require a signed NDA, despite having the opportunity and leverage to do so, undermines the showing of reasonable efforts. Additionally, ProspectCorp's sharing of the information with industry competitors, while a breach if a confidential relationship existed, was enabled by LicenseCo's own failure to establish contractual boundaries. A court might find an implied duty in the licensing context, but also find that LicenseCo's failure to document confidentiality weakens its position. ProspectCorp's appeal to "industry custom" is somewhat circular; if the custom is that licensing disclosures are confidential, then LicenseCo was reasonable not to require a formal NDA. However, if the custom is more permissive, LicenseCo bore the risk of misunderstanding. The likely outcome is that LicenseCo will have a claim, but a weakened one compared to if it had required an NDA.

C. **Incorrect.** Trade secret law does protect disclosures made during pre-contractual negotiations. Courts frequently find implied confidential relationships in negotiation contexts. The absence of a final signed agreement does not automatically eliminate trade secret protection.

D. **Incorrect.** While the nature of the information (pricing models, performance benchmarks) may support a finding that the information is a trade secret, that finding alone does not establish misappropriation. LicenseCo must also establish that ProspectCorp violated a duty of confidence and acquired the information through improper means (or under circumstances giving rise to such a duty).

---

## Question Q5.16 — Successor Liability and Changed Circumstances Post-Disclosure

### 1. Stem
ComponentMaker licenses its manufacturing process to PartnerInc for use in a joint manufacturing facility under a 10-year license agreement that includes comprehensive confidentiality and non-use provisions. The agreement specifies that all confidential information remains the property of ComponentMaker and may be used only for the licensed manufacturing purpose. Five years into the agreement, PartnerInc is acquired by LargeConglomerate, which has divisions competing with ComponentMaker in adjacent markets. At the time of acquisition, the assignment clause in the original agreement required that any successor "assume all obligations of confidentiality and non-use." LargeConglomerate formally accepted this condition. However, shortly after the acquisition, LargeConglomerate's competing division begins a research project on a similar manufacturing process and, through access to PartnerInc's historical records and research notes derived from ComponentMaker's process, makes rapid progress on its own process development. ComponentMaker sues LargeConglomerate and PartnerInc for misappropriation. LargeConglomerate argues that the historical research notes are now part of PartnerInc's institutional knowledge and that LargeConglomerate's competing division is entitled to use any information available in PartnerInc's records as part of the acquisition. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. ComponentMaker will succeed against both defendants because LargeConglomerate's competing division is using information derived from ComponentMaker's trade secrets, which violates the non-use provision in the original agreement.

B. ComponentMaker's case is strong against PartnerInc for allowing a competing division to access confidential information, but is more difficult against LargeConglomerate (which is a successor in interest), unless ComponentMaker can prove that LargeConglomerate knowingly facilitated the use of ComponentMaker's secrets in its competing research.

C. ComponentMaker will lose because the ten-year license agreement is a complete shield against trade secret claims, and any misappropriation claims must be brought under the contract alone.

D. ComponentMaker will prevail against LargeConglomerate even if LargeConglomerate's competing research was conducted independently, because the assignment clause required assumption of confidentiality obligations.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** While ComponentMaker has a strong argument based on the non-use provision and the competing division's use of historical records derived from the licensed process, the analysis is complicated by the acquisition. LargeConglomerate's acquisition of PartnerInc created a changed circumstance: a new entity with competing interests now controls access to the information. LargeConglomerate did assume the confidentiality obligations by accepting the assignment clause, but the question is whether LargeConglomerate actively facilitated the misappropriation or merely passively inherited conflicting interests.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the nuanced analysis of successor liability in trade secret cases. ComponentMaker has a clear contract claim against PartnerInc for allowing a competing division to access confidential information. Against LargeConglomerate, the analysis is more complex. LargeConglomerate did assume the confidentiality obligations through the assignment clause, but assuming an obligation is distinct from actively misappropriating trade secrets. ComponentMaker must establish that LargeConglomerate (1) knew or should have known that the information was confidential, (2) knew that the competing division's project implicated ComponentMaker's process, and (3) facilitated access to the information. If ComponentMaker can establish that LargeConglomerate deliberately authorized the competing division to access PartnerInc's historical records and research notes derived from ComponentMaker's process, then ComponentMaker has a strong case. If LargeConglomerate merely allowed the competing division to use all available institutional knowledge without specifically facilitating access to confidential information, the claim is weaker.

C. **Incorrect.** While the license agreement is the primary governing instrument, trade secret law operates concurrently. ComponentMaker may assert both contract claims and trade secret misappropriation claims.

D. **Incorrect.** Assumption of a confidentiality obligation does not create liability for independent research. However, if LargeConglomerate's research actually relied on ComponentMaker's secrets (rather than being truly independent), then LargeConglomerate is liable.

---

## Question Q5.17 — Due Diligence in M&A Transactions and Disclosure Risks

### 1. Stem
BuyerCorp is conducting due diligence on a potential acquisition of TargetCorp, a software development firm. As part of the due diligence process, BuyerCorp's technical team reviews TargetCorp's source code repositories, architecture documentation, and customer lists. TargetCorp provides access without requiring BuyerCorp to sign a detailed NDA, instead providing only a boilerplate confidentiality commitment that states "Buyer will maintain the confidentiality of Target's information as is customary in M&A transactions." The due diligence reveals that TargetCorp's primary customer—who is also a major customer of BuyerCorp—would likely defect to a competitor if the acquisition price is too high. Based on this intelligence, BuyerCorp reduces its offer price. TargetCorp rejects the lower offer and the acquisition falls through. BuyerCorp then begins hiring TargetCorp's engineers and, in its competing product development, incorporates certain architectural concepts that BuyerCorp's team learned during the due diligence process. TargetCorp discovers this and sues BuyerCorp for misappropriation of trade secrets. BuyerCorp argues that the information disclosed during due diligence is now part of BuyerCorp's engineers' general knowledge and that independent development explains any similarities. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. TargetCorp will lose because information disclosed during failed M&A due diligence is not protected by trade secret law, as the transaction context removes any confidentiality.

B. TargetCorp may succeed if it can establish that BuyerCorp received the information under circumstances creating an implied duty of confidence and that BuyerCorp breached that duty by using the information in its competing product, but TargetCorp's reliance on a boilerplate M&A confidentiality clause and failure to require a more detailed NDA is a weakness, and BuyerCorp's claim of independent development (or mere incorporation of general architectural concepts) may be a complete defense.

C. TargetCorp will definitely succeed because BuyerCorp viewed source code and architecture documentation, which are per se trade secrets.

D. TargetCorp will succeed against BuyerCorp's technical team members but not against BuyerCorp itself, because individual employees are bound by confidentiality even if the corporate entity is not.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** M&A due diligence disclosures are not automatically unprotected. However, the context does create certain expectations. In acquisitions or licensing transactions, courts recognize that a degree of disclosure is necessary and that some information will become part of the buyer's knowledge base. However, this does not eliminate confidentiality; it merely recognizes that the buyer's use will be measured differently.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the complex doctrine of trade secret protection in M&A contexts. TargetCorp may establish an implied duty of confidence based on the M&A transaction context and the nature of the information disclosed. However, the boilerplate confidentiality clause is weak, and a more detailed NDA would have strengthened TargetCorp's position. Additionally, BuyerCorp's claim of incorporation of general architectural concepts (as opposed to specific code or algorithms) and independent development is a credible defense. Courts distinguish between: (1) use of specific, identifiable trade secrets (source code, algorithms, data structures), and (2) incorporation of general architectural concepts learned from due diligence. The former is likely misappropriation; the latter is less clear. TargetCorp's strongest claim would be that BuyerCorp hired TargetCorp's engineers specifically to bypass the need for direct access to TargetCorp's source code, thereby circumventing the confidentiality expectations of the due diligence context.

C. **Incorrect.** Source code and architecture documentation are typically trade secrets, but viewing them during due diligence is not per se misappropriation. The analysis requires examination of the circumstances and the use made of the information.

D. **Incorrect.** While individual employees may be bound by confidentiality, the corporate entity (BuyerCorp) is also bound by the confidentiality commitment it made during due diligence.

---

## Question Q5.18 — Ambiguous Disclosure Agreements and Course of Conduct

### 1. Stem
InvestorX attends a pitch meeting with StartupY and reviews a confidential one-page summary of StartupY's product and market strategy. The summary is marked "Confidential" but contains only a single sentence: "This information is provided in confidence." The summary does not state what uses are permitted, how long confidentiality lasts, or what InvestorX may do with the information after the meeting. At the pitch meeting, InvestorX's representative verbally states, "We're interested in funding this, but we need to discuss it with our investment committee." StartupY gives InvestorX permission to share the summary with the committee "so they can evaluate the opportunity." InvestorX then shares the summary with its investment committee (five people) and, as a matter of course, also circulates it to an advisory board member, TechAdvisor, who has relevant expertise and to whom InvestorX routinely shares investment materials. TechAdvisor is not asked to sign any confidentiality agreement and is not told of the confidentiality marking on the original summary. TechAdvisor reviews the material and, based on its analysis, approaches StartupY directly to offer competing advisory services, disclosing key elements of StartupY's strategy in an attempt to demonstrate his value to StartupY. StartupY sues InvestorX and TechAdvisor for breach of confidence and misappropriation. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. StartupY will recover against InvestorX but not TechAdvisor, because InvestorX breached its duty to limit disclosure to the investment committee only.

B. StartupY may recover against InvestorX for inadvertently creating a conflict of interest (sharing with an external advisor) that went beyond the permission to share with the investment committee, but recovery against TechAdvisor is less likely unless TechAdvisor knew or should have known the information was confidential.

C. StartupY will lose against both defendants because the one-sentence confidentiality commitment is too vague to create a binding duty of confidence.

D. StartupY will recover against both InvestorX and TechAdvisor because the "Confidential" marking creates an automatic duty on all downstream recipients.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** The conclusion that InvestorX breached its duty is reasonable, but the question of TechAdvisor's liability is more nuanced. TechAdvisor's liability depends on whether TechAdvisor knew or should have known that the information was confidential.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the doctrine of limited consent and the breach of secondary recipients' duties. InvestorX's oral permission to share with the "investment committee" reasonably includes the committee members but does not clearly extend to external advisors. InvestorX's sharing with TechAdvisor, without removing the confidentiality marking or explaining the restrictions, is arguably a breach. However, TechAdvisor's liability depends on notice and knowledge: Did TechAdvisor see the "Confidential" marking? Would a reasonable advisor understand that the material was confidential? If TechAdvisor received the material without any indication of confidentiality and without any instruction not to use it, TechAdvisor's liability is less clear. However, the fact that InvestorX shared the material suggests that TechAdvisor should have inquired or made reasonable assumptions about confidentiality. TechAdvisor's proactive approach to StartupY (rather than merely analyzing and discarding the material) is probative of misuse. StartupY's strongest claim is against InvestorX, with a secondary claim against TechAdvisor if TechAdvisor had knowledge or should have had knowledge of the confidential nature.

C. **Incorrect.** A one-sentence commitment marked "Confidential" is not ideal, but it is not so vague as to defeat all duties. While more detailed language would help StartupY's case, the circumstances (pitch meeting context, marking, oral discussion of need-to-know sharing) support an inference of confidentiality.

D. **Incorrect.** The "Confidential" marking is a strong signal, but it does not create automatic duties on all downstream recipients who have no connection to the original understanding. TechAdvisor's liability depends on whether TechAdvisor knew or should have known about the confidentiality and whether TechAdvisor received the material with any indication of restrictions.

---

## Question Q5.19 — Regulatory Disclosure Obligations and Trade Secret Preservation

### 1. Stem
PharmaCorp develops a novel drug formulation and files a patent application that must, by law, be accompanied by detailed technical specifications and performance data disclosing significant aspects of PharmaCorp's manufacturing process and formulation adjustments. Regulatory law requires that similar data be submitted to the FDA in a marketing application. The patent application will be published and become public; the FDA submission may be kept confidential under regulatory protective orders. PharmaCorp faces a dilemma: disclosing all technical data in the patent application will enable competitors to design around the patent, but filing the patent with limited disclosure may result in a narrow patent grant. PharmaCorp decides to disclose the full technical data in the patent application and to request confidential treatment of the FDA submission. Months later, a competitor gains access to the published patent and uses the disclosed technical specifications to develop a competing product. PharmaCorp sues for misappropriation of trade secrets. The competitor argues that once the information was published in the patent application, it became publicly available and could not be protected as a trade secret. How should a court analyze this?

### 2. Options
A. The court will hold that the information is no longer a trade secret once published in a patent application, regardless of PharmaCorp's intent, because the publication requirement is a necessary cost of patent protection.

B. The court will likely find that the information lost trade secret status upon publication, but PharmaCorp may have a separate claim for damages based on breach of patent law or regulatory violations if the competitor's use constitutes patent infringement; trade secret law does not provide ongoing protection for information that has become publicly available through required disclosure.

C. The court will preserve trade secret protection for the information despite publication, because PharmaCorp complied with legal disclosure requirements and did not voluntarily relinquish its rights.

D. The court will hold that PharmaCorp forfeited all claims against the competitor because PharmaCorp chose to file a patent application rather than maintain the information as a trade secret.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** While publication does generally destroy trade secret status, the analysis is not fully captured by saying publication is a "necessary cost." Courts recognize that PharmaCorp faced a genuine dilemma and made a strategic choice to pursue patent protection. The loss of trade secret protection is a consequence of that choice, but it does not preclude other claims.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the practical reality that information disclosed in published patent applications cannot remain trade secrets. Once the information is public, it is "readily ascertainable" by proper means (reading the published patent), and trade secret protection ceases. However, this does not leave PharmaCorp without recourse. If the competitor uses the information in a way that constitutes patent infringement, PharmaCorp has patent remedies. If the competitor's use violates the terms of the regulatory protective order or FDA rules, PharmaCorp has regulatory remedies. Trade secret law simply does not provide ongoing protection for publicly available information. PharmaCorp's choice to pursue patent protection entailed this trade-off.

C. **Incorrect.** Trade secret law does not preserve protection for information that becomes publicly available, even when the disclosure is compelled by law. The "readily ascertainable by proper means" doctrine applies to all lawful disclosures, including required legal disclosures. Once the patent is published, anyone can read it; the information is no longer a trade secret.

D. **Incorrect.** While PharmaCorp lost trade secret protection by filing the patent, PharmaCorp did not "forfeit all claims." Patent infringement claims remain available. Moreover, PharmaCorp's choice to file the patent was strategic and lawful; it does not constitute a waiver of all intellectual property rights.

---

## Question Q5.20 — Contractual Waiver of Confidentiality and Reliance on Negotiations

### 1. Stem
DesignCorp is negotiating a potential design services agreement with ClientCorp. DesignCorp's lawyer proposes a comprehensive NDA that would protect DesignCorp's pre-existing design templates and methodologies that DesignCorp plans to use in ClientCorp's project. Before finalizing the NDA, ClientCorp's legal team proposes a revision stating: "ClientCorp may use any design templates, methodologies, or approaches disclosed by DesignCorp during the engagement for any purpose, including in future projects, without restriction." DesignCorp objects but is pressured by its sales team to "just accept it" because ClientCorp is an important prospective customer. DesignCorp signs the revised agreement. During the three-month engagement, DesignCorp discloses its proprietary design templates and agile development methodology to ClientCorp's team. After the engagement ends, ClientCorp uses the disclosed templates and methodology in a competing project with a third party. DesignCorp sues for misappropriation of trade secrets. ClientCorp argues that the explicit waiver in the contract bars the claim. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. The court will enforce the contractual waiver and dismiss DesignCorp's trade secret claim entirely, because parties are free to contract away trade secret protections.

B. The court will likely enforce the explicit contractual waiver as to the disclosed templates and methodology, barring DesignCorp's trade secret claim, because parties' clear written agreement on the allocation of rights should be respected, even if the waiver was signed under sales pressure.

C. The court will enforce the waiver only if DesignCorp can establish that it was signed under duress, and will otherwise hold that the waiver does not extend to the commercial use by third parties (as opposed to internal use by ClientCorp).

D. The court will void the waiver as against public policy because it permits a party to misappropriate trade secrets, and will allow DesignCorp's trade secret claim to proceed.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This overstates the enforceability of waivers. While parties can contractually waive certain protections, courts will not enforce waivers that are ambiguous, unconscionable, or procured through fraud or duress. However, a clear, written waiver signed by parties with bargaining power is generally enforceable.

B. **Correct.** This reflects the principle that parties are generally free to allocate intellectual property rights through contract. The revised NDA contains an explicit, unambiguous waiver permitting ClientCorp to use the disclosed templates and methodology "for any purpose, including in future projects, without restriction." This language is clear enough to waive trade secret protection as to those disclosed items. DesignCorp's objection to the waiver, while understandable, does not negate its legal effect once DesignCorp signed. DesignCorp's argument that it was pressured to accept the waiver is a question of duress, which would require extraordinary circumstances (threat of harm, inability to negotiate) to overcome. Mere sales pressure is not enough. The fact that ClientCorp uses the templates with a third party does not overcome the waiver; the contract explicitly permits use "for any purpose."

C. **Incorrect.** While duress is a potential defense, mere sales pressure is not duress in the legal sense. Additionally, the contract's language ("for any purpose") does extend to use with third parties.

D. **Incorrect.** Courts do not void waivers of trade secret protection as against public policy. The public policy in favor of freedom of contract generally outweighs concerns about the allocation of trade secrets between sophisticated parties. If DesignCorp was concerned about third-party use, DesignCorp should have negotiated narrower language.

---

## Question Q5.21 — Dissolution and Post-Engagement Confidentiality Survival

### 1. Stem
ResearchPartners enters into a joint research agreement with UniversityLab to develop a new diagnostic test. The agreement requires both parties to maintain confidentiality of disclosures made during the collaboration and specifies that "Confidentiality obligations shall continue for two years after termination of this agreement." The agreement is silent on what happens to jointly developed information and what rights each party retains after the collaboration ends. After 18 months, the parties terminate the agreement due to disagreement on commercialization strategy. UniversityLab wishes to publish a research paper describing the diagnostic methodology that was jointly developed. ResearchPartners argues that the methodology is a trade secret jointly developed during the engagement and that UniversityLab's publication violates the confidentiality obligation that, under the express terms, continues for two years post-termination. UniversityLab argues that the two-year survival clause refers only to information brought into the collaboration by each party, not to jointly developed information, and that academic freedom and publication rights override confidentiality obligations where no express restriction on publication is stated. What is the likely outcome?

### 2. Options
A. The court will enforce the two-year post-termination confidentiality clause and bar UniversityLab's publication, because the agreement's language provides that confidentiality obligations "continue" post-termination.

B. The court will likely enforce the two-year confidentiality obligation but may distinguish between the parties' pre-existing trade secrets (clearly protected) and jointly developed information, the treatment of which depends on the parties' intent as inferred from the agreement's silence on publication rights and the academic context of the collaboration.

C. The court will not enforce the confidentiality clause against publication because academic freedom and the University's First Amendment rights override contractual confidentiality obligations.

D. The court will void the two-year survival clause as an unreasonable restraint on competition and publication.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** While the agreement's language does provide for two-year survival, the analysis cannot stop there. The silence on publication and on the treatment of jointly developed information creates an ambiguity that courts must resolve. The mere invocation of a survival clause does not automatically bar all use of jointly developed information.

B. **Correct.** This reflects a nuanced approach to post-collaboration disputes. The two-year survival clause almost certainly protects each party's pre-existing trade secrets (information brought into the collaboration). However, jointly developed information is more complex. Courts examine: (1) whether the parties intended jointly developed information to be jointly owned and shareable, or exclusively controlled by ResearchPartners; (2) the academic context of the collaboration and the parties' reasonable expectations regarding publication; (3) whether express language prohibiting publication existed. UniversityLab's argument that academic freedom supports publication has some weight in a university context, but it does not override clear contractual language. Here, the silence on publication is ambiguous. A court would likely find that the confidentiality obligation applies during the collaboration but may permit publication after the two-year period if the parties' intent (inferred from the academic context and the silence on publication rights) suggests that publication was contemplated or permitted. Alternatively, a court might find that jointly developed information is subject to the confidentiality obligation for the full two years but may be published thereafter.

C. **Incorrect.** While academic freedom is a legitimate interest, it does not automatically override contractual confidentiality obligations, particularly where the University entered into a specific agreement acknowledging confidentiality. However, the academic context is relevant to construing the parties' intent.

D. **Incorrect.** Two-year survival clauses are not *per se* unreasonable restraints on competition. They are standard in many collaborative agreements. A court would not void the clause as a general matter, though it might construe it narrowly.

---

# CHAPTER 6 – ENFORCEMENT

***

## Question Q6.1 — Recognizing Actionable Misappropriation

### 1. Stem
Acme Biologics suspects that a former researcher, Dr. Lin, has joined a competitor, Genex, which suddenly releases a product suspiciously similar to Acme’s. Acme has no evidence of how Dr. Lin obtained or used any information, but believes her prior access to confidential lab protocols shows a high risk that secrets were conveyed. Acme sues for trade secret misappropriation under the DTSA. Which doctrine’s threshold is most at issue here?

### 2. Options
A. That "possession" alone is actionable as misappropriation under trade secret law.
B. That actual misappropriation—improper acquisition, use, or disclosure—must be proven, not merely suspected, unless evidence supports "inevitable disclosure" under applicable law.
C. That Dr. Lin is automatically liable due to her prior NDA.
D. That similarity in product design proves theft as a matter of law.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Mere possession, or access, is not misappropriation; actionable misappropriation requires improper acquisition, use, or disclosure.  
B. **Correct.** Trade secret law intervenes only when a secret is actually or "threatened" to be misappropriated, as emphasized in this chapter. Acme must plead and likely show facts supporting that Dr. Lin used, disclosed, or acquired secrets by improper means, or in a select few jurisdictions with expansive doctrine, that disclosure is "inevitable"—but even that requires evidence, context, and is jurisdiction-dependent.  
C. **Incorrect.** Breach of NDA is a contract issue; trade secret law focuses on misuse.  
D. **Incorrect.** Product similarity is relevant but not conclusive; independent development or reverse engineering are permissible.

***

## Question Q6.2 — Proving Trade Secret Status

### 1. Stem
ChemCo sues a competitor for taking "our proprietary formula, unique combination of known compounds." In court, ChemCo’s only evidence of secrecy is that it asked employees to be discreet and did not share the combination publicly. The formula was never labeled confidential, not subject to access controls, and retained in open lab notebooks. Which key element of trade secret litigation is at risk of fatal failure here?

### 2. Options
A. Value from secrecy.
B. Reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy, which must include evidence approaching a corporate “way of life” rather than casual or accidental secrecy.
C. Novelty of the formula.
D. Proof of defendant’s intent to cause harm.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Value is necessary, but courts presume value if secrecy and efforts are proven.
B. **Correct.** The chapter stresses that courts enforce the "reasonable efforts" standard rigidly. "Secrecy by accident" or a vague company ethos of discretion isn't enough. Reasonable efforts are demonstrated by tangible, practiced behaviors: proper labeling, access control, training, NDAs, etc.—all of which are subject to adversarial scrutiny in litigation.  
C. **Incorrect.** Novelty is not legally required; a unique combination of known elements may be a secret if not ascertainable by proper means.
D. **Incorrect.** Intent is not the core threshold; improper use/acquisition is.

***

## Question Q6.3 — "Improper Means" and Social Engineering

### 1. Stem
SkyTech’s drone blueprints are stored in a secure database with access limited to the engineering team. Lara, an engineer at a rival firm, calls SkyTech’s front desk, impersonates an employee, and asks for IT to reset a password. She gains access and downloads The files. Is this actionable “improper means” under the DTSA/UTSA, and why?

### 2. Options
A. No; access was technically possible using valid credentials.
B. Yes; using deception (social engineering) to defeat reasonable security is classic "improper means."
C. Only if the defendant uses the information to manufacture a physical product.
D. No, unless there is proof Lara signed an NDA.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Technical access does not equal lawful access.  
B. **Correct.** The deliberate use of deception, such as social engineering to trick company staff, is squarely within “improper means” under §1(2) UTSA and DTSA: “theft, bribery, misrepresentation, breach or inducement of a breach of a duty to maintain secrecy, or espionage” all qualify, as highlighted by DuPont v. Christopher in the chapter.  
C. **Incorrect.** Manufacturing is not required for misappropriation; improper acquisition alone suffices.
D. **Incorrect.** Liability attaches with or without an NDA if the means are improper.

***

## Question Q6.4 — Reverse Engineering as Proper Means

### 1. Stem
A competitor buys a single SkyTech drone at full retail price, disassembles it in their private workshop, and through trial and error, reconstructs the control algorithm. They do not hack, bribe, or breach contract. SkyTech sues for misappropriation. Is this likely to succeed under the standards from the chapter?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because the algorithm was initially secret and copying is always misappropriation.
B. No, because reverse engineering of a lawfully obtained product is a recognized “proper means” for acquiring a trade secret.
C. Yes, if the reverse engineering took less than one month.
D. No, only if a patent also covers the algorithm.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** The law protects secrets from *improper* acquisition, not all forms of learning.
B. **Correct.** The chapter discusses at length that reverse engineering of lawfully acquired goods is proper (e.g., DuPont v. Christopher’s boundaries, Bonito Boats). The key is there was no contractual restriction or use of improper means to acquire the information; thus, SkyTech's claim fails.
C. **Incorrect.** Timeframe is irrelevant; the method is what matters.
D. **Incorrect.** Patent status doesn't govern reverse engineering in trade secret law.

***

## Question Q6.5 — Independent Development Defense

### 1. Stem
StartUpPro is sued by its larger rival BigApps for allegedly using BigApps’ login authentication algorithm. During discovery, StartUpPro produces contemporaneous lab notebooks and Git logs showing years of internal work and no overlap in personnel. BigApps relies on the similarity of the login logic to claim theft. Which best characterizes StartUpPro’s strongest defense under the standards in this chapter?

### 2. Options
A. The secrets were independently developed without reference to or knowledge of BigApps’ approaches, which is a complete defense to misappropriation.
B. Only reverse engineering is a defense, not parallel development.
C. Evidence of similar products is conclusive proof of theft.
D. Hiring an engineer from BigApps is itself misappropriation.

### 3. Correct Answer
A

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Correct.** Independent development (i.e., deriving the information without reference to a secret) is an explicit safe harbor under UTSA and DTSA, as highlighted throughout the chapter. Parallel development is permitted; only theft, breach of a duty, or improper means are actionable.
B. **Incorrect.** Reverse engineering isn't the only defense—independent development is equally valid.
C. **Incorrect.** Similarity alone cannot defeat evidence of independent origin.
D. **Incorrect.** Hiring (absent misappropriation) is permitted.

***

## Question Q6.6 — General Knowledge and Skills: The "Portable Employee"

### 1. Stem
After 10 years as chief project manager at PowerGen, Ruth joins a competitor. She uses her expertise to streamline its project timelines, but does not bring any documents or code from PowerGen. PowerGen sues, claiming "inevitable use" of their confidential scheduling system, which Ruth designed. Which outcome aligns best with the chapter’s guidance?

### 2. Options
A. PowerGen succeeds, as Ruth’s skills are per se their intellectual property.
B. Ruth’s use of general skill and experience, accumulated over her career, is not actionable misappropriation under trade secret law.
C. Any time an employee improves a new workplace, trade secret misappropriation is presumed.
D. Ruth is liable unless she proves she never worked on similar projects before.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Employees own their general know-how absent a non-compete or improper act.
B. **Correct.** Courts and this chapter are clear: trade secret law does *not* restrict legitimate employee mobility, skill, experience, or "general knowledge," only the use or disclosure of specific, protected information by improper means. Otherwise, all job movement would be chilled.
C. **Incorrect.** Presumption runs the other way: lawful activity is assumed unless proven otherwise.
D. **Incorrect.** The burden is on the plaintiff to prove misappropriation.

***

## Question Q6.7 — Proving Secrecy: The Evidentiary Burden

### 1. Stem
DataViz sues for trade secret theft but, when pressed in discovery, produces only documentation that six engineers had access to design files and that no external partners were ever authorized to see them. There is no record of labeled "Confidential" files, password-protected folders, or employee training on secrecy. Which risk, identified in the chapter, threatens DataViz’s case most?

### 2. Options
A. The company’s documentation shows actual secrecy, which is all that is needed.
B. The lack of documentary evidence of reasonable efforts to protect the secret may prove fatal: secrecy is a “living condition,” not a label, and is subject to adversarial scrutiny.
C. So long as trade secrets were not published in a journal, litigation will succeed.
D. Verbal policies are enough for courts.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Internal access alone is not protection; externality matters less if internal controls are inadequate.
B. **Correct.** The plaintiff bears the burden of affirmatively proving “reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy.” As this chapter notes, this is not a mere "paper" requirement—courts investigate actual practice, training, and system controls. Lack of evidence is a common point of summary judgment defeat.
C. **Incorrect.** Avoiding publication is vital but not sufficient.
D. **Incorrect.** Courts demand evidence, not rhetoric.

***

## Question Q6.8 — The Three Pillars of Misappropriation

### 1. Stem
Robotics startup NextStep believes a rival has used one of three confidential subsystems — they cannot yet specify which one, but the rival’s robot is suspiciously similar. Their in-house counsel prepares to bring suit citing "use, acquisition, and/or disclosure." Which best matches the chapter's approach to these pillars?

### 2. Options
A. Plaintiff must plead and eventually prove at least one concrete act: improper acquisition, use, or disclosure; “and/or” claims, without specificity, almost always fail at summary judgment.
B. The mere act of leaving for a competitor is enough if similarity is shown.
C. Use is required for trade secret misappropriation.
D. Only external disclosure counts; internal use is exempt.

### 3. Correct Answer
A

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Correct.** The chapter emphasizes that simply naming the three pillars as catch-alls is insufficient. Plaintiffs must identify at least one specific act, explain *how* it was improper, and provide facts connecting it to the alleged secret; otherwise, a judge will likely dismiss or grant summary judgment for lack of specificity.
B. **Incorrect.** Employee mobility is not itself actionable.
C. **Incorrect.** Any of improper acquisition, use, or disclosure suffices.
D. **Incorrect.** Internal use for competitive advantage can be actionable.

***

## Question Q6.9 — When is NDA Use Sufficient?

### 1. Stem
MegaSys gives all employees a standard NDA at hiring. The NDA refers to "proprietary information" but does not enumerate trade secrets or provide training. An engineer leaves, and MegaSys sues him for misappropriation, presenting only the NDA as evidence of secrecy measures. Defendants show that documents were widely shared internally, and no access controls existed. Will the NDA alone suffice as "reasonable efforts"?

### 2. Options
A. Yes; a signed NDA always wins.
B. No; courts require holistic efforts. An NDA is strong evidence but cannot replace operational or technical controls.
C. Yes, for high-level employees only.
D. Only if the NDA is notarized.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** NDAs are useful but not determinative.
B. **Correct.** The chapter highlights that the NDA is a "ticket to the litigation," not the end of the analysis. Courts scrutinize whether the company backs up the NDA with labeling, access control, and systemic efforts. Isolated NDAs are table stakes, not a shield against poor operational practices.
C. **Incorrect.** All employees are covered; level is irrelevant.
D. **Incorrect.** Notarization is not required.

***

## Question Q6.10 — Vague Asset Descriptions

### 1. Stem
Throughout its risk inventory, Innovatech lists “our proprietary business model” and “customer-centric culture” as trade secrets. In court, it accuses a leaver of misappropriating “these unique approaches.” What outcome should Innovatech expect, based on the warning in this chapter?

### 2. Options
A. Courts aggressively defend vague business secrets.
B. Courts routinely dismiss claims for failure to identify a trade secret “with sufficient particularity” to allow the accused, and the court, to know what is at issue.
C. Litigants can always amend later with details.
D. It is up to a jury to decide what is proprietary.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Courts demand specifics.
B. **Correct.** The "particularity" requirement is central, as shown in the chapter. Plaintiffs that rely on generalities are dismissed early. Only a “defensible, sufficiently clear” description—enough to let the accused defend themselves and allow the court to decide—can pass.
C. **Incorrect.** Amendment is rare and not guaranteed, especially if the deficiency is conceptual, not factual.
D. **Incorrect.** Juries decide facts; what is in issue must be framed by the court.

***

## Question Q6.11 — Ex Parte Seizure: Rare Remedy

### 1. Stem
Facing a partner who is suspected of transferring huge volumes of source code abroad, Acme wants to use the DTSA’s “ex parte seizure” provision. The lawyer warns that this is “almost never granted.” What must Acme *most stringently* demonstrate for the court to even consider this step?

### 2. Options
A. That Acme will win its lawsuit eventually.
B. That ordinary injunctions or TROs would not suffice because there is credible, specific evidence that the adversary will destroy or hide evidence if given advance notice.
C. That the value of the secret is over $1 million.
D. That the defendant has been convicted of trade secret theft before.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Likelihood of success is required, but not sufficient.
B. **Correct.** The chapter walks through the DTSA’s eight-factor test, emphasizing the “necessity of seizure to prevent the propagation or dissemination of the trade secret” (see also *Janssen v. Evenus*). The most critical (and hardest) factor is proof that no ordinary process—TRO or injunction—would suffice because the defendant can’t be trusted to preserve the evidence.
C. **Incorrect.** No specific value threshold.
D. **Incorrect.** Past bad acts are not enough without future risk.

***

## Question Q6.12 — TROs and Irreparable Harm

### 1. Stem
FlexScan discovers a vendor is about to publicly display FlexScan’s prototype at an expo next week, in violation of NDA. FlexScan seeks a TRO. What evidence is most critical to the court’s analysis, as per the chapter?

### 2. Options
A. That disclosure will cause “irreparable harm” by terminating trade secret protection and cannot be adequately rectified by money damages.
B. That FlexScan’s profits dropped last year.
C. That the vendor was once late with a shipment.
D. That a TRO always grants a right to permanent injunction.

### 3. Correct Answer
A

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Correct.** Trade secret law uniquely recognizes that once destroyed, secrecy cannot be retrieved; thus, loss of exclusivity is quintessential “irreparable harm.” Courts cite this (and the “balance of equities,” Winter factors) as the basis for urgent intervention.
B. **Incorrect.** Profit trends are relevant only to damages, not immediacy.
C. **Incorrect.** Lateness does not equate to irreparable harm.
D. **Incorrect.** TROs are a stop-gap, not a guarantee of a permanent win.

***

## Question Q6.13 — Selecting the Right Damages Model

### 1. Stem
At trial, SecureData can show that the defendant used their database to land three large contracts SecureData would have captured ($2M in lost profits), *and* made an extra $500k in margin through efficiencies the plaintiff lacked. Which best reflects the appropriate measure of damages under the framework from this chapter?

### 2. Options
A. Reasonable royalty only.
B. Actual loss ($2M) plus unjust enrichment ($500k), provided there’s no double counting.
C. Whichever is greater, with no adjustment.
D. Awards are capped at the value of the trade secret.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Royalty is a fallback, not first choice when real harm/gain exists.
B. **Correct.** The law allows “actual loss” or “unjust enrichment… not otherwise accounted for,” to ensure plaintiffs are made whole and wrongdoers forfeit gains. Courts are careful to avoid double recovery but will add up losses and additional gains.
C. **Incorrect.** Cumulative awards require double-counting constraints.
D. **Incorrect.** Caps are for exemplary damages; compensatory damages can exceed the secret’s replacement cost.

***

## Question Q6.14 — Fee-Shifting and Bad Faith

### 1. Stem
BioMend sues for misappropriation of “all our methods,” produces no evidence, and later it emerges the defendant’s technology was long public in academic papers. The defendant nevertheless spends $200k in legal fees. Under the UTSA’s remedies section, can the defendant recover fees, and under what standard?

### 2. Options
A. No; each side pays their own fees in litigation.
B. Yes; if the court finds the claim was brought or maintained in bad faith—that is, without reasonable basis and with abuse of process.
C. Yes; fee shifting is mandatory in all trade secret claims.
D. Only if BioMend is a public company.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** UTSA creates a limited exception for bad faith.
B. **Correct.** Section 4 of the chapter notes fee shifting for (1) willful/malicious misappropriation (for plaintiffs), (2) bad faith claims/defenses (for winning defendants). Bad faith here is clear: lack of investigation, persistence in the face of contrary proof, and overbroad accusations.
C. **Incorrect.** It is discretionary and rooted in abuse.
D. **Incorrect.** Corporate status is irrelevant.

***

## Question Q6.15 — The Role of Particularity at Pleading

### 1. Stem
In its complaint, WidgetWorks alleges that a departing manager stole “valuable confidential business strategies, methods, and knowledge.” Defendant moves to dismiss for lack of particularity. What procedural hurdle does WidgetWorks face, as underscored in the chapter?

### 2. Options
A. There is no pleading requirement in trade secret cases.
B. Courts require “reasonable particularity” at the outset—enough specifics to let the defense and the court know what is disputed, not just “confidential information” label.
C. Plaintiffs need only particularize in discovery, not the complaint.
D. Courts allow catch-all allegations and rely on the jury.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** All claims require some specificity to reach discovery.
B. **Correct.** The “particularity” requirement is repeatedly emphasized; it is a filter for summary judgment and a defense against fishing expeditions. Plaintiffs that fail to define the what, where, how of their secret risk early defeat.
C. **Incorrect.** Late disclosures may mean crucial evidence is excluded or the case dismissed.
D. **Incorrect.** The jury gets only cases properly pleaded and proven.

***

## Question Q6.16 — Independent Contractors and “Improper Means”

### 1. Stem
FinanceApp outsources data analytics to a contractor, who—without permission—downloads a copy of raw data set and, after the contract ends, uses it for a separate startup. FinanceApp did not restrict this in the contract or instruct the contractor. Is this likely actionable under the standards set forth in the chapter?

### 2. Options
A. Yes, because independent contractors are always liable.
B. No, unless there was some contractual, technical, or situational duty that was breached; “improper means” usually requires at least implicit or explicit duty to maintain secrecy.
C. Yes, because use after a contract always equals breach.
D. Yes, if the contractor makes money from it.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Contractors are not held to a different standard than employees.
B. **Correct.** “Improper means” covers breach of duty, deception, or explicit contractual obligations. Without a legal or situational duty—contractual NDA, clear confidentiality provision, or at least an understood business norm—the contractor’s use of data after project completion may not be improper.
C. **Incorrect.** Not all post-contract use is prohibited unless restricted.
D. **Incorrect.** Profit is not the test; conduct and duty are.

***

## Question Q6.17 — Spoliation and Preservation of Evidence

### 1. Stem
After launching its lawsuit, CompuNex’s IT department, following routine policy, deletes backup emails older than six months—including several belonging to the key defendant. The opposing party discovers this and moves for sanctions for spoliation. What doctrine, detailed in the chapter, governs the court’s response?

### 2. Options
A. Deletion after suit is filed is per se fatal to plaintiff’s claim.
B. Once litigation is “reasonably anticipated,” parties must preserve relevant evidence; destruction after this triggers potential sanctions, including adverse inference, under spoliation doctrine.
C. Only willful deletion is sanctionable.
D. Routine IT policy excuses deletion.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Sanctions are discretionary and fact-driven.
B. **Correct.** The chapter explains that from the moment litigation is anticipated, a “litigation hold” must be in place, suspending data destruction for potentially relevant materials. Even unintentional violations can trigger severe sanctions.
C. **Incorrect.** Negligent destruction can still trigger inference instructions.
D. **Incorrect.** Routine policies do not excuse destruction *after* the duty attaches.

***

## Question Q6.18 — DTSA: Federal Court and Exterritorial Reach

### 1. Stem
A Silicon Valley company discovers an ex-employee, now working for a firm in Singapore, is using its trade secrets to develop products shipped back to US customers. What options are now explicitly available to the company under DTSA, that might not exist under prior state law regimes?

### 2. Options
A. File suit in Singapore only.
B. Assert US federal jurisdiction and use the DTSA’s extraterritorial reach to seek relief for acts of misappropriation outside the US that cause foreseeable harm within.
C. File suit only in California State Court.
D. Seek only criminal remedies.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** US federal courts now have clear jurisdiction in transnational cases under DTSA.
B. **Correct.** The chapter highlights DTSA’s expansions: uniform federal jurisdiction, and an explicit extraterritorial provision—if the misappropriation affects commerce or interests of the US and is by a US citizen, permanent resident, or company, the federal court may hear the claim.
C. **Incorrect.** State law is still available but lacks the clear extraterritoriality.
D. **Incorrect.** Civil and criminal avenues are both in play.

***

## Question Q6.19 — Criminal Remedy: Economic Espionage Act (EEA)

### 1. Stem
A company’s investigation reveals that a departing engineer has e-mailed sensitive source code to an offshore subsidiary of a Chinese state-owned electronics conglomerate. The company seeks to involve federal prosecutors. Under the EEA (as discussed in the chapter), what hurdle must be met for a successful prosecution under the "Economic Espionage" prong?

### 2. Options
A. That any theft occurred at all.
B. That the theft was intended to benefit a foreign government, instrumentality, or agent—not just a foreign company, but one connected to state or quasi-state entities.
C. That the secret was patented.
D. That the code involved was high-value.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Ordinary theft is not enough for the EEA’s “espionage” section.
B. **Correct.** As explained, the EEA distinguishes between ordinary trade secret theft (§1832) and economic espionage (§1831). For the latter, the prosecution must show the purpose was to benefit a foreign government or its instrumentalities—raising the bar well above mere export or commercial misappropriation.
C. **Incorrect.** Patents have nothing to do with EEA.
D. **Incorrect.** Value is not sufficient in this context.

***

## Question Q6.20 — The Remedy of Reasonable Royalty

### 1. Stem
In litigation, DeltaSoft cannot establish lost profits (the secret was never commercialized), nor can it prove the defendant made any quantifiable sales with the technology. However, the court finds that the secret was used in development and conferred real, albeit unquantified, benefit. What damages measure remains, per the chapter and statute?

### 2. Options
A. None—failure to prove monetary loss bars recovery.
B. Reasonable royalty, a hypothetical license fee reflecting what a willing licensor and licensee would have agreed to for lawful use.
C. Statutory damages of $150,000.
D. Punitive damages only.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** The “reasonable royalty” remedy exists precisely for hard-to-quantify harms.
B. **Correct.** The UTSA, DTSA, and courts accept a royalty as the “hypothetical market value” measure when other damages fail.
C. **Incorrect.** Statutory damages are not provided in trade secret law.
D. **Incorrect.** Punitive damages supplement, not replace, compensatory awards.

***

## Question Q6.21 — Enhanced Damages and Willful Misappropriation

### 1. Stem
Upon a finding that a defendant not only stole a secret but destroyed evidence, threatened witnesses, and then repeatedly lied in depositions, what is the maximum *multiplicative* damages penalty available to the court for “willful and malicious misappropriation” under the DTSA?

### 2. Options
A. Up to an additional 2x the compensatory damages (yielding treble, i.e., 1x + 2x = 3x total).
B. Automatic quadruple damages.
C. Only single damages.
D. The court must refer the defendant for criminal prosecution.

### 3. Correct Answer
A

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Correct.** Both UTSA and DTSA cap enhanced damages at “not more than 2 times” the compensatory amount, for willful and malicious misappropriation. Thus, the maximum is 3x (1x base + 2x penalty). The court may also award attorneys’ fees.
B. **Incorrect.** No fourfold multiplier exists.
C. **Incorrect.** Court has discretion to enhance for willfulness.
D. **Incorrect.** Criminal referral is separate and discretionary.

***

# CHAPTER 7 – STRATEGIC INTEGRATION

## Question Q7.1 — The TSPP Lifecycle and Static Planning Failure

### 1. Stem
TechStart Inc., a software company, implemented a comprehensive Trade Secret Protection Plan (TSPP) five years ago. The plan included detailed NDAs for all employees, access controls for proprietary code, and annual training sessions for the first two years. Since then, the company has tripled its workforce through aggressive hiring, migrated its infrastructure to cloud services, and shifted from a centralized development model to a distributed, remote-first structure. Legal compliance now focuses on ongoing regulatory audits rather than trade secret refresher training. When a former employee leaves to join a competitor and allegedly discloses core algorithms, the company seeks an injunction. However, the court notes that there is no evidence of updated training materials, refreshed NDAs for new hires post-2021, or documentation of access controls adapted to the new remote infrastructure. Which of the following best identifies the critical failure in TechStart's TSPP lifecycle?

A. The company failed to register its trade secrets with the U.S. Patent and Trademark Office.
B. The company failed to recognize that TSPP is a living system requiring continuous adaptation, particularly during periods of significant organizational change.
C. The company failed to secure written consent from all employees before transitioning to remote work.
D. The company focused too heavily on technology safeguards rather than on legal contracts.

### 2. Options
A. The company failed to register its trade secrets with the U.S. Patent and Trademark Office.
B. The company failed to recognize that TSPP is a living system requiring continuous adaptation, particularly during periods of significant organizational change.
C. The company failed to secure written consent from all employees before transitioning to remote work.
D. The company focused too heavily on technology safeguards rather than on legal contracts.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Trade secret law explicitly does not require registration or public disclosure—that is a patent mechanism. The distinction between behavior-based trade secret protection and registration-based patent protection is fundamental to Section 7.1. The Chapter emphasizes that secrecy itself is the basis of protection, not administrative registration.

B. **Correct.** This answer reflects the core principle articulated in Chapter 7 that "a Trade Secret Protection Plan (TSPP) is not a checklist to complete and file away. It is a living system that must evolve with your organization, adapting to new risks, technologies, and business realities." The Chapter explicitly warns that "as they scale, new hires arrive, access spreads, and the original protocols fray," and that "what worked with 20 engineers and one founder-lawyer may collapse at 200 employees across 3 countries." TechStart's failure to update training, refresh NDAs for post-2021 hires, or adapt access controls to remote infrastructure demonstrates precisely this failure to treat the TSPP as dynamic and responsive to organizational change.

C. **Incorrect.** While consent from employees is good practice, the Uniform Trade Secrets Act (UTSA) does not require written consent to conduct business remotely. The problem is not the absence of formal consent but the absence of updated protective measures adapted to the new working model. Legal enforceability of trade secret protection depends on demonstrating reasonable efforts—not on procedural formality with respect to work arrangements.

D. **Incorrect.** The Chapter discusses the importance of balancing technical, physical, legal, and operational safeguards. Overreliance on technology alone is a weakness, but underreliance on it is equally problematic. The issue is not the balance between technology and contracts, but the failure to update any protective measures after the company fundamentally changed its operational structure. Chapter 7 emphasizes that "growth is a vulnerability" and that TSPPs must scale with systems, not individuals.

***

## Question Q7.2 — Reasonable Efforts as a Dynamic Standard

### 1. Stem
BioPharma Ltd. operates a cutting-edge research facility developing novel drug formulations. Five years ago, it implemented what it believed was a state-of-the-art TSPP: all research data is encrypted at rest and in transit, employees sign broad NDAs covering "any information disclosed," access to the primary database is restricted to senior researchers, and the company conducts annual audits. However, the company has never updated its NDA language, never retrained employees on its secrecy obligations since the initial onboarding, never documented what specifically counts as a trade secret (the NDA simply covers "any information"), and judges' recent expectations have shifted toward expecting regular audits and contemporary training. A court is asked to determine whether BioPharma's safeguards constitute "reasonable efforts" under the Uniform Trade Secrets Act (UTSA). What principle from Chapter 7 most directly affects the court's likely analysis?

A. The "reasonable efforts" standard is a fixed checklist of safeguards that, once satisfied, protects all information indefinitely.
B. The "reasonable efforts" standard is context-dependent and dynamic, requiring continuous adaptation to new legal, business, and technological realities.
C. The "reasonable efforts" standard can be satisfied by technology alone, without the need for contractual or organizational support.
D. The "reasonable efforts" standard places the burden on competitors to prove they acted reasonably in protecting their own information.

### 2. Options
A. The "reasonable efforts" standard is a fixed checklist of safeguards that, once satisfied, protects all information indefinitely.
B. The "reasonable efforts" standard is context-dependent and dynamic, requiring continuous adaptation to new legal, business, and technological realities.
C. The "reasonable efforts" standard can be satisfied by technology alone, without the need for contractual or organizational support.
D. The "reasonable efforts" standard places the burden on competitors to prove they acted reasonably in protecting their own information.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** This answer reflects a misunderstanding of the "reasonable efforts" standard. Chapter 7 explicitly states that the UTSA "requires 'reasonable efforts' to maintain secrecy—not just at the outset, but continuously over time."
