However, some of the data—such as environmental hazard predictions and industrial emissions information—could inform public policy decisions on climate regulation and environmental protection. Environmental advocates and regulators argue that ClimateData's secrecy claim should be scrutinized because it may undermine transparency on matters of public interest. According to Chapter 7's discussion of the "information paradox" and Levine and Sarnoff's work, what is the key tension?

A. Trade secret protection should be absolute; public interest in transparency is never a valid reason to override secrecy.
B. Overbroad trade secret protection can create an "information paradox" where firms assert secrecy over information that should be disclosed for public health, environmental, or transparency purposes, thereby undermining accountability.
C. All information should be public; trade secret protection should be eliminated entirely.
D. The information paradox only applies to government information, not private commercial secrets.

### 2. Options
A. Trade secret protection should be absolute; public interest in transparency is never a valid reason to override secrecy.
B. Overbroad trade secret protection can create an "information paradox" where firms assert secrecy over information that should be disclosed for public health, environmental, or transparency purposes, thereby undermining accountability.
C. All information should be public; trade secret protection should be eliminated entirely.
D. The information paradox only applies to government information, not private commercial secrets.

### 3. Correct Answer
B

### 4. Answer-Specific Feedback
A. **Incorrect.** Chapter 7 explicitly rejects the view that trade secret protection is always absolute. The Chapter discusses the tension between secrecy and public interest, and notes that courts are increasingly attentive to this tension.

B. **Correct.** This answer reflects the core teaching of Chapter 7's discussion of the information paradox, drawing on Levine and Sarnoff. The Chapter states: "David Levine and Joshua Sarnoff argue that overbroad trade secret protection can create an 'information paradox': firms may assert secrecy over information that should be disclosed—such as public health data or algorithmic bias—thereby undermining transparency, competition, and accountability." The Chapter further emphasizes: "A living TSPP must walk this line: strong enough to protect valuable assets, but mindful of the public-facing risks of overreach." ClimateData's blanket claim of secrecy over environmental hazard data and emissions information exemplifies the risk Levine and Sarnoff identify. The Chapter also notes: "Courts are increasingly scrutinizing secrecy claims in cases involving public health, environmental hazards, and AI-driven decisions—illustrating how this theoretical debate is shaping real-world doctrine."

C. **Incorrect.** Chapter 7 does not argue for elimination of trade secret protection. Instead, it advocates for thoughtful calibration: protecting genuine competitive secrets while recognizing that secrecy claims may sometimes conflict with public interest.

D. **Incorrect.** The Chapter explicitly discusses "public health, environmental hazards, and AI-driven decisions" as areas where courts scrutinize private trade secret claims. The paradox is not limited to government; it affects private firms that assert secrecy over information with broader societal implications.

***

PART II — COURSE MCQs

# CHAPTER 2 – IDENTIFICATION OF TRADE SECRETS

---

## **Q2.01 — Defining Information**

A small robotics firm develops a proprietary motion-control routine for its warehouse bots. The routine involves a series of timing adjustments and sensor-weighting parameters stored in a private Git repository. A competitor argues that “software logic” cannot qualify as a trade secret because it reflects only the engineers’ accumulated skills and experience. Under UTSA/DTSA, which statement best describes the legal status of the routine?

A. It cannot be a trade secret because employee expertise, even when written down, is categorically excluded from protection.
B. It may be a trade secret because software routines can constitute protectable “information” when reduced to specific, concrete parameters.
C. It cannot be a trade secret because the routine was not patented and thus lacks the formal disclosure necessary for protection.
D. It may be a trade secret only if the engineers can show that they personally authored every component without referencing public sources.

---

## **Q2.02 — Not Generally Known**

A beverage startup sells a unique sparkling tea with a distinctive flavor. Its formulation uses publicly available ingredients but in unusual proportions. The company reveals the ingredient list on the label, as required by regulation, but keeps the proportions confidential. A rival chemist claims this information cannot be a trade secret because anyone can “figure it out” by tasting the drink. Which statement is most accurate?

A. The formulation cannot be a trade secret because regulatory disclosure of ingredients always destroys secrecy.
B. The formulation may be a trade secret if the undisclosed proportions are not generally known in the industry.
C. The formulation cannot be a trade secret because beverages are inherently reverse engineerable.
D. The formulation may be a trade secret only if the company proves that no competitor has ever produced a similar tea.

---

## **Q2.03 — Independent Economic Value**

A pharmaceutical company generates an internal library of failed drug formulations documenting which molecular variations did *not* yield viable results. Competitors lack this data. The company claims these “dead ends” are trade secrets. Which analysis is correct?

A. Negative research results may have independent economic value because they save competitors time and cost.
B. Negative research results lack economic value because they do not produce a working product.
C. Negative research results cannot be protected unless the company simultaneously patents the successful formulations.
D. Negative research results are unprotectable because they cannot be used commercially.

---

## **Q2.04 — Reasonable Efforts**

A data-analytics firm maintains a confidential algorithm accessible to 35 employees through a password-protected intranet. The company requires NDAs, conducts annual confidentiality trainings, and logs attempts to access the restricted folder. One engineer keeps a personal printout on her desk. Which statement best captures the legal effect?

A. The presence of a single printed copy automatically destroys reasonable efforts.
B. The firm’s practices may constitute reasonable efforts despite an isolated lapse.
C. The firm cannot claim trade secret status because more than 10 employees have access.
D. The firm must encrypt the data at rest; without encryption no reasonable efforts can exist.

---

## **Q2.05 — Readily Ascertainable**

A competitor purchases a patented 3D printer and attempts to deduce the printer’s proprietary calibration sequence through trial-and-error testing. The sequence is not disclosed in the patent. After several months of intensive testing, the competitor identifies a sequence similar to the manufacturer’s. Is the original calibration sequence “readily ascertainable”?

A. Yes, because any information that can be discovered through testing is readily ascertainable.
B. Yes, because the competitor ultimately succeeded in discovering the sequence.
C. No, because the required effort exceeded what is considered routine or trivial.
D. No, because patented devices can never be reverse engineered.

---

## **Q2.06 — Compilations**

An e-commerce company curates a database combining third-party demographic datasets with its own proprietary engagement metrics. Each component dataset is publicly available, but their integration required months of modeling. A departing analyst claims the database cannot be a trade secret because “all the data is public.” Which response is most accurate?

A. The compilation may be a trade secret because the combination can have independent value even if the components are public.
B. The compilation cannot be a trade secret because customers expect transparency.
C. The compilation may be a trade secret only if the firm can prove customers relied on it.
D. The compilation cannot be a trade secret because any analyst could recreate it using off-the-shelf software.

---

## **Q2.07 — Skill vs. Secret**

A senior engineer leaves a semiconductor manufacturer and begins working for a competitor. She uses her understanding of typical doping ranges and conventional heat-profile settings she learned over years in the industry. She also uses a confidential, proprietary temperature curve the former employer taught her last year. Which statement is correct?

A. Both the general experience and the proprietary temperature curve are freely usable.
B. Neither the general experience nor the proprietary temperature curve are usable.
C. Only the general experience is freely usable; the proprietary temperature curve may be a trade secret.
D. Both are prohibited because any information learned at a prior job is a trade secret.

---

## **Q2.08 — Case Application: Christopher**

A battery manufacturer builds a new plant. During construction, roofless sections expose the interior process layout. A drone hobbyist, acting at the request of an undisclosed client, flies over the site and captures photos revealing the manufacturing line. The company sues for misappropriation. Under trade secret law, which statement is most accurate?

A. Aerial photography cannot constitute improper means because the drone remained in public airspace.
B. The hobbyist’s conduct may constitute improper means if it defeats reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy.
C. Aerial photography is always permissible because there is no trespass.
D. The manufacturer cannot claim secrecy because any building under construction is at risk of exposure.

---

## **Q2.09 — Case Application: Kolon**

A company sues a competitor for trade secret misappropriation. After learning of the lawsuit, several employees at the competitor delete thousands of files, including emails referencing confidential materials. Forensic tools recover only fragments. In establishing reasonable efforts and secrecy, which statement is most accurate?

A. The deletions are irrelevant to whether the plaintiff had a trade secret.
B. The deletions may support an inference of improper conduct and bolster the plaintiff’s secrecy claim.
C. The deletions automatically destroy the trade secret.
D. The deletions matter only if the plaintiff proves a breach of contract.

---

## **Q2.10 — Case Application: Altavion**

A tech startup pitches an identity-verification system that embeds secure watermarks into PDF files. The general concept of “watermarking for authentication” is widely known. The startup alleges misappropriation when a partner replicates the system after seeing detailed specifications. Which statement is correct?

A. Broad ideas like “watermarking for authentication” are protectable trade secrets.
B. Only the specific technical implementation may qualify as trade secret information.
C. No part of the system can be a trade secret because authentication is a common industry problem.
D. The concept is patentable, not protectable by trade secret law.

---

## **Q2.11 — Case Application: Peabody**

An inventor shares a proprietary textile-weaving method with a manufacturing partner under a confidentiality agreement. The partner attempts to commercialize the method independently. To establish existence of a trade secret, which fact is most important?

A. Whether the inventor intended to file a patent.
B. Whether the method was disclosed under a relationship of trust.
C. Whether the partner improved the method.
D. Whether the final textile is unique in the marketplace.

---

## **Q2.12 — Case Application: Vickery**

A chocolatier sells his small factory and transfers “the entire secret method” for producing a signature chocolate. After the sale, he resumes selling chocolate made with the same method. The buyer claims misappropriation. For trade secret identification, which matter is central?

A. Whether the method was sufficiently memorialized in writing before the sale.
B. Whether the method was exclusive and intended to transfer as a secret asset.
C. Whether the seller later improved the recipe.
D. Whether the seller filed a trademark for the chocolate’s name.

---

## **Q2.13 — Sector Application: AI Model Weights**

A machine-learning lab trains a large-parameter model and keeps the weight matrices accessible only on an air-gapped server. An employee argues that “numbers cannot be trade secrets.” Which statement best reflects the law?

A. Model weights cannot be trade secrets because they are purely mathematical.
B. Model weights may be trade secrets when they reflect non-public training, tuning, and optimization choices.
C. Model weights cannot be trade secrets because any model can be retrained from scratch.
D. Model weights may be trade secrets only if they are used to generate copyrighted output.

---

## **Q2.14 — Sector Application: Training Data**

A company compiles 800,000 hours of annotated audio used for training its voice-recognition models. Most audio files were scraped from public sources, but the annotations and filtering process were proprietary. More than 200 contractors worked on the dataset. Which statement is correct?

A. The dataset is not a trade secret because contractors worked on it.
B. The dataset may be a trade secret if the proprietary annotations and curation have economic value and secrecy.
C. The dataset cannot be a trade secret because the source audio is public.
D. The dataset is automatically a trade secret because it is expensive to produce.

---

## **Q2.15 — Sector Application: Customer Analytics**

A fashion retailer creates a model predicting customer churn using public demographic data combined with internal purchase histories. A former employee downloads the entire dataset before resigning. For identification purposes, which element is most contested?

A. Whether the information qualifies as “information.”
B. Whether the dataset is novel in the patent sense.
C. Whether the integrated dataset is not generally known or readily ascertainable.
D. Whether the company owns the copyright in the model.

---

## **Q2.16 — Sector Application: Biotechnology**

A biotech firm stores a unique cell-culture protocol on a shared internal drive without folder-level permissions. Ten lab assistants and janitorial staff can access the folder. The company claims trade secret protection. Which issue is most problematic?

A. Whether cell-culture protocols count as “methods.”
B. Whether open internal access undercuts reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy.
C. Whether janitorial staff are bound by employment contracts.
D. Whether the protocol is scientifically valid.

---

## **Q2.17 — Sector Application: Semiconductor Manufacturing**

A fabrication plant uses a proprietary doping recipe that is difficult to infer from the finished chip. During a tour, the company allows a supplier to observe monitoring screens showing partial recipe parameters. Which statement is most accurate?

A. Partial disclosure under confidentiality-free circumstances may jeopardize secrecy.
B. Any in-facility disclosure automatically destroys secrecy.
C. Partial disclosure cannot affect secrecy because competitors still lack key steps.
D. The doping recipe cannot be a trade secret because chip fabrication is complex.

---

## **Q2.18 — Organizational Identification: Overclaiming**

An employer labels nearly every internal document—from cafeteria menus to product plans—as “TRADE SECRET — CONFIDENTIAL.” A fired employee argues that no genuine secrets exist because the company “over-claimed.” For identification, which statement is correct?

A. Over-claiming automatically destroys all trade secret protection.
B. Over-claiming may undermine credibility but does not eliminate legitimately secret information.
C. Over-claiming creates new trade secrets by contract.
D. Over-claiming proves that the employer failed to mark documents properly.

---

## **Q2.19 — Organizational Identification: Necessary Disclosure**

A startup must share proprietary sourcing spreadsheets with suppliers to finalize pricing. The spreadsheets contain cost formulas and margin targets. The startup uses NDAs but cannot avoid disclosure. Which statement best captures identification principles?

A. Necessary disclosure automatically eliminates trade secret potential.
B. Necessary disclosure may preserve secrecy if accompanied by reasonable confidentiality protections.
C. Necessary disclosure proves the information lacks economic value.
D. Necessary disclosure requires public filing before protection attaches.

---

## **Q2.20 — Litigation Identification: Specificity Requirement**

In litigation, a plaintiff alleges misappropriation of a “confidential operational playbook.” The defendant moves to dismiss, arguing the plaintiff has not described the alleged trade secret with sufficient detail. Which statement reflects correct doctrine?

A. Plaintiffs must identify trade secrets with reasonable particularity to proceed.
B. Plaintiffs may describe trade secrets in extremely broad categories until discovery ends.
C. Plaintiffs need not identify any details if the defendant “knows what it stole.”
D. Plaintiffs must publicly disclose the full trade secret before discovery.

---

## **Q2.21 — Litigation Identification: Multiple Secrets**

A tech firm alleges that a former employee misappropriated “at least forty” trade secrets but refuses to narrow the list. The employee seeks a more definite statement. Under trade secret identification principles, which result is most appropriate?

A. The firm may proceed with all forty alleged secrets without identifying them individually.
B. The firm may be required to identify each alleged secret with sufficient detail to allow a fair defense.
C. The firm may refuse to identify any trade secrets until after trial.
D. The firm may identify only categories (e.g., “software,” “processes”) without specifying content.

---

# **CHAPTER 2 — IDENTIFICATION OF TRADE SECRETS**

## **Answer Key + Detailed Explanations**

---

## **Q2.01 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Software routines, when reduced to concrete logic, parameters, or code, fall squarely within “information” under UTSA/DTSA. The competitor’s argument improperly conflates unprotectable *general skill* with *specific implementations*. Courts consistently protect software logic, provided it is concrete and confidential.

**A is wrong** because employee know-how is distinct from fixed, proprietary routines.
**C is wrong** because patents are optional; trade secret protection does not require any registration.
**D is wrong** because using public sources does not defeat secrecy; what matters is the *nonpublic integration*.

---

## **Q2.02 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Even when ingredients are public, *proportions* can remain not generally known. Controlled disclosure on labels does not require disclosure of operative ratios. Trade secrets often reside in quantitative relationships.

**A is wrong** because regulatory disclosure of ingredients does not mandate disclosure of methods or proportions.
**C is wrong** because beverages are routinely protected through secrecy where reverse engineering is difficult.
**D is wrong** because the test is not whether competitors have *ever* made a similar product.

---

## **Q2.03 — Correct Answer: A**

**Why A is correct:**
Negative know-how has economic value because it prevents costly attempts to replicate failed experiments. Courts routinely treat such information as protectable “negative research.” Its value arises precisely *because* competitors lack it.

**B is wrong** because value is not limited to commercially successful outputs.
**C is wrong** because patenting is irrelevant to trade secret status.
**D is wrong** because commercial usability is not required for the “value” prong.

---

## **Q2.04 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Reasonable efforts are evaluated holistically. A single careless printout does not defeat systematic secrecy protections. Courts look for *overall adequacy*, not perfection.

**A is wrong** because reasonable efforts do **not** require absolute control.
**C is wrong** because the number of employees with access is not determinative.
**D is wrong** because encryption is a best practice, not a universal legal requirement.

---

## **Q2.05 — Correct Answer: C**

**Why C is correct:**
“Readily ascertainable” means discoverable through *routine or trivial means*. Months of intensive experimentation exceeds that threshold. Reverse engineering is permitted, but difficulty matters for the ascertainability analysis.

**A is wrong** because any discoverability ≠ *readily* ascertainable.
**B is wrong** because the fact of eventual discovery does not make it “readily” obtainable.
**D is wrong** because patented devices *can* be reverse engineered except where legally restricted.

---

## **Q2.06 — Correct Answer: A**

**Why A is correct:**
A compilation may be a trade secret even if individual components are public, so long as the *integration* is non-public and economically valuable. See compilation doctrine.

**B is wrong** — consumer expectations have no relevance to secrecy.
**C is wrong** — reliance by customers is not required.
**D is wrong** — the question is feasibility, not whether someone *could* recreate it with enough effort.

---

## **Q2.07 — Correct Answer: C**

**Why C is correct:**
General industry experience is free for employees to use. However, a *specific proprietary temperature curve* imparted under confidentiality is protectable.

**A is wrong** because confidential data is not freely usable.
**B is wrong** because general skill cannot be restrained.
**D is wrong** because not all information obtained through employment is a trade secret.

---

## **Q2.08 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Aerial surveillance can be improper means when it circumvents reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy. *Christopher* held that improper means extend beyond trespass and include surreptitious methods undermining secrecy efforts.


**A and C are wrong** because public airspace does not automatically sanitize conduct.
**D is wrong** because construction visibility does not destroy secrecy by itself.

---

## **Q2.09 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Deliberate destruction of potentially incriminating documents supports an inference of improper conduct and bolsters the plaintiff’s claim that secrets existed and were mishandled. *Kolon* treated post-litigation deletion as highly probative of misappropriation and consciousness of wrongdoing.


**A is wrong** — spoliation is highly relevant.
**C is wrong** — deletion does not destroy the underlying secret.
**D is wrong** — breach of contract is unnecessary for misappropriation.

---

## **Q2.10 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
*Altavion* distinguishes *ideas* from *implementations*. Broad concepts (e.g., “authentication via watermarking”) are unprotectable. Detailed implementations can be trade secrets.

**A is wrong** — general ideas cannot be trade secrets.
**C is wrong** — common industry problems can still host trade-secret-level implementations.
**D is wrong** — trade secrets and patents are independent routes.

---

## **Q2.11 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Peabody emphasizes that confidential disclosure within a relationship of trust is central to establishing trade secret protection. Breach of that trust constitutes misappropriation.

**A is wrong** — intent to patent is irrelevant.
**C is wrong** — improvement does not negate wrongdoing.
**D is wrong** — market uniqueness is not required.

---

## **Q2.12 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
*Vickery* treats the sale of a “secret method” as conveying exclusive rights. Whether exclusivity was intended—and thus whether a “secret asset” was transferred—is central to trade secret identification.

**A is wrong** — writing is not required.
**C is wrong** — improvements do not change the nature of the original transfer.
**D is wrong** — trademarks are irrelevant.

---

## **Q2.13 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Model weights reflect proprietary training choices, data curation, and optimization—all classic trade secret attributes when kept confidential.

**A is wrong** — mathematics can embody protectable configurations.
**C is wrong** — retrainability does not negate secrecy.
**D is wrong** — copyright has no bearing on trade secret status.

---

## **Q2.14 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Trade secret protection can attach to proprietary annotations, filtering choices, and curation processes even if underlying raw data is public, provided secrecy and economic value exist.

**A is wrong** — contractors do not destroy secrecy when NDAs or controls exist.
**C is wrong** — public raw sources do not negate protection for the *enhanced dataset*.
**D is wrong** — expense alone does not prove trade secret status.

---

## **Q2.15 — Correct Answer: C**

**Why C is correct:**
The most contested element is whether the *integrated dataset* is not generally known or readily ascertainable. Customer analytics models often combine public data with proprietary information that creates secrecy value.

**A is wrong** — it qualifies as information.
**B is wrong** — novelty is a patent concept.
**D is wrong** — copyright does not determine trade secret status.

---

## **Q2.16 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Allowing broad internal access without permissions undermines “reasonable efforts.” Courts hold that unguarded access—even internally—can defeat secrecy.

**A is wrong** — protocols are clearly “methods.”
**C is wrong** — janitorial contracts do not solve unbounded access.
**D is wrong** — scientific validity is irrelevant.

---

## **Q2.17 — Correct Answer: A**

**Why A is correct:**
Permitting viewing of sensitive parameters without confidentiality controls can compromise secrecy. Trade secret law does not require full disclosure to lose secrecy; partial exposure may suffice.

**B is wrong** — not all internal disclosures destroy secrecy.
**C is wrong** — partial disclosure *can* affect secrecy.
**D is wrong** — complexity does not bar trade secret protection.

---

## **Q2.18 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Over-claiming weakens credibility but does not eliminate legitimate secrets. Courts evaluate secrecy based on substance, not labels.

**A is wrong** — over-claiming does not eliminate genuine secrets.
**C is wrong** — labeling does not create trade secrets.
**D is wrong** — marking errors do not determine secrecy by themselves.

---

## **Q2.19 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
Trade secrets often require sharing with partners. Secrecy survives if reasonable protective measures (e.g., NDAs) accompany disclosure.

**A is wrong** — necessary disclosure does not automatically destroy secrecy.
**C is wrong** — disclosures often indicate *high* value.
**D is wrong** — no public filing is ever required.

---

## **Q2.20 — Correct Answer: A**

**Why A is correct:**
Courts require identification with “reasonable particularity” to allow discovery and defense preparation. Vague references to “playbooks” are insufficient.

**B is wrong** — overbroad allegations are not accepted until late discovery.
**C is wrong** — courts reject “the defendant knows what it stole.”
**D is wrong** — full public disclosure is not required.

---

## **Q2.21 — Correct Answer: B**

**Why B is correct:**
A plaintiff asserting multiple trade secrets must identify each one with sufficient detail to permit a fair defense. Courts routinely compel specificity.

**A is wrong** — plaintiffs cannot proceed with a vague list.
**C is wrong** — secrets cannot be withheld until after trial.
**D is wrong** — categories alone are insufficient.

---

---
---

# PART III — CANVAS ITEM BANK

> Source: `PTS_MCQs.txt`
> 
> 75 questions across Modules 1–7. Each question includes a Question Name,
> four answer choices, the correct answer, detailed per-answer feedback,
> Bloom's taxonomy level, and module/lecture mapping.

---

# **MODULE 1: FOUNDATIONS - CANVAS ITEM BANK QUESTIONS**

+++

## **QUESTION 1**

**Question Name:** M1-Reasonable-Efforts-Standard

**Question Text:**  
Under the UTSA, what is the standard for determining whether a plaintiff has taken "reasonable efforts" to maintain secrecy?

**Answer A:**  
Absolute secrecy with zero unauthorized disclosures

**Answer B:**  
Efforts that are appropriate and proportionate to the context

**Answer C:**  
Security measures comparable to those used by competitors

**Answer D:**  
Formal registration with a state trade secret registry

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Trade secret law does not require absolute or perfect secrecy. Courts recognize that some limited, controlled disclosure (e.g., to employees under NDAs) is often necessary for business operations. The standard is reasonableness, not perfection.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Excellent! The UTSA requires "reasonable efforts" to maintain secrecy, which courts interpret as measures that are appropriate and proportionate to the context—considering the value of the secret, the size of the organization, and industry norms. This flexible standard rewards diligence, not perfection.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
While industry practices may inform what courts consider reasonable, the standard is not strictly comparative. A company's efforts are evaluated based on its own circumstances—the value of the information, available resources, and the nature of the threat. Simply matching competitors' measures may not be sufficient if those measures are themselves inadequate.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Unlike patents or trademarks, trade secrets do not require registration with any government agency. Protection arises from the owner's efforts to maintain secrecy, not from formal filing or certification. There is no "trade secret registry."

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1A

+++

## **QUESTION 2**

**Question Name:** M1-Three-Essential-Elements

**Question Text:**  
Which of the following is NOT one of the three essential elements required for information to qualify as a trade secret under the UTSA?

**Answer A:**  
The information must derive independent economic value from not being generally known

**Answer B:**  
The information must be subject to reasonable efforts to maintain its secrecy

**Answer C:**  
The information must be patentable or capable of patent protection

**Answer D:**  
The information must qualify as protectable subject matter (information)

**Correct Answer:** C

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
This is actually one of the three essential elements. Information must provide competitive advantage specifically because it is kept secret—not because it is inherently useful but widely known.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
This is the third essential element of trade secret protection. The owner must actively maintain secrecy through measures like NDAs, access controls, and training. Without ongoing efforts, there is no protection.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Correct! Trade secrets do not need to be patentable. In fact, one key strategic choice businesses face is whether to seek patent protection (which requires public disclosure) or maintain secrecy. Many trade secrets—like customer lists, pricing strategies, or negative R&D data—are not patentable at all. Trade secret law and patent law serve complementary but distinct purposes.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
This is the first essential element. The subject matter must qualify as "information"—formulas, patterns, methods, processes, techniques, or compilations. Abstract ideas, general employee skill, or physical objects themselves do not qualify.

**Bloom's Level:** Knowledge  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1A

+++

## **QUESTION 3**

**Question Name:** M1-DuPont-v-Masland-Principle

**Question Text:**  
In E.I. du Pont de Nemours Powder Co. v. Masland (1917), Justice Holmes emphasized that trade secret protection is fundamentally about:

**Answer A:**  
Property rights in intangible information

**Answer B:**  
Breach of a duty of confidence or trust

**Answer C:**  
Preventing all forms of competitive imitation

**Answer D:**  
Rewarding innovation through government-granted monopolies

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Justice Holmes explicitly declined to label trade secrets as "property" in the strict sense. While some modern courts use property-like language, the historical foundation of trade secret law rests on equity and breach of confidence, not property rights.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly right! Justice Holmes wrote that the wrong at issue is the breach of trust—disclosing or using information given in confidence. This principle remains central to trade secret law: it punishes betrayal and improper acquisition, not independent discovery or reverse engineering.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Trade secret law does not prevent all competitive imitation. Reverse engineering and independent discovery are lawful. Protection applies only when someone acquires, uses, or discloses secrets through improper means or breach of duty.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
This describes patent law, not trade secret law. Patents are government-granted monopolies that reward public disclosure with time-limited exclusivity. Trade secrets require no government action and depend entirely on the owner's ongoing efforts to maintain secrecy.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1A

+++

## **QUESTION 4**

**Question Name:** M1-Perpetual-Duration-Rationale

**Question Text:**  
Which of the following best explains why trade secrets can potentially last forever, unlike patents?

**Answer A:**  
Trade secrets are automatically protected by the government once created

**Answer B:**  
Trade secrets do not require disclosure, and protection continues as long as secrecy is maintained

**Answer C:**  
Trade secrets are more valuable than patents and therefore receive stronger legal protection

**Answer D:**  
Trade secrets cannot be reverse-engineered, ensuring perpetual exclusivity

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Trade secrets are not automatically protected by the government. There is no registration process, no examination, and no official certificate. Protection depends entirely on the owner's ongoing efforts to maintain secrecy through reasonable measures.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! Unlike patents, which expire after a fixed term (typically 20 years), trade secrets can last indefinitely—as long as the information remains secret, derives economic value from secrecy, and the owner continues to protect it with reasonable efforts. The Coca-Cola formula, for example, has been protected for over a century.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Trade secrets are not inherently more valuable than patents, nor do they receive "stronger" protection. In fact, trade secret protection is narrower: it does not prevent independent discovery or reverse engineering, whereas patents block even innocent third parties. The strategic choice between patents and trade secrets depends on the nature of the innovation and business goals.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Trade secrets can be reverse-engineered lawfully. If a competitor purchases a product on the open market and figures out how it works through legitimate analysis, they are free to use that knowledge. Trade secret law only prevents improper acquisition—theft, breach of confidence, espionage—not lawful discovery.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1B

+++

## **QUESTION 5**

**Question Name:** M1-Kewanee-Oil-Holding

**Question Text:**  
In Kewanee Oil Co. v. Bicron Corp. (1974), the U.S. Supreme Court held that:

**Answer A:**  
State trade secret law is preempted by federal patent law

**Answer B:**  
Trade secret protection and patent protection can coexist without conflict

**Answer C:**  
Companies must choose between patent protection and trade secret protection

**Answer D:**  
Trade secrets are unconstitutional because they undermine patent policy

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
The Supreme Court ruled the opposite: state trade secret law is not preempted by federal patent law. The two regimes serve different purposes and can operate side by side.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly! The Court held that trade secret protection does not conflict with patent policy because patents require public disclosure in exchange for exclusivity, while trade secrets depend on keeping information hidden. Each path advances innovation in different ways, and businesses can choose which route (or combination) best suits their needs.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Companies do not have to choose exclusively. In fact, many businesses use both: they patent core inventions while keeping refinements, negative data, manufacturing optimizations, or unpatentable aspects as trade secrets. The two forms of protection are complementary, not mutually exclusive.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
The Court explicitly rejected the argument that trade secrets undermine patent policy or are unconstitutional. Trade secret law operates alongside patent law without conflict, and both promote innovation through different mechanisms.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1B

+++

## **QUESTION 6**

**Question Name:** M1-DTSA-Unique-Feature

**Question Text:**  
The Defend Trade Secrets Act (DTSA), enacted in 2016, provides which of the following that the UTSA typically does not?

**Answer A:**  
A federal civil cause of action for trade secret misappropriation

**Answer B:**  
Criminal penalties for willful misappropriation

**Answer C:**  
The right to seek injunctive relief

**Answer D:**  
Protection for customer lists and business strategies

**Correct Answer:** A

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Correct! The DTSA created a federal civil cause of action for trade secret misappropriation, allowing plaintiffs to sue in federal court. This is distinct from the UTSA, which operates at the state level. The DTSA also provides for ex parte seizure in extraordinary circumstances and expressly addresses whistleblower immunity.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Criminal penalties for trade secret theft were available before the DTSA through the Economic Espionage Act (EEA), enacted in 1996. The DTSA added a civil remedy, not criminal enforcement. The EEA provides criminal sanctions under 18 U.S.C. §§ 1831-1839.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Both the UTSA and the DTSA authorize injunctive relief. The ability to seek injunctions to stop or prevent misappropriation is a core remedy under both statutory frameworks. This is not a distinguishing feature of the DTSA.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Both the UTSA and the DTSA protect a wide range of trade secrets, including customer lists, business strategies, formulas, methods, and processes. The types of protectable information are similar under both statutes. What the DTSA added was federal jurisdiction and procedure, not a broader definition of protectable information.

**Bloom's Level:** Knowledge  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1A

+++

## **QUESTION 7**

**Question Name:** M1-Improper-Means-Example

**Question Text:**  
Which of the following scenarios best illustrates "improper means" of acquiring a trade secret under the UTSA?

**Answer A:**  
A competitor purchases your product and reverse-engineers it to understand the design

**Answer B:**  
A competitor hires your former employee, who uses general industry knowledge learned on the job

**Answer C:**  
A competitor bribes your current employee to provide confidential manufacturing specifications

**Answer D:**  
A competitor independently develops the same formula through its own R&D efforts

**Correct Answer:** C

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Reverse engineering a lawfully purchased product is generally considered a proper means of discovery under trade secret law. If the information can be learned through legitimate analysis of a publicly sold product, it is not protected as a trade secret—unless contractual restrictions (like a license agreement) prohibit reverse engineering.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Employees are generally free to use general skills, knowledge, and experience gained on the job, even if they move to a competitor. Trade secret law does not prevent mobility based on accumulated expertise—only the misappropriation of specific, protectable confidential information.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Correct! Bribery is a classic example of improper means. Paying an insider to breach their duty of confidentiality and steal trade secrets violates both the law and commercial ethics. The UTSA explicitly prohibits acquisition through improper means, including bribery, theft, misrepresentation, and espionage.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Independent development is a proper means of acquiring the same knowledge. If a competitor arrives at the same formula, process, or method through its own research without using your trade secrets, they have every right to use it. Trade secret law does not grant monopoly rights over information that others can discover independently.

**Bloom's Level:** Application  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1C

+++

## **QUESTION 8**

**Question Name:** M1-Peabody-v-Norfolk-Principle

**Question Text:**  
In Peabody v. Norfolk (1868), the Massachusetts court held that:

**Answer A:**  
Secret processes lose protection if not patented within a certain timeframe

**Answer B:**  
Keeping a process hidden rather than patenting it does not forfeit legal protection

**Answer C:**  
Employees have no duty to maintain confidentiality after leaving employment

**Answer D:**  
Trade secret protection requires formal registration with state authorities

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
The court did not impose any time limit on trade secret protection based on failure to patent. In fact, the ruling recognized that choosing secrecy over patenting is a legitimate strategic choice, and protection continues as long as secrecy is maintained.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly! The court ruled that Peabody's decision to keep his jute-manufacturing process secret instead of patenting it did not forfeit his rights. Equity would protect such "hidden know-how" as property, especially when disclosed to others under a duty of confidence. This case helped establish the principle that trade secrecy is a valid alternative to patent protection.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
On the contrary, the court emphasized that employees and associates who gain knowledge under a duty of confidence cannot lawfully disclose or profit from it after leaving employment. Duties of confidentiality can survive the end of the employment relationship, especially when contractually imposed.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Trade secrets have never required formal registration. This 1868 case predates modern statutory frameworks like the UTSA, but the principle remains: protection arises from the owner's efforts to maintain secrecy and from the breach of a confidential relationship, not from any government filing.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1A

+++

## **QUESTION 9**

**Question Name:** M1-In-Rem-vs-In-Personam

**Question Text:**  
What is the primary difference between "in rem" rights (created by trade secret law) and "in personam" rights (created by contracts)?

**Answer A:**  
In rem rights bind only the parties who signed the agreement; in personam rights bind everyone

**Answer B:**  
In rem rights bind anyone who misappropriates the information; in personam rights bind only specific contracting parties

**Answer C:**  
In rem rights require government registration; in personam rights do not

**Answer D:**  
In rem rights expire after 20 years; in personam rights last indefinitely

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
You have the concepts reversed. "In personam" rights (from contracts) bind only the specific parties to the contract. "In rem" rights (from trade secret law) can bind third parties who improperly acquire or use the secret, even without a direct contractual relationship.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly! Trade secret law creates quasi-property ("in rem") rights that can be enforced against anyone who misappropriates the information—through theft, breach of confidence, or improper means—even if they never signed a contract. Contracts create "in personam" rights that bind only the parties who agreed to them. This distinction explains why trade secret law adds essential value in "sparse networks" where parties transact once and third parties may receive secrets without privity.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Neither in rem nor in personam rights in the trade secret context require government registration. Trade secrets are protected through the owner's reasonable efforts, not through formal filing. Patents and trademarks require registration, but trade secrets do not.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Trade secret rights (in rem) can last indefinitely as long as secrecy is maintained—there is no fixed expiration. Patents expire after 20 years, but trade secrets do not. Contract rights (in personam) last as long as the contract specifies or until obligations are fulfilled. The durability of rights is not what distinguishes in rem from in personam.

**Bloom's Level:** Analysis  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1C

+++

## **QUESTION 10**

**Question Name:** M1-Trade-Secrets-vs-Patents

**Question Text:**  
Which of the following statements about trade secrets and patents is most accurate?

**Answer A:**  
Trade secrets and patents protect identical types of information using different legal mechanisms

**Answer B:**  
Patents require public disclosure and provide time-limited exclusivity; trade secrets require secrecy and can last indefinitely

**Answer C:**  
Patents are always preferable to trade secrets because they provide stronger protection

**Answer D:**  
Once a company patents an invention, it cannot maintain any related information as a trade secret

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
While there is some overlap, trade secrets and patents protect different types of advantages. Patents protect inventions that meet novelty, non-obviousness, and utility standards, and require disclosure. Trade secrets protect any valuable information (including non-patentable business data, customer lists, or negative R&D results) as long as it remains secret and provides competitive advantage.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! This captures the fundamental trade-off: patents demand disclosure in exchange for a government-granted monopoly lasting approximately 20 years, while trade secrets require continuous secrecy but can last forever if maintained. The choice between them depends on factors like ease of reverse engineering, patentability, and business strategy.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Neither form of protection is inherently superior—it depends on context. Patents provide stronger exclusivity (blocking even independent discovery) but require disclosure and expire. Trade secrets avoid disclosure and can last indefinitely, but offer no protection against reverse engineering or independent development. Companies often use both strategically.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Companies frequently combine patent and trade secret protection. They may patent core aspects of an invention while keeping manufacturing optimizations, negative data, or unpatentable refinements as trade secrets. Care must be taken to avoid disclosing trade secrets in patent filings, but the two forms of IP can coexist.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 1 (Foundations)  
**Lecture:** 1B

+++

## **QUESTION 11: MODULE 1 - ANALYSIS**

**Question Name:** M1-Patent-vs-TradeSecret-Strategic-Evaluation

**Question Type:** Multiple Choice

**Bloom's Taxonomy Level:** Analysis (Level 4)

**Module:** Module 1 - Foundations

**Related Lectures:** Lecture 1A, Lecture 1B

**Difficulty:** Hard

**Question Text:**

A biotech startup has developed a novel fermentation process that reduces production costs by 40%. The process involves a unique sequence of temperature adjustments and ingredient timing that took 3 years to perfect. The founder must decide between patent protection and trade secret protection. Evaluate the following factors. Which consideration most strongly favors trade secret protection over patent protection?

**Answer A:**

The process is likely to be reverse-engineered by competitors within 2-3 years once products reach the market.

**Answer B:**

The company plans to license the technology to multiple manufacturers within 5 years to scale production rapidly.

**Answer C:**

Documenting the precise temperature sequences and timing would be extremely difficult in a patent application without revealing the critical "know-how."

**Answer D:**

The company operates in a highly regulated industry where FDA approval processes will publicly disclose manufacturing methods.

**Correct Answer:** C

**Feedback for Answer A:**

If competitors can easily reverse-engineer the process within 2-3 years, trade secret protection becomes *weaker*, not stronger. Patent protection would provide 20 years of exclusive rights even after reverse engineering. Trade secrets only protect against *improper* acquisition—reverse engineering is permitted. This factor favors patents.

**Feedback for Answer B:**

Licensing plans favor patent protection, not trade secrets. Patents provide clear legal boundaries for licensing agreements and make infringement easier to detect and prove. Trade secret licensing requires extensive confidentiality provisions and creates ongoing monitoring challenges. Multiple licensees increase disclosure risk.

**Feedback for Answer C:**

Correct! This is the classic "tacit knowledge" advantage of trade secrets. When critical elements involve undocumented expertise, timing intuitions, or sensory judgments that practitioners "just know," patent disclosure requirements can actually weaken protection by forcing companies to articulate what competitors couldn't reverse-engineer. Trade secrets protect this embodied knowledge indefinitely as long as reasonable efforts maintain secrecy.

**Feedback for Answer D:**

Regulatory disclosure requirements that will make the process publicly available strongly favor patents over trade secrets. Once information is publicly disclosed (even involuntarily), trade secret protection is lost. Patent protection would at least provide exclusivity despite public disclosure. This factor weighs against trade secrets.

+++

# **MODULE 2: IDENTIFICATION & INVENTORY - CANVAS ITEM BANK QUESTIONS**

+++

## **QUESTION 1**

**Question Name:** M2-Information-vs-Physical-Objects

**Question Text:**  
Which of the following best explains why trade secret law protects the "method" for making a product but not the product itself?

**Answer A:**  
Physical products are too valuable to qualify for trade secret protection

**Answer B:**  
Trade secret law protects knowledge, not tangible things

**Answer C:**  
Products must be patented before they can receive any legal protection

**Answer D:**  
Trade secret law only applies to information that cannot be seen or touched

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Value is not the issue. In fact, some products are extremely valuable precisely because of the secret methods used to create them. The distinction is not about value but about the type of subject matter—trade secret law covers information (processes, formulas, methods), not physical objects.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! Trade secret law protects knowledge—the "how"—not physical items. A machine is not a trade secret, but the process for building or operating it might be. A product is not a trade secret, but the formula, manufacturing sequence, or design specifications behind it could qualify. This distinction is fundamental to understanding what trade secret law covers.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
This is incorrect. Products do not need to be patented to receive legal protection, and trade secrets operate independently of patent law. Many companies choose trade secrecy specifically to *avoid* the disclosure requirements of patenting. The two forms of IP protection are alternatives, not prerequisites for each other.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Trade secret law does not exclude information simply because it relates to tangible things. Manufacturing processes, for example, involve physical equipment and materials—but the *knowledge* about how to use them (temperature curves, pressure settings, chemical sequences) can be protectable. The key is that the subject matter must be *information*, whether or not it relates to something physical.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 2**

**Question Name:** M2-General-Skill-vs-Trade-Secret

**Question Text:**  
An employee spends five years working for a software company, where she learns advanced debugging techniques, project management skills, and general industry best practices. When she leaves to join a competitor, which of the following is most accurate?

**Answer A:**  
All knowledge gained during employment is protected as a trade secret

**Answer B:**  
The employee can use general skills and experience but cannot take specific confidential methods or data

**Answer C:**  
The employee must avoid using any professional skills for at least one year after departure

**Answer D:**  
Trade secret law prevents the employee from working in the same industry

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Trade secret law does not protect all knowledge gained during employment. Courts distinguish between general skills, experience, and professional judgment (which employees are free to use) and specific, confidential methods, formulas, or data (which may be protected). This distinction ensures employee mobility while still protecting legitimate business interests.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly right! Employees accumulate expertise, professional judgment, and general competence over time. That accumulated skill is not a trade secret, even if learned on the job. What crosses the line is taking specific confidential information—blueprints, formulas, customer lists, proprietary processes—not just general know-how. This balance allows workers to change jobs without forfeiting their career development.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Trade secret law does not impose automatic time restrictions on using general professional skills. While non-compete agreements may limit where employees can work for a specified period, trade secret law itself focuses on what information can be used—not when or where. General skills remain available to employees immediately upon departure.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Trade secret law does not prevent employees from working in the same industry. Such restrictions may exist through non-compete agreements (subject to state law limitations), but trade secret protection is narrower: it prevents misappropriation of specific confidential information, not industry participation generally.

**Bloom's Level:** Application  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 3**

**Question Name:** M2-Altavion-Concrete-vs-Abstract

**Question Text:**  
In *Altavion, Inc. v. Konica Minolta Systems Laboratory* (2014), the California Court of Appeal held that Altavion's digital authentication technology qualified as a trade secret because:

**Answer A:**  
The idea was novel and no one else had thought of it before

**Answer B:**  
Altavion shared specific algorithms and implementation details, not just a broad concept

**Answer C:**  
Konica Minolta signed a confidentiality agreement covering all ideas discussed

**Answer D:**  
The technology was too complex for competitors to understand

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Novelty alone does not determine whether something qualifies as a trade secret. The key issue in *Altavion* was not whether the idea was original, but whether it was sufficiently concrete and detailed. Courts protect knowledge that is precisely articulated—formulas, algorithms, implementation steps—not vague or abstract concepts, even if those concepts are innovative.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! The court distinguished between a general idea (like "use barcodes for authentication") and specific, detailed implementation. Altavion shared concrete algorithms, design specifications, and technical steps—information detailed enough that someone could replicate it. That level of specificity is what qualifies as protectable "information" under trade secret law.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
While the existence of a confidentiality agreement is relevant to proving that reasonable efforts were made to maintain secrecy, it does not by itself make information qualify as a trade secret. The information must still meet the other elements: it must be concrete, have economic value, and not be generally known. A confidentiality agreement alone cannot transform an abstract idea into protectable information.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Complexity is not the test. Trade secret law protects information based on whether it is concrete, valuable, secret, and protected—not on whether it is difficult to understand. In fact, some of the most valuable trade secrets (like customer lists or pricing strategies) are relatively simple. What matters is that the information is sufficiently defined and provides competitive advantage.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 4**

**Question Name:** M2-Economic-Value-from-Secrecy

**Question Text:**  
To satisfy the "independent economic value" element of the trade secret test, which of the following must be true?

**Answer A:**  
The information must be worth at least $100,000 in quantifiable revenue

**Answer B:**  
The information must have already been monetized through licensing or sales

**Answer C:**  
The value must come specifically from the fact that the information is secret, not just from its usefulness

**Answer D:**  
The information must be patentable to demonstrate its economic significance

**Correct Answer:** C

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
There is no dollar threshold for trade secret protection. Courts do not require proof that information is worth a specific amount. What matters is whether the information provides a competitive advantage—whether it increases revenues, reduces costs, improves efficiency, or enhances market position. Potential value is sufficient, as long as there is a reasonable basis for believing it could provide commercial gain.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Information does not need to have been monetized to qualify for protection. Many trade secrets provide value through internal use—manufacturing processes, cost-saving techniques, operational efficiencies—without ever being licensed or sold externally. What matters is that the information contributes to the business's competitive position, not that it has generated specific transactions.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Exactly! This is the key insight: the value must flow specifically from secrecy. If the information would be just as valuable after public disclosure, it doesn't meet the standard. Courts ask, "Would a competitor gain a meaningful advantage by learning this information?" If yes, that's strong evidence of independent economic value derived from secrecy.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Trade secrets do not need to be patentable. In fact, many trade secrets—customer lists, pricing strategies, negative R&D data—are not patentable at all. The economic value inquiry focuses on competitive advantage, not on whether the information meets patent law's novelty and non-obviousness standards.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 5**

**Question Name:** M2-Board-of-Trade-Limited-Distribution

**Question Text:**  
In *Board of Trade of City of Chicago v. Christie Grain & Stock Co.* (1905), the U.S. Supreme Court held that the Chicago Board of Trade's real-time grain price data remained confidential even though it was shared with paying subscribers. This case illustrates which principle?

**Answer A:**  
Information loses trade secret protection as soon as it is shared with anyone outside the company

**Answer B:**  
Sharing information with select parties under confidentiality agreements does not destroy secrecy

**Answer C:**  
Only information that has never been disclosed can qualify as a trade secret

**Answer D:**  
Commercial data cannot be protected as a trade secret if it is sold to customers

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
This is incorrect. Trade secret law does not require absolute secrecy. Limited, controlled disclosure to employees, contractors, or third parties under confidentiality obligations is consistent with trade secret protection. What matters is that access is restricted and recipients understand their duties.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! The *Board of Trade* case established that information can remain confidential even when shared with a limited group, as long as access is controlled and recipients are bound by confidentiality terms. The law requires *relative* secrecy, not absolute isolation. This principle allows businesses to share trade secrets with employees, vendors, and partners as necessary for operations.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Trade secrets do not require that information has never been disclosed. Controlled disclosure under confidentiality agreements is permissible and common. What the law prohibits is *general* disclosure—making information available to the public or to competitors without restriction. Limited disclosure to those with a need to know, under appropriate protections, preserves secrecy.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Commercial data can be protected even if sold or licensed, as long as access is limited and recipients are bound by confidentiality. The *Board of Trade* distributed its data to paying subscribers under strict terms—this controlled model preserved trade secret status. What would destroy protection is unrestricted public availability, not selective commercial distribution.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 6**

**Question Name:** M2-Readily-Ascertainable-Reverse-Engineering

**Question Text:**  
In *Chicago Lock Co. v. Fanberg* (1982), the Ninth Circuit held that Chicago Lock's key code system was NOT a trade secret because:

**Answer A:**  
The company failed to mark its locks as "Confidential"

**Answer B:**  
The locks were publicly available for purchase and the codes could be discovered through lawful examination

**Answer C:**  
The key codes were too simple to qualify as valuable information

**Answer D:**  
Chicago Lock did not have any employees sign nondisclosure agreements

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
While document labeling is important for internal materials, physical products sold to the public do not need to be marked "Confidential" to retain trade secret protection. The issue in *Chicago Lock* was not the absence of labeling, but the fact that the information embedded in the product could be lawfully discovered by anyone who purchased it.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly! The court found that because the locks were sold publicly and the internal coding could be reverse-engineered through lawful examination, the information was "readily ascertainable by proper means." Trade secret protection ends where lawful discovery begins. If your innovation is embedded in a product you sell, and competitors can figure it out by taking it apart, you cannot rely on trade secrecy—you would need a patent or a redesign that keeps the secret hidden.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
The complexity or sophistication of information is not the determining factor. Simple information—like a customer list or a pricing formula—can be a trade secret if it provides competitive advantage and is kept confidential. The issue in *Chicago Lock* was not the value of the codes, but the fact that they could be lawfully discovered through reverse engineering.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
While confidentiality agreements with employees are an important element of "reasonable efforts," their absence was not the basis for the court's ruling in *Chicago Lock*. The decisive issue was that the information was readily ascertainable by proper means (reverse engineering), not that internal safeguards were inadequate.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 7**

**Question Name:** M2-Religious-Technology-Economic-Value

**Question Text:**  
In *Religious Technology Center v. Lerma* (1995), a federal court found that the Church of Scientology's confidential religious training materials could qualify as trade secrets because:

**Answer A:**  
Religious content automatically receives trade secret protection under the First Amendment

**Answer B:**  
The Church's business model—monetizing access to secret materials—demonstrated independent economic value derived from secrecy

**Answer C:**  
Spiritual information is always more valuable than commercial information

**Answer D:**  
The materials were registered with the U.S. Copyright Office

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
The First Amendment protects religious freedom, but it does not automatically grant trade secret status to religious content. Trade secret law applies the same test to all information—whether technical, commercial, or spiritual. The information must have independent economic value from secrecy, not be generally known, and be subject to reasonable efforts to maintain confidentiality.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! The court focused on the Church's business model: members paid substantial fees to access materials, and strict confidentiality rules were enforced. This demonstrated that the organization's revenue and competitive position depended on keeping the materials secret. Economic value does not require manufacturing or technology—it can exist wherever information contributes to market advantage in ways that depend on secrecy.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Trade secret law does not assign different value hierarchies to spiritual versus commercial information. The test is functional: Does the information provide competitive or commercial advantage? Does that advantage depend on secrecy? Spiritual content is not inherently more valuable—it must meet the same legal standards as any other claimed trade secret.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Copyright registration and trade secret protection are distinct forms of intellectual property. Registering materials with the Copyright Office does not make them trade secrets—in fact, copyright registration typically involves public deposit of the work, which can undermine secrecy. The *Religious Technology Center* case turned on the business model and confidentiality practices, not on copyright registration.

**Bloom's Level:** Application  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2A

+++

## **QUESTION 8**

**Question Name:** M2-Airfacts-Reasonable-Efforts-Failure

**Question Text:**  
In *Airfacts, Inc. v. Amezaga* (2018), the Fourth Circuit ruled against the plaintiff's trade secret claim primarily because:

**Answer A:**  
The information was too old to qualify for protection

**Answer B:**  
The company had not taken even minimal steps to protect the information—no NDAs, access restrictions, or documented protocols

**Answer C:**  
The defendant independently developed the same information

**Answer D:**  
The information was readily available in public databases

**Correct Answer:** B

***

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Age alone does not disqualify information from trade secret protection. As long as information remains secret, valuable, and protected, it can qualify—even if it has been held confidentially for decades. The issue in *Airfacts* was not the age of the information but the absence of protective measures.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! The court emphasized that Airfacts had no signed NDAs with employees, no documented access restrictions, and no internal protocols indicating that the information was treated as confidential. The company assumed employees would understand the data was sensitive—but assumption is not enough. Trade secret law requires visible, documented effort. Without foundational safeguards, the court concluded that secrecy was never established.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
Independent development by a defendant is a defense to trade secret misappropriation, but the court's decision in *Airfacts* was based on a more fundamental problem: the plaintiff failed to prove that it had a trade secret in the first place. The issue was not what the defendant did, but what the plaintiff failed to do—namely, take reasonable efforts to maintain secrecy.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
The ruling in *Airfacts* did not turn on whether the information was publicly available. The court focused on the plaintiff's failure to demonstrate reasonable efforts to protect the information internally. If information is readily available in public databases, it would fail the "not generally known" element—but that was not the basis of the court's decision here.

**Bloom's Level:** Comprehension  
**Module:** 2 (Identification & Inventory)  
**Lecture:** 2B

### **QUESTION 9: Proportionality Principle**

**Question Name:** M2-Proportionality-Principle

**Question Text:**  
A startup with 15 employees and limited financial resources has developed a valuable software algorithm. The company requires all employees to sign NDAs, labels source code files as "Confidential," and restricts repository access to engineers only. However, the company cannot afford enterprise-grade encryption or a sophisticated intrusion detection system. If misappropriation occurs, how should a court evaluate whether the startup took reasonable efforts?

**Answer A:** The company failed to take reasonable efforts because it did not implement all available security technologies

**Answer B:** The company took reasonable efforts because courts evaluate measures proportionate to the company's size, resources, and the value of the information

**Answer C:** The company must prove it spent at least 10% of its budget on trade secret protection

**Answer D:** Reasonable efforts require identical security measures regardless of company size

**Correct Answer:** B

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Courts do not require companies to implement every conceivable security measure, especially when resource constraints exist. The reasonable efforts standard is flexible and context-dependent. A startup is not expected to match the infrastructure of a Fortune 500 company. What matters is whether the protections implemented are appropriate given the company's circumstances—its size, resources, industry norms, and the value of the secrets.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! This illustrates the **proportionality principle** at the heart of reasonable efforts analysis. Courts assess whether protections are appropriate given the totality of circumstances—company size, available resources, industry norms, and secret value. The startup implemented foundational measures (NDAs, labeling, access restrictions)—evidence of systematic protection. Perfect security is not required; reasonable, proportionate diligence is. This prevents trade secret law from becoming a barrier to entry that only large companies can satisfy.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
There is no fixed percentage or dollar threshold for trade secret protection budgets. Courts do not require companies to spend a specific amount or proportion of revenue on security. The inquiry is qualitative, not quantitative: Did the company implement measures that demonstrate it treated the information as confidential? Proportionality is about matching efforts to context, not hitting arbitrary spending targets.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
The reasonable efforts standard explicitly rejects one-size-fits-all requirements. What is reasonable for a 10-person startup differs dramatically from what is reasonable for a multinational corporation. Courts recognize that smaller companies have fewer resources and evaluate their efforts accordingly. Requiring identical measures would make trade secret protection practically unavailable to small businesses and startups.

**Bloom's Level:** Analysis  
**Module:** 2 - Identification & Inventory  
**Lecture:** 2B

***

### **QUESTION 10: Totality of Circumstances**

**Question Name:** M2-Totality-of-Circumstances

**Question Text:**  
A manufacturing company has some protective measures in place: NDAs with most (but not all) employees, occasional confidentiality training, and password-protected files. However, the company allows departing employees to keep company laptops for several weeks after termination, does not conduct exit interviews, and has never audited who accessed sensitive manufacturing specifications. A former employee is alleged to have taken proprietary process documents. How will a court likely evaluate reasonable efforts?

**Answer A:** The presence of NDAs and passwords is sufficient to establish reasonable efforts

**Answer B:** The court will evaluate the totality of circumstances, likely finding that gaps and inconsistencies undermine the company's claim

**Answer C:** The lack of universal NDA coverage automatically defeats trade secret protection

**Answer D:** Reasonable efforts are proven because some measures were taken

**Correct Answer:** B

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
While NDAs and technical access controls are important baseline measures, courts evaluate the **totality of circumstances**—not isolated protective steps. Reasonable efforts require a pattern of consistent, systematic behavior. In this scenario, the company took some steps but left critical vulnerabilities unaddressed: departing employees retained device access, no exit protocols existed, and monitoring was absent. Courts will question whether the company genuinely treated the information as secret if it allowed such gaps.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Exactly! This scenario illustrates why courts use a **totality of circumstances** analysis. The company had *some* protections—but also significant gaps. Allowing terminated employees to keep laptops for weeks creates obvious vulnerability. No exit interviews means no reminder of obligations or device return verification. No audit trails means the company cannot prove who accessed what or detect suspicious activity. Courts look for consistency and comprehensiveness. When foundational safeguards exist alongside glaring omissions, judges conclude that secrecy wasn't a genuine organizational priority. *Allstate v. Fougere* teaches that inconsistency undermines credibility.

**Feedback for Answer C:**  
While universal NDA coverage is best practice, the absence of NDAs with some employees does not automatically defeat protection—especially if those employees never accessed the trade secrets at issue. Courts look at the totality of circumstances. However, failing to have NDAs with employees who did have access to the secrets would be significant evidence that reasonable efforts were lacking. The problem in this scenario is not just missing NDAs—it's the combination of that gap with weak exit controls and no monitoring.

**Feedback for Answer D:**  
Taking "some" measures is not sufficient. Courts require that efforts be reasonable and sustained—not sporadic or half-hearted. The presence of a few protective steps does not immunize a company if other fundamental safeguards are absent. *Airfacts v. Amezaga* demonstrates that minimal or incomplete efforts fail to establish trade secret status. Reasonable efforts must be comprehensive enough to signal that secrecy was an operational priority, not an afterthought.

**Bloom's Level:** Application  
**Module:** 2 - Identification & Inventory  
**Lecture:** 2B

***

### **QUESTION 12: Economic Value—Competitive Advantage Test**

**Question Name:** M2-Competitive-Advantage-Test

**Question Text:**  
A consulting firm maintains detailed client contact information, including decision-makers' names, phone numbers, procurement timelines, and budget constraints. This information is publicly available through LinkedIn, corporate websites, and SEC filings, but the firm has aggregated and organized it into a proprietary database. A departing consultant downloads the database. The firm claims trade secret misappropriation. What is the likely outcome regarding the economic value element?

**Answer A:** The information automatically qualifies because the firm invested time and effort in compiling it

**Answer B:** The information likely lacks independent economic value from secrecy because competitors could compile the same data from public sources without improper means

**Answer C:** Aggregation always transforms public information into a protectable trade secret

**Answer D:** The information has economic value because the client relationships are valuable to the firm

**Correct Answer:** B

**Feedback for Answer A:**  
Investment of time and effort alone does not create trade secret protection. Courts distinguish between the **value of labor** (sweat equity) and **value derived from secrecy**. If the information is readily ascertainable from public sources, then competitors gain no competitive advantage by learning what the firm compiled—they can compile it themselves lawfully. The cost-to-develop is low for competitors because the sources are public. *Chicago Lock v. Fanberg* illustrates that when information is readily ascertainable by proper means, it does not qualify as a trade secret.

**Feedback for Answer B:**  
Correct! This scenario tests the **independent economic value from secrecy** requirement. The second element asks: *Does the information provide competitive advantage specifically because it is not generally known?* Here, even though the firm aggregated the data conveniently, competitors could compile the same information from publicly available LinkedIn, corporate websites, and SEC filings without improper conduct. Courts have held that mere organization or compilation of public information typically does not create trade secret protection unless the *selection, coordination, or insight* reflected in the database is itself non-obvious and provides competitive advantage. If competitors can "catch up" quickly using lawful means, value does not flow from secrecy.

**Feedback for Answer C:**
